The Red Page
Obituary—The Jew
There are 12 million Jews in the world
to-day; a century hence there will be
only one, and his name will be Ahaswer.
All but the Wandering Jew will be assimi-
lated, and if the census-takers can catch up
to him he will be assimilated, too. These
conclusions are forced upon us by Dr.
Arthur Ruppin’s interesting work, The Jew#
of To-day (Bell), translated from the Ger-
man. In his introduction, Dr. Joseph
Jacobs, author of Studies in Jewish Stat-
istics, says:—
Dr. Ruppin treats his subject from the
standpoint of a single problem. When the
walls of the ghetto fell some 50 years ago
the admission of the Jews into modern
society on comparatively equal terms raised
the question how far their distinctive char-
acteristics —intellectual, cultural, religious
and the rest —would survive the contact
with modern culture, from which repressive
legislation had hitherto restrained them.
Would they, could they be assimilated into
modern European culture and still remain
Jews in the characteristics they had re-
tained throughout the Christian ages? The
answer Dr. Ruppin gives is a decided nega-
tive.
J*
Can the Jew resist -what has been
called Chinesism, the tendency of modern
times toward a fatal and monotonous simi-
larity and mediocrity invading all sections
of national life, one of the most obvious
signs of which is the standardising of fur-
niture and dress? Thus the Chinese have
dispensed with their queues and taken to
European dress, and everywhere the national
costumes of ; the peasantry are being dis-
carded for a uniform, international dress.
The irony of the position of the Jews is
that just when they have been released from
their ghettos, ‘The faith which kept them
alive, and at the same time isolated them
from their neighbors, has been disrupted by
the assaults of modern rationalism.”
The structure of Judaism (says Ruppin),
once so solid, is crumbling away before our
very eyes. Conversion and intermarriage
are thinning the ranks of Jews in every
direction, and the loss is heavier to bear, in
that the great decrease in the Jewish birth-
rate makes it more and more difficult to fill
up the gaps in the natural way.
Until lately it was thought that, though
this breaking-up process Could be plainly
discerned in Central and Western Europe,
and, of course, in the United States and
Australasia, the six million Jews in Russia
would be untouched, to remain the last
bulwark of Judaism. But the signs are
only too apparent that here, too, the disrup-
tive process has started, and will continue at
a rapidly accelerating rate. The author
distinguishes four classes of Jews: The six
million still, untouched by modern culture,
poor, with a high birth-rate, confined to
Russia, Asia and European Turkey, etc.;
the three million Jews who have emigrated,
and therefore are already discarding their
historic culture; the two million business
men with small families, who have succeeded
as emigrants, and pay. merely a formal ob-
servance to their religion; and the one mil-
lion rich Jews of the large towns, and the
Jews of university education all the world
over, with a low birth-rate, who have com-
pletely broken away from Judaism except in
name. The more completely the Jew pene-
trates the social life of his Christian en-
vironment the less does he remain a Jew.
But the assimilative process is not a new
development.
How great the losses suffered by Judaism
through assimilation have been may
be appreciated by the fact that the
Jews number to-day only 12 million
souls, while in the first century
a’.d. they numbered five million. Even
allowing for the thousands and tens _ of
thousands who succumbed to persecution,
Judaism to-day should be stronger than it
is by millions and tens of millions, were it
not for the continuous secession to
Christianity.
This was the toll taken in times when the
Jew was secluded from the assimilative
forces. Now, exposed on every side to dis-
ruptive agencies, there seems every chance of
a total merging of the Jews into Christen-
dom. The old factors that, prevented assimi-
lation are doomed to extinction. No longer
is the Jew looked down upon as a money-
lender, since we are all money-lenders or
borrowers now; no longer does the Jew
migrate, undet stress of persecution from
countries of a high to those of a lower cul-
ture: persecution now drives the Jew from
unenlightened Eastern Europe to Western
Europe and America, progressive countries
where the danger of assimilation is enor-
mously increased; no longer does the Jew
by his natural prolificness make good the
loss hy assimilation: in many countries his
fertility is less than that of Christians.
So the way is clear to assimilation. It
goes without saying that there can be no
question of the Jews disappearing from oft
the face of the globe in a few decades. A
people numbering some 12 million souls can-
not be wiped out so quickly as all that. It
may be a hundred years and more before the
last Jew is absorbed —the actual time is a
minor detail.,..lt is false optimism to
make, light of this crisis, and say that be-
cause Judaism has endured for 2000 years in
the face of the greatest hardships and dan-
gers, that therefore it is proof against all
happenings. . . .And besides this, do we not
know that in the past the Jewish population
of whole countries did become absorbed?. . . .
We see in the assimilative movement the
greatest danger that has assailed Judaism
since the Dispersion.
A census provided by the author shows
that to-da.v the Jews are more thoroughly
dispersed than ever before in their history.
In former times there was at least one
country in which the majority of the Jews
lived (Babylon, Spain. Poland) ; and only
150 years ago the Jews were almost exclu-
sively concentrated in Poland. Half of
them are thereabouts still, but the other
half are on the wallaby with Ahaswer.
The Jew’s commercial superiority, the
author points out, is merely a relative
superiority. Compared with Indians, Greeks,
Armenians (whom the Jews strikingly re-
semble) and Chinese, the Jews are mere
amateurs in pizness. All these nations be-
long to the oldest civilisations, and in their
hands the mere European has no chance.
It was the Jew’s luck that he had to deal
with the Christian fool.
A further asset in the assimilative pro-
cess comes with the adoption by the Jew
of the language of the country. In the
course of his history the Jew has had many
changes of language —Hebrew, Aramaic,
Greek, Arabic, Spanish and French, and the
modern “Spaniolisch” and “Yiddish.” And
the end of Yiddish as a national language
is in sight.
One may say that the Jewish religion is on
firm ground only where Yiddish is the mother
tongue. With the abandonment of Yiddish
the way is open for the triumphant march
of capitalism, for the adoption of universal
culture, for the depreciation of Jewish reli-
gion. and finally for intermarriage and con-
version.
So modern school education, of which the
Jew, with his worship of learning, is so
eagerly taking advantage, is fatal to Jewish
tradition.
Thq spread of modern school education
among the Jews is the chief cause of the
ferment in the spiritual life of the Jews,
and the principal factor in the process of
denationalism which is going on within
Judaism to-day.
The Russian Government allows the uni-
versity Jew certain privileges of domicile,
since it recognises that a Jew w r ho has re-
ceived a modem education is, in the ordinary
sense of the term, no longer a Jew. And
the decreasing importance of religion with
the Jews holds out no hope of a renaissance
of Jewish faith. “The Jewish religion to-
day is thoroughly disorganised.” The “en-
Jew has not the blind, unques-
tioning faith of the orthodox Jew, the faith
that has glued the race together for 2000
years.
But its fate is sealed ; the conditions which
kept orthodoxy alive so long—the social iso-
lation of the Jews, and their divorce from
secular education —are disappearing with the
advance of capitalism, and the structure of
orthodox Judaism, deprived of their sup-
port, will fall to the ground.
The last bulwark against assimilation,
anti-Semitism, is, shown to be not only an
insufficient check to deliquescence, but even a
factor in accelerating Jewish apostasy. The
gain which anti-Semitism brings to Judaism
is ephemeral—the loss is lasting. The Jew
must disappear.
J*
Having thus, by means of convincing
statistics, shown the doom of the Hebrew,
the author seeks for suggested cures of this
wasting disease. He finds in Zionism the
only hope of the survival of the Jew ; and
gives a most interesting description of the
conditions of the Jews at present in Pales-
tine. His conclusion is that: —
The aim of Zionism —the formation of a
coherent Jewish population in Palestine,
with agriculture as its economic basis and
Hebrew as the national language—is difficult,
indeed, but possible.
Dr. Ruppin has exhaustively the
difficulties—the chief of which is the reluct-
ance of the Jew to become a cocky. One
questions, for instance, how many Jews,
many of whom are now good Australians,
would return to Palestine as farmers or
orchardists, and what success the Australian
Jew would make of his back-blocks selection
in Palestine? But the author sees nq other
way out of the dilemma.
The difficulties must not deter us. The
re-building of a nation requires the utmost
effort, and the bringing into play of all the
latent strength of the Jewish people. For
Zionism is not a mere national or chauvin-
istic caprice, but the last desperate stand of
the Jews against annihilation. Should the
denationalising process which, in Western
Europe, has already crushed all independent
Jewish culture spread to Eastern Europe—
and there are signs that it is beginning to
do so—all is over with the Jews and with
Jewish culture. Once lost, a national cul-
ture can never be re-created, and without a
culture of its own the total absorption of the
Jews by the other nations is only a question
of time.
Well, the Jew has had a long life ; he has
given the world some great men and the
champion long-distance sundowner in his-
tory ; and on tiie whole he has hau an ad-
venturous and interesting career; and the
human race will say many good things of
him, and tell many good stories of him. as
it follows the hearse containing the last Jew
to the last Jewish grave. And by none will
his death be regretted more than by the
cartoonist and the writer of gags. For the
great achievement of the Jew is that he has
provided more jokes for the world than any
other nation in history. But one could
have wished for a more dramatic end for a
race that in its beginnings had many a
dramatic moment. He thrived on persecu-
tion ; he grew strong in his ghetto gaol.
Then civilisation opened his prison gates
and forbore to spit upon his gaberdine ; the
Christian asked him to dine —and was really
asking him 'to die. The Jew has been killed
by kindness.
Flagships Three
Really, C. E. W. Bean, author of Flag-
ships Three (zUston Rivers, 55.) ought to
be appointed Patriotic Writer-in-Ordinary
to the Commonwealth. He is the foremost
exponent of the new Australian school of
patriotism: he believes in Australia, and
he has reason to believe in her; and though
so many of us are like him in this respect,
none has so far succeeded in marrying that
fervent conviction to words that stir and
thrill. There are in this small company
other writers, doing the finest and most
needed service to the Commonwealth —one
might mention names like .T. H. M. Abbott
and Arthur W. Jose (whenever his em-
ployer. London Times, lets him) ; and over-
seas there is Richard Jebb, with his enthu-
siastic appreciation—it no longer needs a
defence —of our growing nationalism. The
existence of a band of writers like this is
one of the most stimulating signs of the
new spirit stirring the backwaters of this
continent.
One need not be told that the author of
On the Wool Track and The Dreadnought
of the Darling can write. Flagships Three
is an altogether fascinating book. The first
flagship was the ancestor of all the flagships
of our race.
“She lies as on that night when some of
the first ancestors of our race, coughing the
North Sea fog through their sagging red
moustaches and heavy beards, crept down
past the ghostly white forelands of their
newest colony, and beached her safely for
the night, under cover of the Channel
mists, in some unknown cove along the
English shore. It is more than a thousand
years since the .old flagship, with her stately
dead on board, turned homeward from her
cruise, and felt, for the last time, the kiss
of the wavelets in the home-fiord. . . The
centuries have dealt wondrous gently with
her, and there, in Christiabia a few months
since, some of us —Australians —saw the
first flagship of our race.”
It is a brilliant description that Bean
gives of this old boat, preserved merely be-
cause some forgotten Viking chief was
buried in the shore, beneath a great
mound of earth. “We were expecting to
find something rough hewn, awkwardly
shaped, bulky, clumsily fitted with a few
rough expedients. What we saw was a
long, lightly-built galley, as graceful as a
swan, shaped to lines as delicate as those
of the P. and O. liner at Circular Quay.”
Picking up the book, the present critic
said: “Bean ought to be able to tell us
something worth reading about H.M.A.S.
Australia.” But the critic glanced at the
first paragraph about the first flagship....
and had to read the whole chapter. Still
eager to get to the Australia, he came to
the Powerful —the old scrap-heap British
flagship, in which as an Australian the
present writer had no interest. .. .and Bean
made him read every one of those absorb-
ing chapters about the dear old Powerful
-—chapters more fascinating than anything
Kipling ever wrote about ships.
And the Australia, when Bean let him
come to her, was worth reading about.
Bean is, among other things, a brilliant
journalist, and his telling of the story of
the making of a dreadnought is a piece of
fascinating description. His account of the
launching of a dreadnought is a thrilling
bit of work, lightened with fine art by a hint
of delightful comedy. And, withouPbeing di-
dactic or dull, he manages to tell his readers
all that they could ever want to know about
our first flagship, and the happenings that
brought her into being. His tribute to
Senator Pearce comes well now that this
fine administrator is temporarily lost to our
Defence Department. And the appreciation,
by those (fhalified to judge, of the Austra-
lian seaman, is worth remembering.
A book for Every Australian—a book that
will make many a good Australian —that
will rouse the old sea-spirit that belongs to
all islanders —even though their island is a
continent.

The Modern Novel
The worth-while novelist of to-day l n
got tired of telling a romantic story: most
of the romantic stories have been told - am
most of the heroes are the same hero. ’ I'm.
modern novelist is beginning to see that
his public is getting surfeited with the mere
hero: it wants something bigger and some-
thing more interesting than a man.
man like Wells is dispensing with the way
of a man with a maid and substituting f O ,
romance a bigger subject, such as Marriage
Even that born storyteller, Arnold Bennett
has, for his latest novel, chosen for his hero
no mere man, but a large thing like a
theatre; and Margarete Bbhme, the German
author of The Department More (Apple-
ton, per Sydney Kook Club) has a Berlin
Anthony Hoydern’s for her heroine. Each
author has something more interesting to
write about than girl: each has set out to
tell us something about a modern develop-
ment than interests us. And each imparts
the information in an altogether fascinatine
way.
The Department Store is a faithful ac-
count of the growth of one of those
vast retail emporiums which are becoming
more and more important in the life of a
city. We see the growth of these huge busi-
nesses in Australian capitals. In Berlin the
process is apparently further advanced
than here, though npt so far advanced as
in the United States. Margarete Bbhme
traces the fortune of one of these vast con-
cerns from its foundation to.its impregnable
position as a city in itself. She shows how
the great shop affects its proprietors, its
customers, its employees; she exposes its
defects and eulogises its merits and suggests
its enormous ideal possibilities for good.
And she has a detailed knowledge of its
inner working, and in her vision of its:
future she applies the same knowledge to ;
embody the marvellous department store of
the future. Interwoven with this are
the life-stories of a score of individuals
whose development or whose tragedies am
dependent upon the shop. A novel of ab-,
sorbing interest, solidly Constructed, frank
in its speech, and full of suggestions for the
directors of our own department stores.
Bennett stresses more the human interest
in The Regent (Methuen, per Dymock, Syd-
ney), and allows his genial humor full play
in this story of the building and the running
of a London theatre. “Let’s write a story
about a theatre,” one can imagine Bennett
saying. “I know lots about theatres: my
connection with theatres has vastly inter-
ested me. If me, why not my readers?
But. of course, my public expect a ‘Five
Towns’ interest. Well, let’s make one of
my characters build a London theatre. Now,
who from my ‘Five Towns’ could run a
London theatre?” A quick glance at the
titles of his bookshelf full of Arnold Ben-
netts, and the question is answered. “They
liked The Card. Well, Edward Henry
Maehin will build my London theatre.
Rather arousing to see how he’ll manage it,
won’t it be? But ‘The Card’ is married—
happily married. Can’t risk making him
unpopular with a love-affair with his lead-
ing lady. We 11.... there won’t be a love-
interest^—at least, only a hint of one. Trust
‘The Card’ to get out of that _ all right.
He’s a man from the ‘Five Towns,’ you see.”
Hence a popular novel, amusing, informa-
tive, adventurous, realistic. The shrewd
provincial placed in the London theatrical
world, with pictures of typical managers,
actresses, solicitors, suffragettes and pro-
ducers. The scene where Edward Henry
settles Elsie April, who was beginning to
interest Edward Henry, and who had never
asked him if he was, by chance, _ married, is
admirably done. He brought his wife and
family to London, and in the park by
chance he met Elsie. He was wheeling the
new pram, because his daughter was yelling,
and accompanied by his family:—
“Flow do you do. Miss April?” said
Edward Henry, loudly. “I was hoping to
meet you. This my wife. Nellie, this is
Miss April.” Nellie bowed stiffly in her
black silk. And it has to be said that Elsie
April, in all her young and radiant splendor
and woman-of-the-worldiness was
stiff. “And there are my two boys. And
this is my little girl—in the pram.”
Maisie screamed, and pushed an expen-
sive doll out of the perambulator. Edward
Henry saved it by its boot as it fell. „
“And this is her doll. And this is nurse. (
he finished. “Fine, breezy morning, isn t it •
In due course the processions moved on.
“Well, that’s done!” Edward Henry
muttered to himself. And sighed.
It was time we had a counterblast to the
pretty little books of Golden Thoughts tiom
Everybody; and in his “little book ot 1 -
suits,” Sydney J. Endacott, of
gives us a delightful collection
Brass Thoughts— The Littleness of Crw-
ness (fid.), “a small collection of things th
the great have said about the great. - -
of them are delightfully libellous. ,
editor has confined himself for the P re - ,
to literary blokes; but he threatens to aeai
similarly with historical heroes. R® t
implored to continue, and we may sugg ■
that a book containing all the insults
tralian politicians have hurled at -Wffl ‘ ,
politicians would make an equally dele
volume.

Sporting Tradition and
The Modern Arm
CLEAN, trim lines mark the thoroughbred in
any field of sport* And if you judge an arm
as you do a horse or dog, you must surely
use a Remington*
There is a suggestion of speed—of all
around shooting ability about a Remington rifle
or shotgun that has made it the first choice of
thousands of sportsmen the world over. A
thorough try out has ever confirmed that choice*
In refining the heavier lines of the old breech-
loading action, the protruding hammer is done
away with, the breech completely closed. These
are safety features which alone might well govern
the shooter’s choice.

simply these and other technical points of gun
construction. Just send your name and post-ofiice
address on a postcard to-day.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sic Transit.
After a high tide on an eastern shore
ww+® d Wl u h DODe beside uie on the sand,
tVih the S , e ? with dul1 ’ unceasing roar
Told on its age-old story to the land.
Even and white, as though hand-smoothed,
the beach
shelLs ° er With Weeds and broken
Wh£“ d tbe , thwarted ocean’s utmost reach
Wh tinels a " birdS St °° d like watchiQ g sen-
Singing, because the lavish beauty filled
The qoo Wl t h song I journeyed on and on;
Wiffc (what shield of sultan ever thrilled
ith equal glory?) azure-breasted shone.
Ever and ever on that golden morn
1 V S WaS S^a^ed ’ c° u l d n °t choose but
Tu l ! lea / kener uP O . n whose ears was borne
ime strange, insistent message of the sea.
Others have heard it—yea, ten thousand men
Who coasted, noon or night, the sea’s ex-
panse ;
To them, as unto me, beyond their ken
he meaning of that pregnant utterance.
Lovers and loved ones paced the beach last
night—
The red moon rising late, saw arms en-
twined,
Before them lay the long sands silver white,
l rai l lQ g footprints stretched away
behind.
Rising, the strong waves marched a mightv
band ;
The lovers, lingering, left the moonlit shore ;
And, like their footprints on the gleaming
sand,
Their roseate hours, perchance, shall be no
more.
Doer and Dreamer, here a truth abides:—
*°r ye (though great be with sword or
pen)
Time marshalls his obliterating tides—
Wherefore —sic transit gloria.... and what
then?

RODERIC QUINN.
Kissing Time.
We glimmer down the twilight way,
The white moon follows after,
The little leaves that dance and play
Are full of elfin laughter;
The tall trees shiver in the wind,
The bamboos creak and chatter,
The old dull town is left behind,
And nothing seems to matter.
The spring airs whisper down the world
lts gladness thrice refining,
Your hair about my cheek is curled,
Your black eyes keep on shining;
Your waist is moulded to my arm,
Your lips on mine are tasting
Like wine some old god’s smile made warm
When joy his youth was wasting.
The wide, sweet night is all our own.
And old grey care’s a feather
As light to fly as moonbeams blown
Down gusts of laughing weather;
The noisy day is fast asleep,
All careless that it misses
The happy hour the breezes keep
For all the choicest kisses.
N.S.W. '
D. M. W.
Repression.
I lay rose petals on your baby, sleeping;
Your rose that fast was fading in my keep-
ing—
That they might live for me a little longer,
And tell if love or loyalty were stronger.
“She loves me, loves me not” —the old trick
lingers.
Ah, they are fragrant from your dainty
fingers.
Stay! How this prattle all my message
misses !
See, they are crushed and blighted with my
kisses !
Kisses like those I pressed upon the roses
Of your warm lips where the young god
reposes,
Playing with passion like a heart-whole
maiden,
Pleased with his poppies, all with poison
laden.
I have sipped poison, and the wild blood
surges.
Like the lashed billows that the tempest
urges.
I have drunk poison, yet I idly prattle,
Like a hired soldier, dicing before battle,
Singing a tavern song amid the clamor
Of drum and cannon, mattock, sword and
hammer.
Still, ’tis my battle eve, your rose my
banner;
And, though I sing, ’tis in no idler’s manner.
My gage and challenge lie in every petal,
Calling as sternly as the clang of metal.
Oh, to be up in fight, in truth, and boldly,
Weapon in hand, not calculating coldly !
Oh, that love’s battle must begin in flying,
Tricking, concealing, feigning, aye/ and
lying!
Oh, for the ways of old, the rush and rap-
ture
Of sword well swung, life staked upon your
capture!
Must I cry “Come!” and leave to you the
doing?
This has the savor of a coward’s wooing!
Is there no other way? My whole soul
sickens,
Striving to stifle every throb that quickens.
Ah, Love, what should be may not, and
what should not
Were easy, would we do all that we would
not.
So must those petals be my parting blessing,
A fragrant memory, past all caressing,
Which to recall, in the unsullied zest
Of years that may be ours, Heart’s-life!
were best.
spain.

paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 4, 1913.
THE SIX WONDERFUL PUBLIC
DEBTS.
Australia has been staggered, in modera-
tion, by the news that its great Cook and
Bottlewasher Ministry is preparing a
measure by which the Commonwealth is to
take over the State debts. This is a great
idea —a most commendable idea —and the
matter should have been attended to long
ago. If Cook carries it out on a satisfactory
basis The Bulletin will hail him (to his
great surprise and to its great surprise also)
as the greatest financier that Australia has
produced. And he will deserve the applause,
and he will merit an increase of salary.
Even a reasonable life pension, when he
drops out of office, wouldn’t be wholly amiss.
It was set forth in the original Australian
Constitution that the Federal authority had
power to take over the State debts as they
existed at the foundation of the Common-
wealth. That proposition died still-born.
No one was ready on the spur of the moment
with a scheme for effecting the transfer. In
fact, until the first Federal Ministry was
formed, and George Turner, in his unpre-
tentious way, had seized a cane chair and a
battered desk and commenced financing, there
was no one to even look at the matter. And
by the time Parliament had passed the formal
Acts which were necessary to establish its
authority, and had got over the first vote of
censure, and survived the first, second and
third stonewalling episodes, the chance was
gone. Old loans had been repaid, and new
loans had replaced them, and by reason of
overdrafts, casual advances and sundries,
also increases of interest and reductions of
interest, it was just as easy to find out the
exact State liabilities on the day when the
Commonwealth began as to find one particu-
lar grain of dust in a haystack. Later, by
an amendment of the Constitution, the Aus-
tralian Parliament acquired a general power
to take over State debts. But the problem
of putting these debts on an equitable basis
was so complicated that again nothing hap-
pened. The idea of just taking over the
debts as they stood, without any basis,
equitable or otherwise, was too terrifying to
be seriously considered by timid politicians.
* v * * *
If it is proposed to satisfy all the States,
to be absolutely honest and accurate, and to
do no penn’orth of wrong or possible wrong
to anybody, there are these matters to be
considered :
(1) The debts of the States vary greatly according
to population. No one can ever present an up-to-date
statement of the amount per head which the six
States owe, for it takes time to work out the figures,
and while they are being worked out the kaleido-
scopic debt has shifted, and the kaleidoscopic popu-
lation has shifted, and there is a change (not a large
one, yet an annoying one) in the aspect of affairs.
But, according to the calculations of Statistician
Knibbs, the liabilities at June 30, 1912, varied from
£44 16s. lid. per inhabitant in Victoria to
£B6 18s. Bd. per inhabitant in Westralia.
(2) On the other hand, Westralia is the most
thinly peopled of the States, while Victoria is the
most thickly peopled. The former has vast possi-
bilities, on paper at least, of acquiring new popula-
tion, \vhich means new Federal taxpayers, to carry
its debt, which the latter hasn’t. Westralia’s empty
spaces are, or ought to be, an asset to set against
its debt. Unfortunately no one can tell exactly how
the asset will pan out. *
(3) Also, some States supply much more revenue
per head to the Australian Treasury than others. If
the debts are taken over bodiU, and if it chances
that the State with the big debt is also the State
with the big income, that fact is some excuse for the
State with the big debt.
(4) At the same time no one can tell how long the
State which supplies the specially big income will
continue doing so. When Kalgoorlie dies, for in-
stance, Westralia may become a very poor contribu-
tor while remaining a very big debtor.
(5) Some States cost the Australian Government
much more per head than others. This circumstance
ought to be regarded, along with the varying sizes
of the contributions, as a set-off against the varying
sizes of the debts.
(6) Unfortunately no one can tell how long the
circumstance will last, or how soon some new cir-
cumstance may arise.
(7) The debts carry varying rates of interest. At
June 30, 1912, the State that paid the lowest in-
terest rate was Westralia (.£3 9s. 3d. per cent, per
annum). The State that paid the highest rate was
Queensland (£3 13s. 6d.'per cent, per annum). But
N.S.W. (quoted at £3 10s. 7d. per cent, per an-
num) has since been borrowing at £4 7s. 6d., and
has spoiled its average somewhat. Now £1,000,000
of debt at 3 per cent, may be really a smaller
liability than £900,000 at 4 per cent.
(8) Again, the loans run for varying terms. A
£1,000,000 loan at 4 per cent, with 20 years cur-
rency may really be cheaper than a £1,000,000 loan
at per cent, with 40 years currency. The former
may, 20 years hence, strike a low market and be re-
newed for half a century at 3 per cent. The latter
may, 40 years hence, strike a high market, and be
renewed with difficulty at 6 per cent. No one can
say that either thing will be, or that it won’t be.
Some absurd insurrectionist in Mesopotamia may
start a shindy that will make all the difference.
(9) Most' loans have no sinking funds attached.
A few—notably those of Westralia —have. Wes-
tralia, though it represents only about one-fifteenth
of the population of the Commonwealth, owns more
sinking fund than all the other States put together.
This affects the value of its liabilities to a wholly
indefinite extent. Also, it affects the problem of
taking over the public debt. If the Commonwealth
turns the State debts into a uniform liability it can t
repudiate the sinking fund arrangement which Wes-
tralia has made with its creditors. The only way
to secure uniformity is to make a similar arrange-
ment with all the other creditors.
When a Government.which makes a speci-
ality of State Rights •* and which makes a
strong point of refusing to be uncivil, even
in the smallest degree, to State Governments,
proposes to take over the public debts, it
runs up against all these propositions and
impossibilities. If it can Settle them or com-
promise them, to the satisfaction of every-
body, all will be well, save for the fact that,
if it leaves the States their six separate
borrowing powers, a new trouble will begin
to grow the day after the old trouble is got
rid of. If it can’t compromise with the State
Governments, then the best thing it can do is
to join the Unification and Reconstruction
movement. The central idea of this move-
ment is that the people should be
persuaded and educated into passing an
amendment of the Constitution whereby
everything—railways, railway construction,
public lands, public debts, borrowing powers,
revenues, expenditures, shortages, dog-tax,
mad-houses, everything, in fact, from Kos-
ciusko to a beetle —shall be handed over to
the Federal authority, and lumped together,
without any calculation or stock-taking
whatever. That would finish the debt-con-
solidation question in one act, and every-
body would be satisfied, or everybody would
be angry, or part of the people would be
pleased and part would be raving mad. Any-
how, the thing would be done. Then the
Federal authority could subdivide the coun-
try into a number of small States, according
to* the local geographical features, and sup-
ply each with a cheap Lieutenant-Governor
and a cheap Legislature of one House, and
furnish them, for so long as was considered
advisable, with such powers and such endow-
ments as the Federal authority thought fit.
But their railway muddles and their borrow-
ing muddles would remain Federal property,
so the State debt question, having been once
settled, could never start again. There
might be a discrepancy in the accounts to
begin with, but there would only be that
original discrepancy. Once it was got over
the trouble would be finished. Another de-
vice would be for the Commonwealth to take
over the existing debts and leave the States
their borrowing powers, but to also
take over all new State debts as fast as they
ai’e incurred, and to secure authority to levy
special and senarate taxes on each State to
provide a sinking fund against its special
and separate debt, or to stop its sinking
fund contribution out of the Federal subsidy
of 255. per head. If the State which
borrowed £10,000.000 was struck with a new
Federal tax of £200,000 a year for sinking
fund purposes, and the one that borrowed
£50,000,000 was struck with a new Federal
tax of £1,000,000 a year for the same pur-
pose, the fact that borrowing was a poor
game would probably dawn on the
£10.000.000 State, while it would hit the
£50.000.000 State like an avalanche striking
a fowl on the neck.
* ■» * *
Another device —a milder and slower one
certainly—would be for the Commonwealth
to establish a sinking fund out ■of its own
means for the abolition of the State debts
without putting any new restriction upon
the State loan madness. It could greatly
enlarge its note issue by compelling all banks
to hold, say, 40 per cent, of their cash re-
serves in Federal paper, and to pay cheques
up to a certain amount in Federal 10s. notes
on demand. With the money thus acquired
it could buy up a great mass of the cheapest
State Government bonds on the market.
These would be good security for the Fed-
eral paper money, and with the interest
received on them the Commonwealth could
buy up more State Government _ bonds,
and continue the process until it had
become the States’ sole creditor. In
fact, it could start, at no public cost, a long,
stern chase after the State Cohen —though
not quite so long as the stern chase which
Christianity maintains after the Devil. If
the sinking fund wasn’t robbed by the way
it would Get There at last, no matter how
fast Cohen might be. And when the Com-
monwealth, by this process of making much
money out of nothing, had become the
States’ sole creditor and its own sole cred-
itor, it would be in a position to
burn all Australia’s lOU’s in a pile as big
as a mallee hen’s nest, and abolish the in-
terest bill, and start afresh. This it would
do amid a shriek of recrimination from the
State —which would probably be every State
—which believed that it had contributed
more than its share to the sinking fund.
The device would be a reasonably fair one,
though rather gradual; yet means of accele-
ration might perhaps be found. Every State
would contribute to the original sinking fund
according to its wealth, and every State
would pay its own interest bill —no more and
no less—till its < liability, whether large or
small, was extinguished.
# # * *
The Prime Minister who proposes to take
over the State debts should enjoy himself if
he is the right kind of man. He ought to
be a financial genius and a lover of fighting
for its own sake. Lie should have no more
respect for conventional State Rights than
a wart-hog, and no more respect for the con-
ventional bumptiousness of State Premiers
than if these persons were dead. That sort
of Prime Minister might start Australia on
a new path of financial honesty. Certainly
JosEpn Cook has shown no signs so far of
being the person required, but he may have
been concealing himself under a bushel, so to
speak. If he likes to start now and show
himself the right man for the business he
will make history.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Budget that Wasn’t Presented.
It is difficult to imagine anything more com-
prehensive in the way of effrontery than the
financial statement —if it can be called one
—issued by the Holman Government of
N.S.W. The traditional horse-liar, and fish-
liar, and snake-liar, not to mention the news-
paper-circulation-liar, have done large things
in the way of assurance, but even in that
sort of company the Holman Ministry keeps
its end up very well. It has had the biggest
borrowing boom in Australian history, de-
spite its assurance that it would reduce bor-
rowing, and spend money only on works tiiat
would pay full interest ana something over.
Also it has enjoyed the biggest revenue in
N.S.W. history, it has accomplished, in one
year, a deficit of anytning between
iI,OOO,QUO and £1,500,000. It doesn't
apologise, or even pretend to apologise, for
its shortage and its muddle, nor does it pro-
vide means by which the shortage may be
met and the muddle redeemed. It doesn’t
even present, or promise that it will present,
a formal Budget or Financial Statement,
though it is still in office, and professes to
think that it has done well enough to justify
it in assuming that the State will retain it
in office. It simply demands something over
£8,000,000 to carry it over the next six
months, which means, as things are going,
that it will get to the end of 1913 with
another deficit of anything between £500,000
and £150,000. It doesn’t say in detail how
the is to be spent, but asks for a free
hand; just as Charles I. did when he ran
public affairs without a Parliament. When
the elections are over, and the new Assembly
meets, the Holman Government, if it is still
in charge, threatens to levy certain new taxes
in order to make ends meet, but the new
taxes appear hopelessly inadequate. And at
the best they can hardly arrive until the
beginning of 1914, seeing that the Holman
Ministry doesn’t want to incur public dis-
like by piling on fresh imposts before the
elections. By the beginning of 1914 there
will be very little time to collect more
money, seeing that the financial year ends
on June 30, and a number of Bills must be
passed before anything can be done. And
meanwhile the deficit will have piled up like
a pyramid. Incidentally, one of the proposed
methods for making ends meet is the aboli-
tion of the sinking fund. The N.S.W. sink-
ing fund has always been a fraud. It was
never allowed to accumulate at compound
interest as a real sinking fund should do,
so its name was a misrepresentation. It
never amounted to more than a fraction of
the Westralian sinking fund, though
Westralia has a very small debt compared to
that of N.S.W. The McGoWen Government,
which is now the Holman Government,
promised to put it on a sounder and more
solvent footing. But instead it actually cut
the payments to the fund below the amounts
paid by the previous Liberal Ministries. Now
it strikes the bedrock of financial casualness
by proposing to stop all payments to the
fund. Apparently the troubles of the Hol-
man-McGowen Ministry are these three:
(1) it has no political conscience; (2) it
doesn’t know that it lacks a political con-
science; (3) it doesn’t know that there is
such a thing as a political conscience.

Old and New Bagdad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Old Bagdad when things were good
Two millions in that city stood
(Or walked; or ran; or else were borne
In palanquins that mocked the morn-
Or lay, evolving gorgeous plans,
On silken cushions and divans,
Or strolled within their gardens green-
Or in their harems lolled serene;
Or traded near; or trafficked far;
Or crowded to the crammed bazaar;
Or braced themselves to face their jobs
With honey-cakes and hot kabobs; ’
Or thronged the baths and sherbet-kiosks-
Or knelt to Allah in the mosques) 1 ’
The mighty Tigris flowed beside
The city of the Caliph’s pride—
The City of Al-Mansur brave,
That Timur to the jackals gave!—
And argosies of galleys bore
The wealth of Asia to her door
And Bagdad’s merchant-traders grew
More affluent than any Jew.
Old Bagdad had a mighty Boom—
’Twas hard to find mere squatting-room;
And even those who camped in tents
Were forced to pay enormous rents;
Kabobs rose to a sinful price,
And so did honey-calces, and rice,
And oil and butter, fruit and spice!
But, oh! it was a stirring time;
The sight of Bagdad was sublime,
With towers and spires and minarets,
And marble fountains flinging jets
Of crystal water here and there,
And life and motion everywhere!
The Bagdadites were bright and gay,
They knew the Boom had come to stay;
And “water-fronts” to Tigris sold
At very near their weight in gold!
And Western Suburbs lots brought in
To syndicates a heap of “tin,”
Despite the fact that they were known
To be composed of barren stone—
The city was so full of cash
That even sweepers cut a dash,
And bath-attendants bought more wives,
And lived the liveliest of lives;
While Commerce stuffed the City full
Of carpets, jewels, silks and wool,
Damascus blades and Delhi wares,
And filigrees and like affairs,
And every quaint and precious thing
That keel or caravan could bring!
And feast and laughter, wine and song
Rolled Bagdad’s starry nights along—
A gay and splendid boom they had,
Long, long ago, in Old Bagdad !
Alas ! one night the City woke
To see itself go up in smoke!....
The merry Bagdadites, it seems, .
Among their multifarious schemes.
Had had some business with a band
Of land-agents at Samarkand,
And also much annoyed a man
Who lived in Eastern Turkestan.
This gentleman put on a sword,
And, followed by a hungry horde
Of semi-devils, headed south
And west, towards the Tigris’s mouth,
Where Bagdad lay in fatted sloth ;
En route more joined him —nothing loth
To visit Bagdad, rich, immense;
(Of course, at that gay town’s expense!)
The land-agents of Samarkand
Considerately bore a hand;
They said that Bagdad owed them rent,
For which repeatedly they’d sent,
And never got—a tribute owed
For keeping open some old road
To Bagdad’s commerce; and the man
Who hailed from Eastern Turkestan
Remarked that he was in good health;
His trouble was a lack of wealth.
But he had read in some small pub
About the Ravdad Millions Club,
And how that fatted City teemed
With wealth like that of which he dreamed.
So they joined forces —rank on rank
They marched their hordes to Tigris’s bank;
The Boom just crumpled there and then
Before those brown faced, bow-legged men
Whose horrid energy knocked spots
Off all the Western Suburb lots —
The “water-fronts” just slumped to nil
When Samarkand began to kill
The merry Baedadites by dozens.
And ripped their sisters, aunts and cousins..
Ah, me! the Slump was very had
Long, long ago, in Old Bagdad!
In New Bagdad—built round a bay— (
Things look all right; the Boom must stay.
For how on earth can any harm
Assail a place of so much charm?
The bare idea !... Bah !... Pooh-pooh!. ■ ■
What could a mod n rn Ttmur do?
The New Bagdad dreads no fierce band
Of land-agents from Samarkand!....
Oh, this is quite a different age!....
No longer men in wars engage,
Or. rarely—and, then, but for health—
Whv, who would covet Bagdad’s wealth.
And what a beastlv. caddish joke
To send New Bagdad up in smoke!
No decent horde of semi-devils
Would think of wild and bloodv revels
’Mid scenes of lust and bestial rage.
Or starve our Caliph in a cage!
C!o! Put that proverb on the shelf
That History repeats itself!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

should reach us at once to ensure
supply.
Depreciation alone runs to a good deal if a
house becomes obsolete and is condemned
when it is 40 years old, and some houses
become obsolete in 20 years. Loss bv non-
paying tenants is a very large item in
bad times—and in all times at a
place like Broken Hill, where strikes are
numerous, and all rent-paying ceases so long
as the strike lasts, and not even 100 bailiffs
could collect arrears. Now at the present
moment there are fairly solid bank invest-
ments which yield anything up to 8 per
cent., and while it is possible to sit on a
reasonable wad of scrip, and get 8 per cent.,
the habit of building houses for the poor at
5 per cent, is likely to be killed rather dead
All these disadvantages, as already hinted
at, apply to the landlord who charges 30s
or less for his cottage. The one who
charges 31s. or more can drag 20 per cent,
out of his investment with impunity. The
extra income is the reward of extortion and
the premium put on rack-renting. It is a
complicated situation, but the mental pro-
cesses of Holman and McGowen are some-
times past comprehension. That Govern-
ment made a great attempt, a year or two
ago. to fix the income-tax of the rich
absentee landlord on a lower scale than that
of the hardworking Australian manufactur-
ing co. which paid the wages fixed by the
Arbitration Court, employed Australian
workmen, and as a rule divided a very
moderate dividend among a multitude of
Australian investors. The amazing scheme
failed, but the reason why it was attempted
is a mystery to this day. Apparently it be-
longed to the realm of Chinese logic or Chow
metaohysics. And the Fair Rents Bill is
to some extent in the same category.
The “Unworkable” Machine.
Fusion journalism has suddenly changed its
views of Cook s tiyspeck majority. A month
ago Melbourne Argus, for instance, professed
to rejoice in tfle forthcoming salvation of the
country by means of Liberal measures, both
progressive and remedial. The franchise
whereof poor, bedridden citizens had been
robbed by Labor was to be restored to those
moaning sufferers. All the misdeeds of Labor
were to be corrected, and ail the dreams of
Liberalism to be realised —by Cook’s major-
ity of one. It was even suggested that its
sipallness gave it a wondrous power for good,
which an unwieldy majority of, say, three
would have lacked. Everything in the out-
look was lovely in Tory eyes, a month ago.
But now the Argus admits: “It is generally
recognised that the Parliament, as a whole,
as returned at the recent elections, is an
unworkable machine.” All the same, this
Parliament, which expresses the thought of
the country fairly accurately, should be as
workable as any other Parliament. What it
needs is an Elected Ministry to work it. The
present Party Ministry cannot work it. A
dissolution and re-election of both Houses
would result in another Ministry, of one
party or other, equally incapable of working
the machine. Seeing, then, that the precious
machine seems likely to endure for ages, the
obvious cure for its existing uselessness is a
new system of constructing Ministries. What
Cook cannot do because Fisher mustn’t let
him, or Fisher couldn’t do because Cook
wouldn’t let him and John Forrest and
Irvine would block the way, could be accom-
plished by a composite Government, neither
the creature of a caucus nor the slave of
anti-Labor traditions. The old conservative
arguments against elective Ministries cannot
withstand the logical arguments in favor of
them, therefore conservatism avoids the ques-
tion. But the growing demand for a work-
ing Government in a workable Parliament
cannot be eternally ignored. Fatheaded ad-
mirers of the Constitution seem to labor —or
puff and wheeze—under the delusion that the
party system of government is an imperish-
able law of the land. They have an idea that
nothing short of an earthquake, or a referen-
dum at least, could shift the distinction
between the “ins” and the “outs,” or obliter-
ate the difference between Government
benches and Opposition ditto. Yet if Cook
were to take in his resignation as a Party
Premier, and advise the G.-G. to desire the
whole House of Reps., sitting in open caucus,
to elect a new Ministry as it elects a new
Speaker, the ancient foolishness of party
politics would go reeling to its grave. If a
majority in the House refused to accept the
new order of things, and a dissolution came
to pass, what would be the result? The con-
tents would be re-elected whilst the non-
contents were mostly getting defeated by up-
to-date candidates. It is only necessary for
the Non-Party Government system to be put
before the voters for it to be approved by the
Voice of the Country. Then the Parliament
machine would work. Every measure that
can be put before Parliament must have a
majority either for or against it, even if the
majority is only the Speaker’s vote. There-
fore every question can be settled on its
merits provided its merits are not; mixed up
with the necessity for keening" C<)ok or
Fisher in office, or putting Cook ! or Fisher
out of office. Let Cook or Fisher,
or any other person that Parliament ap-
proves. be fixed in office for three years ; let
his colleagues be also nailed down for three
years; and let them just go on administer-
ing the departments for three years whether
their measures are accepted or rejected. Then
nobody would waste time over votes of cen-
sure. because they would make no difference.
Also, nobody need vote against his conscience
to keep his own boss politician in or to push
the otl|er party’s boss politician out, for
until the three years were up they wouldn’t
go out. and then they would go out automati-
cally. Tbore would be no such spectacle as
that of N.S.W. Labor member McOtrß de-
nouncing the deranged scheme bv which an
English firm is to supervise N S.W. railway
construction at a huge fee. and vet refusing
to vote against it lest he should burst the
blessed old narty. In fact, the Legislature
might, at last, have a chance to go straight.
And it would never become “unworkable”
because of an election, any more than the
staff of the A.M.P. Society or the Bank of
N.S.W. is unworkable after the annual meet-
ing.
The Coal Export Madness.
The thirsty and shameless individual, who
begs with tears in his eyes for the price of
a pint, has only one rival as a pathetic tale
pitcher, i.e., the rich man. The man who
hasn’t got it does not realise how much grief
wealth brings, until some apparently
comfortable capitalist arises before . a
Wages Board and sorrowfully details
Sll,ii r 0U u b r' And even then he can
nartuy believe it, so dreadful does
Vi# carrative sound. John Brown
',i i a n (b A’ Brown, the coal magnates,
who dwell and boom at Newcastle, N.S.W )
gave evidence, last week, before the Miners'
> ages Board, and if the Chairman’s heart
wasn t touched it wasn’t the fault of John
iiROWN. Ihe unprofitableness of the for-
eign trade was one of his chief themes.
According to John, the whole of the foreign
trade had been carried on at a loss, or some-
th very like a loss, during the last three
jears. let he appeared to grieve in advance
over the opening of the Panama Canal,
\yhich work, he considered, would deprive
the local coal barons of some of this alleged
unprofitable trade, and give it to the United
states, India and China. At long range, it
looks rather like a man lamenting the loss
or a dangerous tiger with an immense appe-
tite. But perhaps John is fond of his tiger.
Meanwhile, and quite apart from J. Brown,
these points are worth remembering: (1)
more coal is being manufactured in
rsature s laboratory, and the substance has
become such an essential of civilisation that
it may almost be said that the nation which
digs out and sells all its coal first will perish
flrst. .(2) Australia, so far as the evidence
goes, is not at all well supplied with coal
s . sl f e ’ an( l it is certainly not overloaded
with timber, and it doesn’t show up well in
the matter of oil. (3) Therefore, unless it
vvants to die young, for the temporary en-
richment of fleeting Browns and people
ot that sort, it should shut down absolutely
on the coal export madness. Let other coun-
tnes do the coal export business, and sell
their birthright for a splash of pottage. If
Australia gave itself a decent tariff, and
doubled its local manufactures (which, by
the way, it could do without using any extra
coal, if it only had sense enough to avail
itself of the water-power of the Snowy, Vic-
toria, Roper, Barron and other rivers), it
would make so much money that it could
throw its coal export business overboard and
not miss it. It could eyen throw Brown
overboard, and not miss him, except as an
interesting citizen. A country which tries
to get rich by selling its coal abroad is in
the same category as a besieged city which
tries to turn an honest coope l ’ by selling
its battlements, bit by bit, to the enemy Un-
building material.
Unification and Reconstruction.
The growing disgust which is becoming evi-
dent on all . sides, with both Labor and
Liberal parties, in Federal and State poli-
tics alike, promises to give the Young Aus-
tralia movement its required opportunity.
This paper hag but one carp to level at the
Young Australia party, and that has refer-
ence to its name. An atmosphere of imper-
manence surrounds such a title. In the
natural course of events the Young Aus-
tralia party must become the Middle-aged
Australia party, which would be banal; and
ultimately the Elderly Australia party,
which would be dreadful and ludicrous. Why
not, quite frankly, the Unification and Re-
construction party? A few years ago the
word Unification frightened certain Aus-
tralians as much as the word Socialism
used to frighten certain Britishers a couple
of decades back. The menace of Unification
is now as ruined a myth, almost, as the
menace of Sosh. Factors which had much
influence in the matter were the Referenda
campaigns and the events which led up to
them. When Australia federated the elec-
tors imagined that tlm National Parliament
would have power, under the Constitution, to
pass effective Trade Marks and New Protec-
tion legislation, and so forth. They dis-
covered presently that Parliament had no
power of the sort. So drastically has the
High Court limited the powers of Parlia-
ment, by its interpretations of the Constitu-
tion, that, under the present Federal sys-
tem, it is extremely doubtful if as much as
half those bgdly-needed measures which, 10
years ago, lawyers, and laymen alike assumed
would shortly be in full working order, will
ever be effectuated. As regards the control
of Trusts, as regards (Vupulsory Insurance
legislation, and in a nundred other urgent
directions that might be cited readily, the
Commonwealth Parliament is impotent. The
general body of electors has only lately come
to realise the anomaly ; the Referenda cam-
paigns having served to educate it in the
matter. The Liberal howl of warning that
the recently-proppsed amendment in the
Trade and Commerce clause would amount
to Unification (with Reconstruction to fol-
low) had the unexpected effect of suggesting
to a vast number of electors that there
might be worse things than Unification, any-
how. This is the ground in which the seed
sown by the Young Australia party will be
bound to germinate. The party has no in-
tention of playing a thinking role merely.
In fact, it intends to butt violently into
both Federal and State politics at the
earliest opportunity. The annual confer-
ence is to be held in Melbourne on the first
Wednesday in November —the. day after
the great local Horse Feast—and on
that occasion the full obejective will
be expounded. Incidentally, a “Handbook
of Nations l Reconstruction” will be issued,
in which volume the need for Unification,
followed by a new division of the country,
and a new distribution of authority, will be
argued, and a brand-new Constitution sub-
mitted to the consideration of- this leg-roped
Commonwealth. ,One of the first planks of
the new movement is “Abolition of Party
Government. Ministers to be elected by
the Legislature to administer National De-
partments.” Andtltfr is “The Abolition of
the power to borrow money, at present exer-
cised indiscriminately bv State and local
Governments.” And a third is the Initia-
tive and Referendum. The new partv is.. of
course, absolutely sound on Australian
defence. Altogether, the programme is one
that the more progressive electors should
adhere to with enthusiasm. The worst luck
The Bulletin wishes the new movement
is that Australia may be energetically push-
ing it along for years after both Liberal and
Labor parties have been cimsigngd to the
clammy tomb. 2
The Federal Muck-rakers.
There is an unusually virulent epidemic of
mud-slingine raging in the House of •Repre-
sentatives just now. A certain amount of
muck-raking is. of course, inseparable from
the Party Government system. But the
present outbreak is over the odds The Cook
Ministry, partly because ft feels that it
must make some sort of a demonstration,
and partly because a section of it is built
that way naturally, is working overtime at
tfle unsavory job of trying to prove that its
predecessors conducted themselves, while in
office, a collection of rogues and vaga-
bonds. The most astonishing accusations
have been hurled round casually—charges
which, were they made outside the shelter
of Parliamentary privilege, would provide
material for enough criminal libel actions to
keep the lawyers happy for years. No one
seems to wholly escape. The suburban
mansion of one Laborite, the terrace of
houses of another, the Gore llili residence
of W. M. Hughes, the new hat of King
O Malley —each and all of these things pro-
voke scandalised nods and becks and whis-
pered hints of well-concealed corruption.
Liberalism never assumes that a thrifty man
who suddenly found himself in the posses-
sion of an income several times greater
than any which he had previously enjoyed,
might well achieve these assets and more
out of his salary, without robbing the com-
munity of a farthing. It does not, while
circulating its vague libels, add the infor-
mation that some of the suspects are fairly
competent professional and business men, in
addition to being politicians. The Party
game is not played that way. No point is
too low or dirty to be taken; no reputation
is too transparently honest to be assailed.
It is often argued, in condonation of all this
flagrant blackguardism, that it is only the
W»rk of the iacaals anyhow—that the
Cooks, the Irvines and the Glynns hold
sternly aloof from it. Ana so they do, so
far as actual personal participation is con-
cerned. But inasmuch as their words carry
weight, and they never utter a syllable in
reprobation of the malicious lying that they
know to be going on in what is considered
to be their interests, they are morally as
guilty as the actual offenders. In fact, they
are rather more to be blamed. They want
the results without any of the ignominy.
The Laborites are little better in this re-
spect. If half the idiotic statements they
have made in the last month or so about
Tory bribes to their opponents’ coffers were
true, it would mean that the Liberal party is
absolutely rolling in wealth. The cold, drab
fact is that the Federal Liberal party is such
a palpably opportunist crowd—-so chronically
ready to sink its Defence principles, or its
anti-Socialist principles, or its fiscal prin-
ciples at the first dim hint that recantation
will mean a few votes —that the local
capitalist hardly regards it as worth
buying. And that is apart altogether from
the circumstance that there are few, if any,
Liberals who could be bought with hard cash
—undesirable though they may be in other
respects. As a natural outcome of the
slangwhangine that is going on between the
parties, in the name of Responsible Govern-
ment, the most ludicrous accusations are
being levelled at outsiders. This paper
speaks from experience. One of the pro-
prietors of The Bulletin happens to be in-
terested in a wireless co. which failed to do
business with the Postal Department. The
paper, both before and after that episode,
said some of those things about the Postal
Department which every other citizen was
saying, in a considerably more envenomed
tone of voice, every other day. The P.M.G.
of the day immediately announces, in effect,
to a shocked audience that he is being vic-
timised, in this matter, because he refused to
give what would have amounted to a bribe.
A writer considers that Dalgety, by reason
of its unfailing water-power supply, would
be a better capital site than Canberra. It
is pointed out, on no evidence whatever, that
he owns a vast estate at Dalgety. A trust
which does not pay particularly good wages,
take it for all in all, and which charges ex-
orbitant prices for its wares, is assailed. An-
other trust which,, in the important matters
of its wage-sheets and in the reasonableness
of its charges is hardly assailable, happens
to be left alone. The inference is that it has
paid to be left alone. And so on, till further
notice. The late W. J. Lyne was a militant
Protectionist. He was. incidentally, an honest
man. The Liberal muck-rakers of the sort
who are now blowing busily upon the reputa-
tions of Fisher and his colleagues circulated
the most hair-raising yarns - concerning the
boodlings of Lyne. He might have been a
rich man. He certainly would have been a
very rich one had he dope half the infamous
things his enemies accused him of doing. Yet
he died poor. And even with that evidence
in his favor, the fairer-minded papers felt
called upon to insist in their obituaries on
his uprightness, so deeply had the reiterated
lies concerning him bitten into the popular
mind. In this paper’s opinion, the time has
arrived for a truce in the matter of assorted
political slander. A modus operandi should
easily be arrived at between the party lead-
ers, since the most superficial consideration
of the question must satisfy their intelli-
gences that this, as much as anything else,
is sickening the constituencies not only with
the Party Government system, but with them
as individuals. The present campaign of
calumny will peter out the moment the big
men on either side give their jackals and
heelers the word. If it isn’t given moder-
ately soon, there is a likelihood, at the pre-
sent rate of progress, of some villainously-
assailed member resorting to the only arbi-
trament left to him —that of the gun. Con-
sidering what a rotten shot the average
Parliamentarian is, that possibility is
fraught with peril to the entire Legislative
body.
From an old S. Aus. politician :
We in S. Ans. have been treated in a most nnfra-
ternal fashion in connection with the Murray waters
question. We have gone over and over again to con-
ferences wph the Eastern States in the hone of arriv-
ing at some mutuallv satisfactory agreement in re-
gard to the rival claims of navigation and irrigation,
and have come back with the belief that at last some-
thing definite had been done. But invariably one or
the other of the upner-river States has, at the
twelfth hour, gone back on the undertakings. Re-
cently N.S.W. and Vic. magnanimously agreed that
S.A. should be allowed at its sole expense to store ‘
water in the Lake Victoria basin to provide for a
navigable, stream lower down. The work will cost
£483,000, and it will benefit the up-river States by-
giving them more water for irrigation. But although
S.A. wrote three or four times to N.S.W. to urge the
passage of the Enabling Bill in time to allow of the
work being begun this year, no reply of any kind
had been received when the Bill for the work Was sub-
mitted to the S.A. Parliament. This sort of conduct
is not calculated to engender brotherly feelings among
the States. The agreement was signed by the three
Premiers who met in conference two years ago, so
there was amnle time to consider ft in all' its
aspects, while S.A. was getting the advice of Captain
Johnston, the American expert, on the scheme. He is
due to arrive in September to carry out the work •
yet the Eastern States have not had the courtesv to
ratify their own offer. Once they agreed to share the
expense of the Lake Victoria works with S.A., as they
recognised they would get as much good out of it as
wou^c * ’ hut when Premier V err an showed, in
1911, that he was over-anxious to make a start, the
eastern Premiers varied their agreement to the extent
of stipulating that S.A. must do all the paying,
lhat hard condition being accepted, the other States
become again suspicious and hesitate to let the busi-
ness go on. Once upon a time, Addison, M.L.C., who
in his day was a champion rifle shot, and whose son
won the King's prize at Bisley, threatened to lead an
expedition of sharpshooters up the Murray to compel
the Eastern States to concede S.A. its rights in the
water. It may come to that yet.
A SPRING OUTBURST.
Now is the spring upon us, when man’s
fancy lightly turns to thoughts of iced
lager and the Melbourne Cup prospects and
green peas; and when woman’s imaginings
are of bilious billions of hats or what passes
for hats in these days of fashionable luna-
tics ; also, the soul of the spring
poet leapeth like the mountain goat, and
the spring chicken toughens in his old age
and tuings generally buck up and chalk their
slioes for another go at the greasy boom of
hate. Wherefore, the Royal Art Society,
in the warming days of last week, spread
its tender canvases upon tue wall space at
its Sydney home in Pitt-street. in its
haste to blossom forth it got ahead of the
delivery of its catalogues, so that there were
none,available on press view day (Friday)
nor on private view day (Saturday) ; at
least, there were a few, but apparently only
for the Elect, of whom The Bulletin
found, in a chastened spirit and a rebuked
and humbled attitude, that it was not one.
Some time, or thereabouts, when the mere
herd can purchase the official book contain-
ing the names of the contributing artists,
and what their contributions are supposed
to represent, and the prices asked, and a
tew other mere trifles of the kind; nothing
will prevent it obtaining the information.
Till then, it will struggle with the subject
in a general and somewhat casual way.
This is the easier done because the cata-
logues of last year’s show, and the show of
the year before, and of the previous or prior
or preceding year are available, and the
work was of somewhat the same character
on those occasions as it is this year. Maybe
there is a width of difference through which
a small fore-and-aft elephant could be
pushed, but the animal would have to be
melted down first and strained carefully,
and the bones thrown at the president of
the Society by way of encouragement all
round. Whatever difference does exist in
this spring’s show as compared with similar
exhibitions of the past _ few years by the
R.A.S. is on the side of improvement in the
general average. That is satisfactory to
contemplate. So is a life of rectitude, but
•there is nothing like a dog fight or two if
you want enthusiasm. The R.A.S. would
be the better for a little more Philistine in
its composition.

It was rough on the individual sufferer,
but only fair to her sex, that the latest
sufferer from the hatpin evil should have
been a woman. She was standing outside
the theatre in Ballarat when a passing hat-
pin penetrated the pupil of her eye and
nearly blinded it. In previous cases a male
eye received the dagger thrust, and the
guilty Sex viewed the matter with haughty
indifference.
S.A. Supreme Court has had to consider
a case which it shied at deciding. If you
cannot read or write, and consequently keep
your bank account in your wife’s name, and
she, after 20 years of married life, clears
out with your nephew, who gets the money?
It was shown that the wife had done a good
bit towards earning the fund, so Judge
Gordon—who is fond of King Solomon’s
precedent—-suggested that the parties might
go outside and chop the bank-account in
halves. Chopped accordingly.
From “Red Tape” :
The N.S.W. Government has made a fresh effort
to deal with its rather troublesome dependency, Nor-
folk Island. Some time ago, when McGowen was
Premier, it is understood in undiplomatic circles
that he and Governor Strickland had done quite
serious battle over the way the affairs of the island
had been conducted. Whether or not, a change was
decided upon, and Michael Murphy has started duty
as Administrator and Chief Magistrate of the island.
Mike has been for 40 years in the Sydney office of
the Department which looks after the affairs of the
islanders, and if he doesn’t know all about them
he has wasted an unusual quantity of time in not
finding out.
Thanks to the Federal grant of £500,000,
the first instalment of which blew into
Treasurer Payne’s office this year and made
it £95,000 the richer, Tasmania’s famously-
persistent floating deficit has been totally
sunk, for the first time since 1884. The
Federal windfall, after paying £lB,OOO as a
special aid to mining, was big enough to
obliterate the floating incubus of £44,762,
and leave the State with an alleged surplus
of £32,238. The Government of the Fly-
speck is so overjoyed at this unusual good
fortune that it proposes to splash up £20,000
of the surplus on the main roads of the
island, which the municipalities are too par-
simonious to keep in order for themselves;
£5OOO goes in “repairs and renewals to roads
and tracks” ; and £6OOO goes on patches and
additions to public buildings.
When the Turks came back and emptied
the Bulgarians out of Adrianople it was
announced that Russia in particular and
Europe in general would apply the boot to
Abdul if he did not leave the town quietly.
A Russian army was said to be hovering on
the Armenian border, and a Russian
squadron was alleged to be approaching the
Bosphorus. But Abdul sat tight, though he
was quite sober. And nothing happened.
So Abdul went abroad and emptied the
Bulgarian out of Kirk Kilisse. This brought
more threats of what is known as interven-
tion. But Abdul had decided that inter-
vention was a mere jimjam. When the
Allies grabbed practically all his European
territories Abdul waited for the Powers to
redeem the declaration made at the begin-
ning of the war, that they would not be
allowed to annex. And while he waited the
Allies proceeded to divide the spoil, and
Abdul had to formally cede it at the Treaty
of London. So when his turn came he pro-
ceeded to recover some of his lost loam, and
took his chance. Nothing has happened
again. The idea of intervention has been
given up in favor of a financial boy-
cott. if _ one can be arranged. There are
doubts if it can, as the brunt of it would
fall on Abdul’s foreign creditors. So the
chances are that he will keep what he has
grabbed or recovered—till the Bulgar or the
Greek is strong enough to turn *him out.
Meanwhile many things may happen, but
the least likely is that the man with the'fez
will pull himself together and start a com-
prehensive system of water supplwand drain-
age in Adrianople—the only thing that
would justify his presence there.

It is many years since Edward Dyson
wrote the story of “A Golden Shanty” for
The Bulletin—the story of the Shamrock
Hotel, which hung out its disreputable sign
“not a day’s tramp from Ballarat, set well
hack from a dusty track that started no-
where in particular and had no destina-
tion worth mentioning.” One Michael Doyle
owned the shanty, which was built by an
eccentric German, of sun-dried bricks made
of gold-bearing dirt. Doyle bought the pub
for a trifle at the tail end of a “rush,” and
subsequently was on the verge of selling it
for £5O to a syndicate of Chinese who had
discovered that the shanty went about 3J
grains to the brick. Then an accident put
him wise to the attempted outrage on his
Irish innocence. The air was filled with the
fury of Doyle, and the shouts of Tipperary,
and the embitterment of Galway, and the
lament of China fleeing in its loose blue
dungaree pants from the wrath to come.
And when these disturbances were shovelled
away the Shamrock was demolished and put
through the battery, to the great profit of
Michael Doyle.
* * * *
That is the framework of Dyson’s- com-
edy-drama, “The Golden Shanty.” which
was set before a crowded and highly-inter-
ested audience at the Palace (Svdney) on
Saturday night by the Bert Bailey Co.
But to build up the piece the author threw
out some of the original characters and in-
troduced others from his wonderfully fertile
mind. Michael Doyle, as the shanty pro-
prietor, is fired in favor of Mrs. Edna Jack,
better known as Brummy Peters, “the
washerwoman of Jacker’s Flat,” that hard-
faced, good-hearted female who damned the
bar-keep’s eyes in “The Pick and Barrow”
because there was no stout available, and
‘made it gin.” Lilias Adeson is a trifle
forced in her mannishness, but, probably,
“THE GOLDEN SHANTY," AT THE
PALACE THEATRE, SYDNEY.
Chiller Green (Bert Bailey) on the track
of work.
the original was likewise. Anyhow, a lot
of better-known actresses could play the
difficult part with much less appreciation.
But the most striking character introduced
is “Chiller” Green, the pride of the “push.”
As a support of distressed Virtue and a
nark ’ to Villainy, especially when it sports
a, pigtail, Bert Bailey dominates the entire
piece and to a considerable extent drags the
dramatic interest behind his high-heeled per-
sonality. On the low comedy of Chiller and
Minnie, his clinah (a lady rouseabout, for-
merly of “push” ■ society K the author has
spread himself, and, but for occasional ex-
aggeration in “clobber.” he has great reason
to be satisfied with the return he receives
from Bert Bailey and that clever young
actress, Laura Roberts.
The mam conspiracy in the play is not
the original one of a colony of Chow fos-
sickers to get possession of the Golden
Shanty, though that is a prominent side
issue, but the location and pegging-out of a
rich quartz reef. This gives the regulation
hero, heroine and villain (the old team —
Guy Hastings, Ada Oakley and Richard
Bellairs) the opportunity to earn their sal-
aries. Of the Chinese characters,
Arthur Bertram, as Ah Cow, can take
three cheers to himself. No other
“Pong” is in the same province. And
Guy Hastings, hero and selector, is as able
as usual. Several of the others in the cast
are not lip to the standard of the leads nor
the requirements of ,tjje show. Probably
they’ll improve, but, before they get settled
down, some of parts, particularly those
of the derelicts and fan tod specialists, want
the pruning Shears. An improvement
has been made, and by the time this
sees print “The Golden Shanty” should be
running more smoothly. The scenery is well
done by Robert Vaughan. The opportunity
to enjoy a good laugh and to hear some of
the smartest and quaintest dialogue that
has occurred in Sydney for a long time will
continue to occur until further advice.

The Booze King (Fred McDonald) farewells 6 '

“A Marriage of Convenience,” the dainty
costume play with which the Waller-
Titheradge co. is filling one fugitive week
at Sydney Royal, will be just finishing its
brief run when this Bulletin is being read
by the playgoer with a sprig of golden wattle
in his buttonhole and Spring in his heart.
Consequently, this paper feels like the pant-
ing wedding guest, who, while hurrying at
top speed to the festivity, knows quite well
that he will arrive too late to dash his two
pounds of confetti on the bridegroom’s
neck. “The Marriage” is one of the stage’s
everlastings, and it blooms in its beautifully
delicate and artificial way at long intervals.
Probably it wouldn’t have faded off so
soon if Sydney had been itself. Grundy’s
adaptation of Dumas’ love story, with its
beautiful clothes and manners and pictur-
esque immorals, affords Waller the best
chance he has had thus far. He gives a
fine, bevelled-edge impersonation of the
Comte de Candale—the elegant husband who
allows his young wife’s admirer to infest the
house. Madge Titheradge, as the fluttering
little convent-trained Comtesse, is another
sort of Butterfly—a charming, highly-colored,
eighteenth-century variety. Marianne Clad-
well plays brightly as Marton, the maid;
Reginald Dane is perfectly satisfactory as
Valclos, the magnificent hanger-on; and Hal-
liwell Hobbes, as an ancient and shocked
fire-eater, seems quite probable. On Satur-
day night “A Fair Highwayman” will be
staged for the first time in Australia, under
the personal direction of author William
Devereux.

That earnest young press agent—well, his
name need not be mentioned—has been utter-
ing plaintive and scandalised cries in this
office and elsewhere concerning the writer’s
remarks about the Genee show at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney). If press agency is
persevered with long enough it gets
into the blood. and either blights
severely or totally annihilates the sense
of proportion. The victim sees malice,
combined with imbecility, irreligion and
a tendency to drink rum at breakfast, in
all comment on the boom of the moment
which is not absolutely, boundless in its adu-
lation. When in that state the agent really
believes the multitudinous things which it is
his business to clamor about his firm’s reign-
ing idol. Of course the writer said nothin
that the normal human being could regard ««
derogatory to Gen6e—a dancer whom he ha
admired heartily for 15 years or more wC
he did mention was that 21' hours of
her was over the odds, to the extent s 0 far
as he was concerned, of being boresome • and
to that great truth he still adheres. T 0 ho
unable to distinguish between that point of
view and an attitude of pronounced hostility
is simply to be —well, a prqss agent.

Deaths, 39; seriously smashed up 47 •
slightly wounded, 84; escaped, two—a nigger
who should have died and a hero who mwer
should have lived. That is as near as Thf
BunLETrN can get to the score at Sydney
Adelphi on Saturday, the first night of “The
Queen of the White Slaves.” The villain
of last season and the season-before is
“chucked” by the usual hero’s sweetheart
He yells for the same old revenge and
Fong Low (a Chow Prince) aids and abets
him just as Lord Marmaduke did in
the first mellowdrama that ever was
The. choicest attractions are—(l) the
moving walls, between which the hero
and . heroine are dropped by the Chow
villain to be gently squoze (and would
have been had a semi-hero minded
his business) ; (2) the flaming pit, from
which the villain escapes only after a long
fight (casualties, about 30. Hereabouts a
parson, who was sitting next the writer
got up and left) ; (3) the row on the
yacht, which ends in the heroine’s brother
(an opium fiend; being heaved overboard
followed by the hero; (4) the rescue
of the couple on the raft, by which time the
opium-smoker is an uninteresting corpse-
and (fifthly and finally) the Great Inva-
sion of China by eleven supers. At
odd times there are 29 or so other violent
deaths and numerous casualties, of which
only a blurred recollection remains. In
one of the placid moments of the play the
heroine and her brother are abducted from
Frisco by the villain, the Chow Prince
and numerous Chow servants ; but in such a
welter the sight of a dozen Chows, one of
them in princely robes, and a gent, with a
silk hat dragging a squealing and kicking
female through the streets of ’Frisco, is not
enough to arouse any suspicion or much
interest. Incidentally, the scenery is ex-
cellent. Miss Nellie Fergusson is quite a
success as the wronged heroine; Herbert
Leigh has charge of the impossible Chow
prince, Hilliard Vox of the still more im-
possible bad egg. and Cyril Mackay of the
utterlv impossible hero. There is no loss
of life among the audience so far.
