While the Sydney Crystal Palace was
unwinding the mile or so of film that the
cinema-production of “Ivanhog” called for
and got, much to the satisfaction of every-
one who saw it, the Colonial was enjoying
the excitement of a Wild West “round-up”
in Law and the Outlaw.” This picture
is as chock-full of daredevilism as any “cow-
boy” picture to date. At the same theatre a
scenic of the snowy ranges in Colorado and
thereabouts provides. those artistically in-
clined with much edifying entertainment.
On Saturday Miss Willette Whittaker
and Wilbur Hill, purveyors of various
brands of harmony, appealed against a con-
viction recorded at the Sydney Tivoli eight
, a£r ° - Their defence is after the style
ot Nella . Webb’s, only 50 per cent, more
entertaining and less liable to become
wearisome. Miss Whittaker provides the
songs and harp, and Hill the fiddle accom-
paniment. The Court upholds the appeal.
Other items . also worth while are
those of Parish, the acrobatic musician;
Menan s Dogs, the Primroses and the
Golden Troupe.

The freak and novelty dances that are just
now advertising Uncle Sam about as much
as his Panama Canal have a pair of smart
interpreters in Marcell and Bell at the
National (Sydney). Another dancer at the
show, Huntress, appeals more to the classic
taste. Those who are not satisfied either
way are squared, with a long helping of acro-
batics, music, singing (which isn’t always
the same thing), comedies, and so on, the
so on” being extremely varied.

There is little to add to the foregoing
except that Allen Doone gave a mild and
innocuous portrayal of the name character
at Melbourne Princess’s on Saturday. Miss
Edna Keeley, Miss Bashford, Tom Buckley,
Prank .Cullenane and others thickened the
proceedings with Hibernian brogue, but the
play on the whole demanded more assistance
than they -could give it.

After musical life’s fitful fever of 17
operas in 16 days at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
the earnest patrons of Quinlan are presum-
ably sleeping well. To-night (Thursday)
they will leave the theatre to be rushed by
plain, ordinary customers who have been
crowded out of the procession. A final
performance of “Hoffmann” is billed, and
revivals of “Tannhauser,” “Louise” and
“Aida” are to follow in that order. Last
week the Wagner “Ring” series was com-
pleted by capital productions of “Siegfried”
and the still more lengthv “Gotterdammer-
un£>” or “Twilight of the Gods.” One
trouble with Siegfried” is the prevalence

As regards Tannhauser,” an insufficiency
ot brazen uproar and the uncouthness of
the \ enusberg ballet are the ojily serious
detects in an impressive performance. The
ballet reminds one of the historic Wain-
wright, who could think of no particular rea-
son why he murdered his girl relative except
that her ankles were thick. In “Aida ” the
most evenly-balanced of all the Quinlan pro-
ductions to date, Edna Thornton and Brola
repeated their fine acting of a year ago, and
T V.- J- was again the Ethiopian
King. The black monarch is not Samuell’s
best character, however. Instead of pre-
senting a noble savage down on his luck, he
almost realises one’s conception of Will
Whitburn in a terrible scot. As for tenor
Maurice D’Oisly as Radames, his sweet
voice must be incapable of any effort to
speak otherwise it would have got going
in the famous aria. D’Oisly is stodgy and
repressed in every hero part he plays.
* * * *
Not that the sedate tenor was all to blame
i j feebleness as the ardent lover who
led Charpentier’s “Louise” astray. The
I rench opera is very weak in its tenor char-
acter, otherwise it is a strong, haunting
composition. Charpentier supplied his own
libretto, which hits harder than the mawkish
stones of ‘Bohfeme” and “Camille,”
whilst the music, which seems to imitate
the melodiousness of Puccini in the first
act, develops a sweet manner of its own as
the opera proceeds. It fascinates at a first
hearing, and explains why “Louise” ran
100 consecutive nights in Paris. In the;
first and last acts the melodrama has mo-
ments of human family interest. The street
scene, though wholly superfluous, is strong
in its touches of character, and the two
rowdy scenes for the chorus have each a
dramatic climax. Louise is the eternal
young feminine who breaks awav from
parental control to lead the sinful life, with
a lover. Mother lures her back by telling
her ; father is ill. When he gets better he
can’t bounce her into remaining virtuous.
Ultimately the vituperative, loving dad
throws a kitchen chair after Louise as she
makes her get-away, and her fond, scolding
mother pities herself for being unsympa-
thetic. Jeanne Brola (the daughter)' and
Edna Thornton (mother) act exceedinglv
well, Samuell makes a hit as the rugged
father, Spencer Thomas is valuable, and
many others figure on the bill. Scenery and
lighting help to prevent this long novelty
from becoming tedious, yet there is no rea-
son why, with a shortening of the waits
between acts, the start should not be made
at 7.30 instead of seven.

At Melbourne Royal the heavenly smile
of Nathaniel Niblo continues to attract the
womenkind of Radville Park to poor old
Sam Graham’s drug store. Everybody
speaks kindly of “The Fortune Hunter.”
whilst noting the fact that “Wallingford”
has, in a measure, reduced it to an echo, of
the patent carpet-tack comedy. Happily
the differences between the Wallingford of
yesterday and the Nathaniel of now are all
in favor of Niblo’s personal claim to adora-
tion, so Miss Enid Bennett, who snatches
the prize packet from the grasp of Rad-
ville’s, leading heiress, is about the most
envied little girl .iji Melbourne these times.
On the other hand, Nathaniel is regarded
as a lucky dog.

At Melbourne Opera House all is as it
was a week ago, except that the De Bruns
have just waltzed back into the bill. The
Hoboes, the Saxones, and Diamond and
Beatrice are staying on for a while; also
Lola, whose personal attractions are
assisted by a very moderately beautiful
waible, and Martynne, who has few attrac-
tions. Billie Reeves, the Mullers,
blynn and McLoughlin and Whitehead
are leaving to-night (Thursday), but the
lamentable roosters will remain on their
foolish job until to-morrow.
** * #
Wirths’ Circus, being a distinct improve-
ment on many previous shows of its kind
that Melbourne has seen, is getting its
name up at the Hippodrome building. On
the stage and in the ring two dozen items
come and go. The elephants, of course, are
bh®, weightiest feature on the programme
( feature is the right word here, for even
hant’s face must weigh about
llzlb.) ; moreover, Toby, the crafty old
leader of the gang, has learned a new trick
since the polar bears threatened to put his
long nose out of. joint. But the star turn
this circus time is La Belle Victoria. Vic-
toria on the swinging wire is a thing of
beauty and a joy.

“ Tannhauser.”
Tannhauser (Fra. cis
Costa).
Many people who had lately seen
* anny’s First Play” put through by
McMahon and his amateurs, at the Athen-
aeum Hall, hastened to meet the comedy
again at the Bijou on Saturday night. Nat-
urally Fanny’s effusion gets a better showing
at the theatre, with its adequate facilities
for staging and lighting, and in most re-
spects it is better acted : yet the difference
between pros, and amateurs hardly calls
for a donation of bouquets to the Buckler
co. The ladies are the weakness of the pres-
ent combination. Miss Shirley Huxley in
the giddy part of Dora Delaney does least
to justify her obvious faith in herself: she
is. stagey and self-conscious. Nor is Miss
Violet Paget always, convincing as Margaret
Knox, or Miss Lilian Dloyd as Fanny
O’Dowda. Miss Pigott and Miss Stephen-
son are good enough, as a rule. Among the
men, Kenneth Brampton as Bobby Gilbey
is an entertainment all the time: Alfred
Bristowe is palpably disconcerted by the
part of Lieutenant Duvallet; and Hugh
Buckler’s Juggins, despite his garb, resembles
& white-headed hero in a picture play.
Lardley Turner as Count O’Dowda revives
the ideas of the 18th century, and the sus-
tained vigor of Arthur Cornell keeps Bobby
Gilbey’s old parent alive.
** * *
The feature of the bill at Melbourne Olym-
pia on Saturday was “The Dead Secret,” the
story of a careless female and a baby. Judg-
ing by its mamma’s casualness in handing it
over to a demented stranger, the infant was
exceedingly reckless in its choice of a parent.
“The Honor of a Soldier” was an ancient
story badly told, and “A Victim of Hered-
ity” crudely suggested that a burglar’s off-
spring wants to play with’ a jemmy almost
before he begins to teeth. “Highway of the
Alps” was a fsne strip of Alpine scenery, but
the comic “Amateur Juggler” was too slow
in action, and is hereby cautioned for loiter-
ing.
if * *
“Struck Oil” is renewing its popularity
at Melbourne Kihg’s for a limited number
of nights. Maggie Moore in any old drama
is a winner from the start, and Maggie,
with her renowned plait of pale Dutch hair
hitched on. and her mouth-organ in her
pocket, and her warm ’art alwavs in the
right place, gets away from the barrier as
quickly as ever. The co. looks much the
same collection of familiar figures that lived
in Oilsburg of yore. J. F. Forde and C.
Brown are there again, and Roberts is the
sa.me good old John who returns to life
with a damaged head and a long-lost
memory.
“D.”: From the S. M. Herald's report (B. l/9/’l3)
of the Genee show:—
“The curtain rises on a splendidly-set hunting
scene. Nothing is left out at all. Even the setters
are there.”
There is something wrong about this. No horse that
was ever foaled could last throughout a sharp burst
over a stiff line of country with a pack of setters.
The Herald must have meant bulldogs.

And Lady Mary Bannon runs the Hall.
Swate Mary is a haughty “piece,” a pure
and proud colleen, P
Determined to keep Barry in his place;
She fain would stab his feelings with a wit
which isn’t keen,
But he warbles songs beguiling in a voice of
verdant green,
And gets his smiling answer on her face.
He “narks” her villain suitor (who is titled

By all the tattered peasants in the play)
So they meet in mortal combat; and the

When he might have put the baronet to bed

Which stamps him as a Hero, right away.
And thus the dauntless songster wins his
lady at the last,
But ere he’ll grab the prize that he has
won
He means to bring Lord Bannon, with the
territory vast,
To recognise the offspring he deserted in the
past—
And when Tom means to do a thing, it’s
done!
The noble deeds of Barry could be numbered
by the score ;
His artless jests are scattered o’er the
plot:
His ballads are suggestive of the late
lamented Moore:
Shure Ballymore’s his castle, as was stated
once before,
And the ballad of the bard is Ballyrot.
VICTOKIA.
JEAN SIBI.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Although perfervid choreographiques, soul-
asthetes, peripatetic philosophers and deeply erudite
members of the cognoscenti may not impossibly
desire to hurl the clod of contumely and reprobation
at the Philistine who propounded the Genfie critique
(B. 21/8/’l2), yet after profound and bottomless
consideration of the show, one feels that his propo-
sitions were, on the whole, accurate. Viewed with
the dispassionate eye of the Morning After, all these
pirouettes and abundant calisthenics and castings
of the human frame through the circumambient
atmosphere towards the welkin' may be described as
superfluous. Why does a dancer go up ? That she
may come down again. Why does she come down?
That she may go up again. “All that can be said of
her is that when she is up she is up, and when she
is down she is down, and when she is half-way up
or half-way down she is neither one thing nor ‘the
other ; but is half-and-half, which is the same as
shandygaff, or even dog’s-nose (beer and gin). It
is all a leaping of supererogation. It is a case of
the unfixed throwing herself perpendicularly against
the unattainable. She is trying to hitch her back-
hair to a star and leave her hinder hairpin in the
Milky Way—and she doesn’t. A dancer is an in-
complete aeroplane and an inefficient balloon. She
rises, but she doesn’t stay risen long enough, and
she can’t take up any passengers with her, or any
luggage, or any dynamite to drop on a hostile city.
She represents ambition, but not attainment. She
whirls, certainly, but she doesn’t whirl half so
effectively as the solar system. She revolves, but
after the French Revolution all other circular
attempts are poor and ordinary. To dance person-
ally is good, for it is healthy exercise, even if you
tread on your partner’s feet. (There is a case in
history of a commander-in-chief who lost his job
because he weighed about 1801 b., and he trampled
shamefully on the Queen’s feet at a Court ball, yet
all the same it was healthy for the commander-in-
chief). But to look at someone else dancing doesn’t
interest me. lam thrilled every time the lady rises,
but I am mad every time she falls. There was a
better fall in an early chapter of Genesis. With
bitterness, I note that she is back in the same old
place, and I want my money back in the same old
place, but can’t get it.
Jimmy Nunn, for the last 14 years Bilanderson’s
stage manager, retires from the profesh. James is
a much-travelled mummer. He has been pursued by
the spotlight through the United States, Hingland,
China, Japan, India and Egypt—he was the first
English mime to take a company to Cairo. He first
plastered his grave countenance with grease-paint
38 years ago, since when he’s used no other. He
served with Bandmann, William Creswick and W.E.
Sheridan, was in Rignold’s first production of “Henry
>•” Sydney Royal, at a later date was stage
manager for Rignold, and supported Mrs. Brown-
Potter and “Curly” Bellew during their first Mel-
bourne season. Twenty-three years ago he was in
the company with George Miln and Louise Jordan
had here, and in “Othello” Nunn alternated the
parts of lago and the Black Agony with Miln himself.
In “The Merchant of Venice” he was Gratiano, and
at his benefit matinee at Melbourne King’s next
Tuesday he will play Shylock in the alleged trial
scene, with Eugenie Duggan as Portia.
“0.K.”: Of Wagner’s four “Ring” operas, “The Val-
kyrie’ appears to be most popular, for it is the one
that is often played apart from the other three.
Yet the more “The Valkyrie” is considered by
sober music lovers, the more unnecessarily long-
winded it seems. The scene between Wotan, the
cronk-eyed god, and Brunnhilde, his daughter, at the
end of the opera, is a tedious extension of one idea.
The heavy father dooms his noisy girl to sleep for
an indefinite period within a zone of protecting
flames (intended to keep vicious tramps and disre-
spectful animals away), and she begs that only a
hero may at last rescue her from her hot corner.
This concession the old god decides to grant. Then
he puts her to sleep and sets the fire raging. But
the time they take to arrive at this conclusion!
One would not expect them to hurry, but their
deliberateness is over the odds. Their posing whilst
they sing is in keeping with the matter of their
discourse and supernatural character, but their tre-
mendous silent pauses whilst the band plays are
mere dallyings with the inevitable. Wagner is old-
fashioned. He lived in the time of heavily padded
novels, slow travelling and protracted theatrical
entertainments. Tragedies which ended at midnight
were preceded by curtain-raisers at 6.30 in Wagner’s
time. Shakspearian actors mouthed their speeches to
preposterous lengths when he was making operatic
history. Hustle and bustle were unknown. Sick
people dredged themselves with physic for ills which
are now cured by tabloids. The genius of condensa-
tion and impressionism had not yet come into litera-
ture, music and art. The tendency everywhere was
to go slow. And Wagner went slow —darned slow.
Musical cranks will tell you that not a bar of
Wagner’s opera music can be spared, that it is all
essential to the effect of the work, as a whole. But
who are the Wagnerian cranks? Lineal descendants
of the old Shakspeare devotees who thought that no
manager could cut a line of the Immortal Bard
without maiming and disfiguring the wordy play.
“Macko”: At Harrie Ireland’s benefit, I see that
Walter Baker gave Tennyson's “Charge of the Light
Brigade.” As a rule, I would run a mile rather
than listen to that recitation ; but to hear Baker
give it, I would willingly miss my last tram. Baker,
unfortunately, was the adopted son of Drury Lane
drama. With a fine appearance and a beautifully
sonorous voice, he should have played sopiethino-
better. He had good chances, too. Nat Ooodwin
wanted him for an American tour, and Rankin
offered him the part of Macbeth with Nance O’Neil;
but Walter was unable to close with either offer.
He was bound by other contracts. When I think
of the puny importations who have starred here, I
want to know why Baker, a sound actor and’ a
Shakespearian scholar, plays breezy melodramatics, or
is allowed to “rest.”
“0.K.” : A recent “Poverty Point” notice of the death
°i. Edward Poole, “the Australian Roscius” of the
mid ’so’s, is not quite correct. His father (Pole)
was a tailor at Ballarat—not Melb.—when the aspir-
ing Edward got stage-struck, and it was at the
Montezuma Theatre in the mining towm that the
young man made his debut as a star. He had no
personal qualifications for playing hero parts. It
was only the old man’s money that kept him going
Nobody, save the proud paternal tailor, accented
Edward as the “Australian Roscius,” even in those
days, when the local profesh was in its infancy.

“Lex” : On July 3, 1912, a carcase butcher
named Field was asked a question as wit-
ness before the N.S.W. Pure Foods Commis-
sion. He refused to answer it, on the ground
that to do so would injure his carcase busi-
ness. Whereupon he was adjudged to be in
contempt of court. Dissatisfied, he has been
arguing the points in the law courts ever
since. The Full Court has now decided the
wrangle, and at the same time has smitten
with a severe club on the heads of long-
winded Royal Commissions. It was found
that Letters Patent created the P.F.C. on
July 3, 1911, with a life of four months.
Extensions were granted from time to time
by some kindly disposed person, but that
mild courtesy didn’t affect the Letters
Patent, which fatally petered out on Novem-
ber 2, 1911. As the carcase butcher shied at
the question put him on July 3, 1912. it fol-
lowed that he had escaped the penal powers
granted to the Royal Commission. To what
extent the doings of the Commission, which
had been sitting for two years after its first
appointment, were valid the Court didn’t say.
All it was asked to do was to settle the
argument between it and the stubborn car-
case trafficker, and it said that the Commis-
sion didn’t have a leg to fly with.

The week’s ecclesiastical note:—
Australian Anglicans from all parts of the Com-
monwealth meet in Brisbane from September 9 to 17
for a Church Congress. The meetings, which differ
from synods in that any person may attend who
chooses, and that no legislation is attempted, are
held every five years, the last having been at Perth
in 1908. A similar body meets annually in Eng-
land. It was at one such gathering—at Birming-
ham in 1893-—that a relative of Oom Bill Lvne
caused a sensation. The present Bishop of Oxford—
the Liberal prelate who refused to join the protests
against Church Disestablishments, and voted for the
Welsh Disestablishment Bill in the Lords—was then
the Rev. Charles Gore, and was down to read a paper
at the congress. As Gore was called upon, the Rev.
Joseph Leycester Lyne—better known as “Father
Ignatius”—strode up the hall in the garb of a monk
and shouted: “In the name of Jesus Christ, I forbid
Charles Gore to speak.” The explanation lay in
Gore’s alleged unsoundness as to the precise time
Jonah remained inside the whale, or some similar
Biblical heresy. But Gore spoke, and is now a
Bbhop, while poor old Father Ignatius, after being
“inhibited” from preaching in nearly every diocese
in England, died some years ago in his North Welsh
“abbey,” of which he was at last almost the only
“monk.” The Brisbane congress will be chiefly
notable as being the largest assemblage of Anglican
gaiters on record in Australia. Twenty-four bishops
will attend, several of them from abroad. Among
the subjects for discussion is the good old “Social
Unrest.” The conservative party is not represented
on this topic, as the three selected readers of papers
—Bishop Mercer, of Tasmania ; Dean Stephen, of Mel-
bourne ; and Parson H. N. Baker, of Svdney—-are all
Labor supporters. Ex-Premier McGowen, of N.S.W.,
is to talk on “The Function of the State in the
Making of Citizens,” but is not consulted as an
authority on Sunday-schools. He is the only poli-
tician hilled-; and a solitary pressman-—L. V. Biggs,
of Melbourne Age —is also among the pro-
phets who are entered for the race. He
is down to explain how Australian conditions
affect church attendance. Mostly they seem to re-
duce it to vanishing point, so his task should be
easy. “Autonomy”—otherwise known as “the nexus
problem” l —is a star subject. It isn't something to
eat; but it comes practically to be something to
wear. Nationalist instincts among Australian-born
Ann’icans are kicking against their Church being
called “the Church of England in Australia,” and
being governed by English church law and Privy
Council decisions. They want an Australian name
and independence in government. North Queensland
and W.A. —the democratic centres —head the move-
ment, and the best of the imported clergy are on
the nationalist side; among them, Bi-hops Thomas,
of Adelaide: Goldsmith, of Bunbury (W.A.) ; Riley, of
Perth ; Feetham, of N.Q. ; White, of Carpentaria ; and
Archbishop Donaldson, of Brisbane. Donaldson is
president of the congress. But the silly vestments
squabble comes into the matter. Anglicans of the
“Low,” or Orange, variety are afraid that if the
Church becomes Australian instead of English, it
will have sense enough to let clergy wear any vest-
ments they please so long as their congregations
don’t object; and so they cling to the Engli-h con-
nection and to some aneffent Privy Council decisions
which stamp “vestments” (chasubles, dalmatics,
amices, fanons and other weird-sounding garments)
as illegal. Archgaiters Wright, of Sydney, who was
specially imported by Sydney “Low” churchmen to
put down vestments, heads the anti-Australian party.
A Sydney evening paper described him as “opposed
to the wearing of garments in church.” He takes a
very subordinate place in the Brisbane congress,
being put on to preach a perfunctory opening ser-
mon as Primate, but not being given any part in
the subsequent deliberations. But if “High” church-
men dominate this congress and the rest of Aus-
tralia, Sydney “Low” churchmen can afford to laugh
(for the present), as they absolutely control the
most populous and wealthy diocesa, with the Pri-
macy attached, and refuse to let ffito it any more
parsons, or to give much share in its govern-
ment to those already there. How is it that in
politics, ecclesiastical and secular, Sydney always
manages to be out of touch with the rest of the
Commonwealth? Probably because it is so engrossed
with Sydney that it hasn’t yet discovered Australia.
* * * *
Died at Richmond (Vic.) on August 26,
Mrs. M. A. Astley, who was born in Bligh-
street, Sydney, on the site of the Union
Club House, in 1834. In that year Robert
Campbell lived there—in one of the finest
Sydney houses of the time. He built it in
the '2o’s. On the death of Mrs. Campbell,
about 1860, the Union Club came into pos-
session. and for some years occupied the
place. Then it was demolished to make room
for - the present club-house—designed, with
the exception of the dining room, by W. W.
Wardell, the architect of St. Mary’s, in
Sydney, and the Governor-General’s resi-
dence in Melbourne. Campbell’s old store
still stands at the rear.

Given up, even by Lloyd’s reinsurance
gamblers, the four-masted U.S. timber
schooner Americana, which, heavily laden,
left the Pacific slope for Sydney some months
back. It sailed in comnany with the Lyman D.
Foster, and the two were together 23 days
out. Then a devil of a zephyr blew the
L.D.F. ashore at Suva. The Americana,
with its crew of 15, presumably blew into
the Port o’ Davy Jones. The cook of the
boat fortunately missed his passage, owing
to a disagreement with the then skipp'er,
which ended in the sudden death of the
latter. Slushy was comfortably at anchor
in a San Francisco gaol cell, doing four
years for manslaughter, the night his old
pals fought their last fight with the infuri-
ated sea.
“History-cuss”: Modern journalists
prattle innocently about the Bulgars’ alleged
deeds being “unparalleled in history.” They
have short memories. It is only a, century
ago that the cultured French, on taking
Saragossa-—we have the sober Napier’s word
for it- —issued orders that every woman
the city, between the ages of 13 and 50,
should attend at. the Town Hall that even-
ing, and should leave all her clothing at
home ; and death was the penalty for failing
to be there. The English were never quite
so organised in their vices as the French.
But one need not go beyond Lever’s rollicking
novels to learn the fate of non-combatants
in a captured town. A besieged place was
encouraged to give in quietly and get a
lighter sentence, like a modern criminal. If
it held out, and then was found guilty—
that is, was taken by storm, at last —it had
to pay for giving extra trouble. The Bul-
garian general’s remark that his men couldn’t
be debarred from their little relaxations is
called cynical or brutal to-day; yet it has
been said in simple earnest by many a Brit-
ish commander up to Wellington’s time. It
is only since the long peace which followed
Waterloo that the illogical idea of fighting
with a gloved hand has come in—and it may
do Australian “peace” cranks good to re-
member that there is no assurance that it
has come in for good. In any case, as the
Bulgarian experience proves, it has only
come in patches.
One of. this world’s long-distance circum-
stances is the person, usually American, who
blows into these parts with an intimation
that he is walking round the world to win
a vast prize put up by Phineas Q. Spring-
buffer, of Syracuse (U.S.A.) ; that lie has
already done 175.39 G miles in 150 years
without anybody hearing of him ; and that
he lias only to do another 92,111 miles in
100 years to win the prize. He blows in,
he gets a paragraph in the local dailies, he
blows out again; and obscurity swallows
him un. Some people think he really
started from Strathfield and walked to Syd-
ney ; or that he lighted out from Heidelberg
and . reached Melbourne partly on foot and
partly by tramcar; or that he began his
world’s march from Cleveland and got nu
farther than Brisbane. Anyhow, he is here
again, and he is the same old weariness.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Percy Hunter, Director of the Immigration
and Tourist Bureau, returned to Sydney yesterday.
Mr. Hunter, during his travels, has seen much, learnt
much and formed many opinions. But Mr. Hunter
is even more secretive than the Premiers, who, in
their conferences, had the advantage of his experi-
ence. He would not talk when he arrived. Mr.
Hunter would not submit to the process of inter-
viewing. Mr. Hunter would not even indicate the
probable date of his return to England.—News item.

“Binnacle”: An unconfirmed London
cablegram says that the British Board of
Trade has now decided that officers in the
mercantile marine sitting for higher grade
certificates won’t have to pass color vision
tests. Just what is meant isn’t clear. When
some years ago an _ aged and infirm and
almost blind pilot piled a P. and O liner
on Point Nepean (Port Phillip Headsi
ridiculous and stringent precautions were
t3.k6D, and tiiG l>oard doubled its eyesight
tests, for everybody except pilots. A curious
feature about these tests is that none are
required as to the eyesight of the man at
the wheel or the look-out for’ard The
whole responsibility is throwm on the officer
on the bridge. If his vision fails him sud-
denly at a pinch trouble is almost certain
to arise owing to no auxiliary vision bein«'
hand)’. He stands to lose his job ■ the eo°
stands to lose its ship ; those on board stand
to lose their lives.

birth in 1911 alone; but nobody
has so far been mad enough to suggest the
abolition of childbirth in consequence.

Melbourne will have to get along without
a State Governor on the Carnival job when
next Cup week comes to pass. John Fuller,
wrapned Id thought, slipped quietly away
by the Adelaide express las£ week to catch
the P. and O. steamer for England. The
Fuller Ex. has three months’ leave of ab-
sence. The first official announcement of
his being about to go away stated that his
Lady would “probably” return with him,
but "the record of his departure made no allu-
sion to this interesting probability. Any-
how’, they will both be missing from the
festive Cup scene unless her tall, blonde
Ladyship is going to await her John on the
wharf ready to hustle him aboard the next
outgoing boat.

“Brolga” : Are these defence preparations
all flam, or does Australia really want us
to learn how to use a rifle? Up here in the
North of Queensland, where a few good
marksmen may be a valuable possession
some day, about 50 of us, mostly young
farmers and permanent settlers in the dis-
trict, decided that it was up to us to do
something. YY r e couldn’t join a cadet corps,
and there was nothing else for it but a rifle
club. So we sent in our application—this
was last April—and were told that we
should be gazetted as soon as the proposed
site was inspected. Weeks passed, with the
enthusiasm of some of the 60 oozing out, as
it always will when nothing is done, and
then the Department was stirred up. The
reply was to the effect that the business was
being rushed along furiously, but that an
inspection couldn’t be made till November.
Of course the 60 ought to be just as enthu-
siastic in November as they were in March;
but I put it to Millen, Minister for Defence:
Supposing he had got hold of a man and
persuaded him to buy a homestead lease at
Bourke in March, what would he think of
himself and his chance of earning a com-
mission if he then let the negotiations go
cold and didn’t broach the subject again till
November?
* * * #
Almost any old gentleman, when roused,
will throw out his chest and tell you he was
a d—d sight happier when he worked 14
hours out of every 24 for an absurdly small
wage, and had to walk five miles to and
from work. If the listener is inhuman
enough to doubt him, he will try to have an
epileptic fit by way of proving his boast.
The harmless brasrging of elderly gentlemen ,
is all very well in suburban drawing-rooms,
after business hours; but it is annoying tm
get it during the sitting of a Wages Board,
which exists to collect facts and serviceable
information. Last week, the Miners’ Wages
Board, sitting at Newcastle (N.S.W.), got
hold of a mining superintendent, who in-
sisted on telling the members how much more
he enjoyed life when he was a miner, and
how absurdly safe the job was. He wouldn’t
admit there was any daneer in mining; he
was “as hanpy as a lark” while he was at
it; and he was often tempted to go back to
it. Of course, talk of that sort is no earthly
use to anyone, and the time of the Board
was squandered. These old persons forget
that it wasn’t the hard work, the low pay
and the five-mile walk to the job and home
again which made them feel so glad. It
was just Youth—glorious Youth. Even a
kitten is full of joy, while a grandmother
cat is staid and occupied with sad reflec-
tions, and sits gloomily on its tail. It isn’t
the necessity for toil, and the poor pay, and
the long tramp outward in the morning to its
plate of milk, and the long tramp home to
its mouse in the evening that makes the
kitten glad. It is glad simply because it is
a kitten.

dence cackle in the

country.” There are

when his vote comes

while later dozens of

differences with a view
to. sinking food and

disturbance is to end

partly provided by

“Chasuble”: Consternation seizes Mel-
bourne Baptists—Fred Spurr resigns from
Collins-street, after four years of triumph !
He has swelled too big. London did not
appreciate him at the right value until he
captured Melbourne; now he “goes Home”
to some good billet. Yes, Home! English
clerics in Australia, be they Clarkes, Spurrs
or Wrights, alwavs have their eyes in the
backs of their heads. So Leyton Richards
of the Collins-street Independent, goes ’Ome
too, after only three years. What encourage-
ment is it for Australian college men when
they must climb an impossible greasy pole
before they can reach the prize on top? The
Collins-street Baptist has imported all its
pastors: Taylor, Hill. Martin. Chapman,
Carey. Spurr. So with the Collins-street
Independent with its Henderson. Hebditeh,
.Tones, Hamer, Bevan and Richards. Give
it a rest! Or go the whole hog and import
our editors and Judges and Prime Ministers.

WHEN THE AD. IS YOUNG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
meet eS rw C nPnfl e }i dark young woman > 45 - wishes to
meet respectable young man, view above.
Advertisement in Melbourne Age.
The flying crows of Time have left their footprints
near her eyes;
But middle age has mellowed her, and she is kind
and wise;
It matters not that Autumn is to her Life’s cheerful
Spring ;
I d rather wed her than some bird who’s still upon
the wing ;
Some tender, flutt’ring thing
Who wants to own and boss the earth and social
top-notes sing.
Take not the young bird from the nest, if you’ve a
tender heart,
For young things want a splendid nest and high
roosts at the start;
They dream at dizzy altitudes and look with lofty
scorn
Upon the small suburban cots men think tliev would
adorn.
As sure as you were born,
Mama’s soft nestling will regard that cot with lofty
scorn.
Oh, let them flap their youth away if you would
wed in peace;
Be wise and let the twenties go. Wait till the
thirties cease
Before you let your fond eye stray amongst the
chatt’ring flock;
They do not squawk so loudly now—they do not flap
and mock.
Just pick her from the flock.
They’re ready for the cages now. (New flappers are
in stock).
But if you care to wait until the first few forties
go,
Why then your eager, buxom mate will fly down to
you, so !
If you don’t care to coo and woo with sugar and
with wheat,
Then here’s your pretty, sober mate announcing her-
self, “Tweet!”
It’s winning and it’s sweet —
That trusting echo of late Spring—that matrimonial
“Tweet I”

THE MIDDLEMAN.
This is “Hop’s” deep-breathing competition. The N.S.W. State Treasurer has breathed into the ears of his colleagues that there is no more money for
railway building, and he doesn’t know where to get any. The colleagues are breathing into the ear of an English firm of capitalists, asking it to get the money
and supervise N.S.W. railway construction for 5 per cent, commission. The money-lender —on the left — is waiting to have his ear breathed into by the
English capitalist. It is all breathing, and ears.

JUSTICES POWERS, DUFEY AND RICH
dispense justice at the Australian High Court.
A droll story is told of the late Bill Knox
by a man who was a fellow-voyager with
him from England to Australia at the time
of the Melbourne financial crisis. At Port
Said a cablegram informed Knox that a lot
of banks were closed for reconstruction, and
investments in general had gone to the
deuce. He was posed on the hearthrug in
the smoking-room of an hotel when he took
the terrible message out of his pocket to
read it once again. Gazing earnestly at his
companion, Bill removed his square hat,
wiped his brow with a handkerchief, and
said: “Sir, as I stand here, I believe 1 am a
ruined man.” The ingenuousness of Knox’s
egotism was beautifully expressed in that
speech. He felt that the ruination of Knox
was as tremendous an event as the down-
fall of Napoleon at Leipsic.

sat back during the “Smokeoh” interval, reflecting
on the change brought about by “Smith’s, the Sack
Specialists,” in the calico and hessian (BAG) indus-
try ? You will doubtless reniember the dark period,
when you wanted your bags to look a picture; and,
instead, had to put up with an atrocity that burst
on the slightest provocation. Have you seen the
perfect work turned out by “Smith’s” ? Possibly
not. Well, grab your pen and ask them to submit
proofs. Be up to date. Here’s the address (do it
now) : T. Smith, Ltd., Sussex-street, Sydney.*
A brief obituary :
Died in England last week Michael Maybrick,
known to and beloved by millions as “Stephen
Adams.” He was a prolific composer of popular
music, much of it of the “sacred song” type, such as
“The Holy City,” “The Throne of Grace” and “He
Wipes the Tear from Every Eye.” Probably his
most successful song was “The Holy City,” which
is said to have brought him over £40,000 in roy-
alties. He was a man of very lovable nature, with
a deep, wide streak of religion in his composition;
but, like some other great musicians, he had also a
big streak of the wild beast in him. His brother
was .Tames Maybrick, who died of poison in 1889.
It was the untiring efforts of “Stephen Adams”
which obtained the evidence which led to the arrest
of the dead man’s wife, the notorious Mrs. May-
brick. The trial of this woman, who was an Ameri-
can, created a tremendous sensation. She was found
guilty and sentenced to death. An uproar ensued.
WATTLE DAY.
The great Australian festival, celebrated daily.
“WOT'LI you 'ave ”
The United States went mad. Thousands of Ameri-
can women tried to force the United States Govern-
ment to take action, even to the extent of thr aten-
ing to invade Canada, to secure the woman’s release.
Uncle Sam declined to make an ass of himself on,this
colossal scale. lie didn’t mind being a donkey on
a small scale, but he objected to this frightful ex-
travaganza, and just sat tight. So Mrs. Maybrick
went to the condemned cell. But she did not hang
—at the last moment the sentence was commuted
to penal servitude, and in 1904 she was liberated
under a ticket of leave on condition she took part
in no demonstrations and wrote no book. She went
to the United States, and a publishing firm an-
nounced her book. The British Government appplied
for her extradition, and, to avoid arrest, she returned
to England, and the book was never published.
“Stephen Adams” brought forward a mass of new
evidence, and wanted badly to get the ticket-of-leave
cancelled and the lady sent back to her cell, but the
heart-sick British Home Office declined to be aware
of his existence.

Ancient James Balfour, M.L.C., pre-
sident of Melbourne’s Council of Churches,
has passed out, and his painful holi-
ness and aggressive integrity as a
Christian, a politician and a man of
business were obituarily noticed at
great length in the dailies. Not a word was
said about his land-boom past. Five and
twenty years ago the holy man was some-
thing of a Get-rich-quick Balfour, and the
financial companies whereof he was chair-
man fell to pieces when the Melbourne boom
busted. Angry shareholders reviled the
name of Balfour. It smelt almost as badly
as the name of Matthew Davies, and rather
worse than that of Jimmy Munro. Rut
whereas the other fellows fell out of public
life when they overreached themselves, Bal-
four contrived to remain standing. The
pillar of respectability lost a lot of its gleam-
ing whiteness, but it stuck to its position.
Perhaps James Balfour was more honest
than some of the other fortune-hunters, and
not less honest than any of them. Anyway,
he escaped ruin. The Lord advised him to
shift his liabilities, and he got somebody else
to “carry the baby.”
Passed out at Badgery’s Creek (N.S.W.)
on August 24, Mrs. Matha Brabyn Shad-
low, aged 77, a grand-daughter of Aus-
tralia’s old chaplain, Samuel Marsden. She
was the second daughter of the parson’s
only son, Charles Simeon. Her mother was,
before her marriage Elizabeth Howard
Brabyn, the daughter of Captain John
Brabyn, of the smellful 102nd Regiment. The
late Mrs. Shadlow was born on Marsden’s
historic old homestead at St. Mary’s. It
was from this holding that the first com-
mercial exportation of Australia wool to
England was made by Marsden in the ship
Admiral Gambier, which sailed on Novem-
ber 28. 1811 —years before John Macarthur
started to send wool for sale. The Sydney
Gazette of April 37, 1813, says : “The wool
sent in the Admiral Gambier, consisting of
10 or 12 packs, has been sold, and averaged
ss. a lb.” How that price would make
squatterdom’s mouth water to-day!
“Melb” : Let others write of Knox’s exit:
me of his entry. He followed the bad old
principle that a man must not enter public
life until he has made a pile. Knox emerged
with a Grand Reception in the Melbourne
Town Hall. Two thousand invites to every-
body. Me included. Men asked. “Who the
deuce, is Knox?” but of course they went.
Me included. Knox stood at the door,
and shook hands, while a fugleman said
“Mr. Smith—Mr. Knox.” “Glad to see. you,
sir.” Then the strangers sat down to Knox’s
churchwarden pipes and bottled beer. Then
the fugleman again. “Order, gents. The
Toast of Our Host.” “For he’s a jolly good
fallow, and so say all of us.” Me included.
Then mysterious paragraphs. Mr. Knox
would contest a constituency. Like
Crummies, he couldn’t understand how
these things got into the papers. But they
did ; and Knox got into Parliament. Weli,
he was a good fellow. And always very
religious. I think it was he who proposed
that they should open proceedings every day
with prayers! Which they still do.
Gone where halos take the place of broad-
brimmed hats, William Knox, mining mag-
nate and something of a nolitician. In his
youth Willy—afterwards Bill—was a clerk
in the Bank of Victoria, but there wasn’t
enough coin in that for a born money-
spinner, and, after . lie had tried and
failed as a saddler’s ironmonger, some
parish connection or other got him
the job of secretary to the Broken
Hill Prop, when that co. was formed
by a mostly-Caledonian crowd of West
Darling squatters. Willy Knox’s name
wasn’t on the prospectus of the co.;
Charles Chappie, of Silverton, was the first
secretary, but as soon as the office was
shifted to Melbourne Knox took charge. His
first big coup .was the flotation of
the British “pup” in London for over half a
million. After that he took *» hand in the
Mount Lyell float, and presently, as his
credit balance swelled, he left off being sec-

Died in a palatial house on Neutral Bay,
Sydney, one day recently, Edwin Horace
Trudgeon Bottrell, aged 66 years. Away
back in the ’7o’s Bottrell and one R. H.
Gilbert established themselves as “surgeons,"
and had “consulting rooms” next door to the
Barley Mow pub, in Castlereagh-street. The
business of these pioneers must have flour-
ished exceedingly, for. in 1882, the pair
removed into more fashionable quarters
in Liverpool-street, where daily the
postman labored under a heavy load
of mail matter. The pair “prac-
tised” until 1896, when they were
succeeded by another “medical practitioner.”
Bottrell, as he made his pile, put it into
bricks and mortar. Gilbert, if he ever
existed, kept out of the public eye.
Squatter Andrew Tennant, of S.A.. who
left more than half a million behind him
the other day. was a fine judge sheep,
cattle, horses and men. On the West Coast
in the early days the Tennants were neigh-
bors of the Hamps, another pioneering fam-
ily, the head of w T hich was murdered by
the blacks. In his youth a grandson (W.
E. Hamp) shepherded for the late Andrew.
One day Tennant remarked of the shep-
herd boy: “That young man will go high
in the business. He minds sheep so well.''
Hamp got as high as any man in the busi-
ness in S.A., but the game worried him into
an early tomb. At 40 he looked 60. and
after the tragic death of his close friend.
Julius Grunike. he aged fast, and, when he
died recently, looked an old man. Hamp
read whilst shepherding, and had a better
head for apt ouotations than any other man
in the State. Tennant was of a philosophical
turn. One day, whilst he was taking an
afternoon nap in his city home, they
awakened him to tell him that Port Aug-
usta had telegraphed that one of his stations
was burning. “Well,” said the old man
testily, “I can't put it out from here, can I?"
The N. S. Wales Lands Department’s old
friend. Wm. Nicholas Willis, has just had a
book published in London —“Western Men
and Eastern Morals.” On quitting Aus-
tralia Wm. Nicholas went more or less into
the horse trade and pervaded Batavia. Sin-
gapore and other parts where the pretentious
nigger potentate and the truculent
Chow merchant pays big prices for
“Walers.” There Wm. Nicholas, being a
stern moralist, felt his soul scarified when
he saw his brother-white making a tempo-
rary alliance with a brown beauty —and
Wm. Nicholas’s London book is the result.
THE HIGH THINKER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Cook : “We shall strike at abuses wherever and
whenever thev rear their ugly heads. We are against
favoritism, against privilege and preference. We are
for freedom, a square deal, and just relations between
all numbers of this community.”
Mr. Pace: “Oh, stop it!”
—Argus Parliamentary Report.
The thinker of high thoughts got up and spoke a
bunch of them —-
Chicanery, crook dealing and all fraud he did con-
demn.
“We stand,” he said, for uprightness,
For freedom of the stump and press—
For all things sweet and square!” he cried.
“Yes —we’re the purest goods outside
The New Jerusalem!”
And then some silly fool broke in the stream of
speech upon ;
Rocking with sudden, vulgar mirth, he bellowed,
“Oh, go lion !”
In earnest tones the great soul spoke of blessing and
uplift,
Of holy ways, and ways in which some sinful persons
drift;
He did a balm-and-honev preach
On hopes he had of heights to reach.
“All craft and meanness we must try,”
He said, and wiped a moistured eye,
“From public life to shift.”
A coarse voice crashed across his speech, with loud
mirth megaphoned—
The voice of one who rudely roared, “Oh, don’t!”
and, grinning, groaned.
In psalmish strain the preacher proach of True Re-
form, and sich ;
Bright, new remarks on virtue did his passioned
prose enrich.
“Yes, we stand up for Freedom—now!”
He yelled. “For flag-flap rights”—and how
His helners raised the roof when he
Cried that the car of Liberty
Was wheelless in the ditch ! . . . .
Vet Freedom profits little if, when men with Brains
orate,
Rude, coarse, bold, common, jeering blokes bawl out,
“Aw —ain’t he great?”

Like most of the other English banks doing business
in Australia and Maoriland, this one has been push-
ing the foreign-exchange business for ail it is worth ;
and it is worth a lot. Everything has helped them —-
even the Dominion’s vices. For when the Dominion
airily borrows another few millions, those few
millions don’t come out in coin—they invariably
come out in goods. And when the interest has to be
paid, the Dominion ships goods to do it. The banks
pay one end and collect the other, and mostly they
can pay each end with what they collect there. In
other words, very little money goes backwards and
forwards. Mcßrown, in Dunedin, sells Smith, in
London, a thousand frozen lambs; and the bank
undertakes to send money to pay Mcßrown, and
charges for doing it. At the same time, McJones, of
Auckland, has bought 100,000 rat-traps from Robin-
son, of Birmingham ; and the bank undertakes to
send money to Robinson, and charges for doing it.
All that really is done is to send McJones’s money
from Auckland to Mcßrown in Dunedin, and Smith’s
money from London to Robinson in Birmingham.
The development of Maoriland’s frozen meat and other
export trade has enormously increased this exceed-
ingly profitable banking side-line —so increased it, in
fact, that it is scarcely a side-line any longer. This
is, no doubt, the explanation of much, if not most,
of the big spring in profits during the last few years.
And there has been a good deal of profit that the
table doesn’t show ; for £15,000 a year has been
written off premises for some time past.
Next come to some of the details of the balance-
sheet :-

Deposits have shrunk, and probably because it isn’t
likely that they will be plentiful at a cheap rate for
some time to come, it was lately decided to increase
the capital. At the end of the co.’s year (March 31)
there were 200,000 shares of £7 10s. paid to
£2 10s. ( £500,000). Authority was got to issue
another 200,000, and half of these have lately been
offered to, and taken by, old shareholders at a pre-
mium of £1 ss. This adds £250,000 to the capital
and £125,000 to the very solid reserves. Incident-
all}', it greatly strengthens the position from the
depositors’ point of view, though it was by no
means weak previously; and it also puts the bank
into a comfortable position to take a good deal more
business.

S. Aus. has been having a fine time the last few
years, and the profits of this old-established co. show
it. The wool clip has been good, prices have been
high, iambings have been satisfactory ; and the wheat
cocky has been browsing in almost as rich a patch
of clover as the bigger wool cocky. All this has
meant not only bigger commissions and very few
bad debts, but also probably the recovery of a good
many debts that had been written off as bad. Yet,
though it had begun to think otherwise, S. Aus. is
still liable to be hit by drought, as it has been re-
minded by something very like one which has been
plaguing the State this year. And when a really bad
drought comes along it isn’t a matter of knocking
out the profits of such a co. as this for one year;
the effect lasts until the flocks can be bred up again.
The very experienced people who run this business
know as well as anyone how uncertain the industry
is, even though lots of things have been done to make
it less uncertain than it used to be; hence, though
there has been year after year of great prosperity,
no larger dividend than 10 p.c. has been paid, and
when a while ago paid capital was very properly
increased from £190,000 to £350,000, there was
no talk of bonus shares —-it was hard cash that was
called up. There is a yarn told of Willy Mortlock, the
rich S. Aus. squatter who died the other day—he
had a fair holding in this co., too. “I don’t want
to make money,” he said, “but every day I pray
that' I may be able to hang on to what I’ve got.”
That was rather characteristic of the fairly-rich S.
Australian. Elder Smith’s probably want to make
money, but they certainly want to keep their cap-
ital. There are only four directors, but they can
muster about 270 years among them; and that per-
haps accounts for it. Anyway, the business has been
made very solid. Goodwill ( £ 40,856) was written
off years ago. Premises and plant, which cost
£211,385, have been steadily written down to
£133,948. (Nothing is shown in the table as
written off during the last few years, as premises
and 'plant depreciation is charged direct to profit
and loss.) Finally, while in the past eight fat vears
£211.319 has been paid to shareholders in a steady
and satisfying 10 p.c., no less than £254.998 has
been put by, in addition to what has been written off
or drooped into the invisible contingency account.
Altogether an excellent record of prudent manage-
ment. And the policy is right absolutely. For tbo
co. has to protect a heap more than its own capital
at risk in this erratic pastoral business. Here are
some more figures :• —•

This co. has made a good recovery. In 1900, when
its paid capital was only £50,000, of which no less
than £25,000 was represented by goodwill and a
few other poor trifles, it had £86,924 out in advances
and book debts ; and some of those debts were des-
perately bad. Even five years later, when dividends
had been resumed, the market said the shares were
worth only 4s. 9d., and the market was probably
right. If a valuation of the assets had been made
in 1900 it is doubtful whether, apart from goodwill,
there would have been £15,000 in excess of liabili-
ties. Since then not a penny has been called for
from shareholders; yet to-day capital has been
brought up to £lOO,OOO and a nice little reserve of
£26,776 has been accumulated; and though certainly
one can’t tell from the accounts whether the £126,776
of capital and reserves is all worth 20s. in the £,
there are these facts to comfort shareholders: (1)
the goodwill asset has been written off, the froth
being replaced by solid assets; (2) the average an-
nual profits for the past five years have shown over
17 p.c. on the present capital of £lOO,OOO. The
latter fact isn’t conclusive proof, of course, that the
assets are not overvalued ; but it is proof that, if
the assets are not highly productive, the busi-
ness is extraordinarily profitable. The chances
are that the assets are worth all they are said to be,
for a management which could so resolutely clean
up the old mess wouldn’t be likely to make another
of the same sort immediately afterwards. Further,
the fine profits have been made without borrowing
much money to lend out in more or less risky ad-
vances to other people. Here are the principal
figures:—

The total amount owing by the co. (£53,759) is
slightly less than the amounts owed to the co. on all
accounts; and, assuming that the latter debts are
good, this means that the whole of the capital and
reserves are represented by the co.’s extensive city
office premises (largely let), and the wool-stores and
sale yards. If the co. can hold its business without
making advances when the tide runs out n*xt time
and clients want money, shareholders won’t do too
much if they give three cheers for the management.
Going back for a minute to the first table, it will be
seen that the dividend of late has been anything
from 16 to 35 p.c. Actually, as a rule, a 10 p.c. cash
div. has been paid ; the balance the co. has held on
to in order to pay up the shares gradually to £l.
The paying-up has now been completed ; and though
some shareholders may itch for something more than
10 p.c., first consideration should be given to the re-
serves, which in this class of business ought to be
much stronger than they are at present.

The new co. had it valued, and in the prospectus it
was alleged to be worth £51,500. whereas all that

In the middle of the co.’s first year a div. of 9d.
ner share was declared. Brennan refused to take his
ILtle lot; hence the total paid out was only £lBB5.
That first year wasn’t a fair criterion. The co., re-
lying on having a new theatre readv in Melbourne
in December, engaged performers ; ‘ but the place
wasn't ready till four months later, and these per-
formers were thrown on the co.’s hands. The Bul-
letin won’t swear that the past year was a fair
criterion either, for work has been exceptionallv
plentiful and wages unusuallv high. A heap of this
work has been paid for with borrowed money, and
you can’t spend the same loan twice. On the con-
trary, after spending it once you’ve got to repay it
once, and until it is repaid Cohen must have his in-
terest, and what Cohen gets Brennan isn’t likely to.
The slump isn’t reaJlv here yet; nevertheless Knibbs
shows in the Labor Bulletin for the Commonwealth
issued the other dav that, on trade unions’ returns,
for every 55 men idle at the end of 1912 there were
64 at the end of March and 73 at the end of June,
and that sort of movement is going to hit amusement
enterprises as hard as anybody or anything. On
the other hand, Australia, compared with other
countries, has undoubtedly been understocked with
music halls of the better type; and with a dozen or
more halls on its “circuit” this co. can afford to
lure attractive performers with good engagements.
Still, it has a big lump of dead wood to carry. Be-
sides the £51,396 represented by paper, there is
£69,237 owing on mortgage, and that means a big
first bite out of profits in bad times as well as good
ones. The capital is £160,000.

The new Gas Act is responsible for the ragged ap-
pearance of the bottom of that table. The capital
was £66,370. Then something over six months ago
share for share was issued. That made the total
6132,740 in 66,370 £2 shares. The Act gave the
co. six months in which to capitalise any remaining
shares. Assets were thereupon revalued, and
V 67,801 was in consequence added to the reserves.
This brought the reserves to £99,555, which was
distributed in another 66,370 £2 shares, paid to
£1 10s. The old shares were called “A”; the
second lot “B.” Then 33,185 new “C” shares were
offered to “A” shareholders at par—a share for
every two old ones. Under the Act these “C” shares
can carry only 7 p.c. That limitation apparently
scared off some people, so that only 28,681 were
applied for. These are £2 shares paid so far to
4s. The “A” and “B” shares are limited to 10
p.c. ; the only way they can get more is through
a reduction of the price of gas below the standard
now charged. The old shareholders have done very
well: the man who in 1911 had 100 shares has now
(if he hasn’t soldi 200 fully paid to £2 and 100
paid to 305., and he could have applied for 50
carrying 7 p.c. Apart from the latter his present
10 p.c. on £550 is as good as p.c. would have
been on the old £2OO. Still it isn’t quite a 27 A
p.c. div. for all of them, because on some of the,
old shares a premium was charged by the
co. In any case the public now knows, or believes
it knows, the worst. There can’t be any more bonus
shares, and there can’t be any more dividends in
excess of 10 p.c., except on the condition mentioned
above; and on any further capital that may be got
into the business no more than 7 p.c. can be paid,
likewise except on the condition mentioned above.
And as the amount available after paying the present
divs. is not large, and as the tendency is to give
gas-workers a much bigger wage than t.hev have
had hitherto, it seems prettv certain that there won’t
be more than a 10 p.c. div. on one lot of shares
and 7 p.c. on the other for many a long day.

The table isn’t complete without some explanation.
The co. paid Philip Hart £11,700 (in shares) for
the business; but it also paid him £7OOO for the
lease. The lease may have been worth that
much; The Bulletin doesn’t know. Leases always
are doubtful items in a balance l sheet. They may
be assets or they may be liabilities. Many a man
has in good times taken premises and agreed to
pay more rent than he could ever hope to get if he
wanted to sub-let. However, this £7OOO is passed
as an asset, and only the £11,700 paid for goodwill
—a purely paper item —is shown as the co.’s deficit
to start with. Now other troubles arise. The
balance-sheet lumps goodwills and leases together,
and throws in “etc.” on general principles;
and this item lately jumped £1873; and that may
be more money paid for goodwill, or it may be
consideration for lease, or it may be “etc.” Any-
way, it has been reckoned as something intangible
and added to the deficit. So far that deficit hasn’t
been pruned down much; but that/is probably due
to the fact that, when the co. was formed, the ven-
dor, who seems to have been anxious to do the fair
thing all through, put up 20,300 shares to guarantee
an average 7 p.c. div. till the end of 1913, after
allowing £2OOO a year for depreciation. Recently
the co. purchased the business of Baumann’s, Ltd., for
£5500; but whether that is included in the present
accounts does not transpire. In addition to the
doubtful leasehold asset there is another: “Leasehold
improvements, £9528.” Apparently neither the
leasehold nor the improvements on the lease-
hold are being written down ; but, as shown
above, solid amounts are being written off
against general depreciation. Still this is
not a satisfactory plan: Profit and Loss ought to
be charged with an amount calculated to extinguish
the wasting asset within the life of the lease;' and
preferably this amortization fund should be accumu-
lated in deposits. Here, then, is the position. The
co. has a paid capital of £50,000 and £2012 of
undivided nrofit; and £30,645 of that is represented
■‘by leaseholds, leasehold improvements and goodwill,
less a proportion of the general depreciation fund of
£lO,BOO. The principal is not, therefore, well se-
cured by tangible assets. The balance of the capital
phis £9827 of liabilities is represented by cash
(£1129), stock, plant, etc., some of it of doubtful
value if it had to be sold. On the other hand, the
business has been fairly remunerative. In the first
half of last year profits were small; but there were
a good many non-recurring expenses. Last half they
were excellent. It’s a business in which a heap, of
money can be made when it is profitable; but it’s
not a business that you can make money in by
simply onening vour doors. In London Spiers and
Pond, Ltd., show scarcely any profit nowadays; and
the fact that Harts, Ltd., could buy out Baumann’s
for £5500 is significant.

The man who buys coal shares on the strength of the
information he gets from the published reports and
statements of account is a fool nine times out of ten.
A coal mine is a wasting asset; yet the official report
never by any chance mentions whether the mine is
one year or 20 years from its end. There presum-
ably are people who know, or have a very fair idea,
but if they are the people who write the official re-
ports they take all sorts of care that the information
isn’t furnished to the general body of shareholders
and the public. This Vale of Clywdd isn’t a worse
sinner in this respect than most. In fact, it isn’t
quite so bad a sinner as most, for its directors do
occasionally let in a little light on the dark places.
For instance, this time they say: “The co. is now
working on its leasehold property in good coal. We
are pleased to say that the good seam in which the
co. was working towards this leasehold is carried on,
with every prospect of its continuance.” The co.
belongs to the Lithgow Coal Association, which is
the selling agent for it and the other principal pro-
ducers of Western coal ; and it is only
since the association was formed and put an end to
the old throat-cubing tactics that the co. has done
any good. Latterly, Vale of Clywdd has been putting
a lot of money into brickworks also, and has thus
become an industrial as well as a coal-mining con-
cern. How the new enterprise will turn out it is
too early yet to say. Capital is now £60,000 —for
many years it was only £30,000 —and the surplus
of liquid assets amounts to very nearly £20,000, in
spite of the recent outlay of £12,000 or so on brick-
works.

“The co.’s mine, rolling stock, railway, machinery,
plant, etc., have been efficiently maintained, and
are in good working order.” the directors report. No
doubt. But what shareholders want to know much
more is how much longer it is estimated the mine
will last, on an output equal to the present, aw!
whether it has been well opened up to maintain
that output without extraordinary outlay in the
future. Something, it seems, is wrong. Only three
years ago these shares were worth £22; a little
before that they were £25; now they have gradu-
ally sagged to £l6, and the increased dividend put
scarcely any heart into the market. Coal stocks are
included in the Investment section of the market:
presumably they are reckoned to be a cut above
the speculative silver-lead-zinc and copper concerns.
Yet if the silver-lead-zinc concern didn’t give more
information about its ore reserves and its probable
life than the average coal co. does, it would be
crucified-—and would deserve to be. All that The
Bulletin knows about the Newcastle Wallsend is
of comparatively little consequence. It can tell
what profit is disclosed ; but a mine whose end was
booked for to-morrow could show just as much
profit to-day as one which had 50 years in front
of it. For the rest, this paper can merely offer
the following comparison:—

During the past 10 years the co. may have made
purchases which will extend its life for many
years. If it hasn’t done so it ought to have been
accumulating liquid assets. Only this much is
plain: profits during the past 10 years, after pro-
viding for dividends, have not been sufficient to
purchase whatever additions have been made to
property and plant. Nevertheless, the financial
position is very sound. There are 10,000 shares
paid to £lO, and the net liquid assets represent
about £7 4s. per share. And there is some coal-
mining plant (trucks, for instance) which in nor-
mal circumstances is nearly as good as gold.

for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a

consult and advise.*

were given last week. Principally because of the
Silverton tramway strike the input at the concen-
trating mill was nearly 30,000 tons 1e55—151,742
tons as against 180,080 tons. Assay values were,
however, more than maintained; the lead averaged
14.4 p.c. and the silver 6.50 z., both a shade better
than in the previous half. And the mill recoveries
were also better —78.3 p.c. of the lead and 56.2 p.c.
of the silver. The quantity of ore developed in the
mine was estimated six months ago at 3.250,000
tons. Since then a good deal of prospecting has
been done at 1070 ft., and a bore put out west of the
footwall ore channel went for 47ft. through sul-
phides worth 17 p.c. lead, 6oz. silver and 131 p.c.
zinc, and for another 14 ft. there was ore averaging
20 p.c. lead and 25 p.c. zinc. The reserves of pay-
able ore can therefore be safely said to be larger
now than they were six months ago. As to the value
of this ore, everything depends, of course, upon the
erratic metal markets. But this is what has hap-
pened in the past 64 years:—
Ore Treated. Divs. paid.

June, 1908 . . . . 78,003 „ 20,000

June, 1909 . . . . 137,839 „ 40,000

June, 1910 .. .. 132,968 „ 20,000

Those dividends have been derived partly from accu-
mulated zinc tailings. During the past six months
the De Bavav Co. took 20,809 tons of these old by-
products and the Zinc Corporation 39,270 tons;
total, 60,070 tons. There are 996,163 tons left, or
more than eight years’ supply at the recent rate of
shipment. On top of that there are 329,211 tons of
slimes, rich in lead and zinc; and they are going
to be worth a lot of money presently. Current produc-
tion will therefore be assisted by the old by-products
for many years to come- —for more than 10 years,
anyhow, assuming that they are drawn on at the re-
cent rate. If, then, the average of the past 64 years
is maintained, during the next 10 years 2,880,000
tons of ore will he treated, and (with the help of
the aforementioned by-products) dividends of
£1,430,000 will be paid. There are 200,000 shares
in the co., and that means £7 3s. per share. After
10 years there ought still to be left a considerable
tonnage of ore. Also, there are net liquid assets
(exclusive of stores) equal to £135,000 on hand,
and that means, another 13s. 6d. per share. Most of
this figuring is built on the assumption that prices,
costs, extractions and values will average in the next
10 years what they have averaged in the past 61
years ; or, if there is a bettering of one, that there will
be a kick of equal force coming in the opposite direc-
tion. One can never tell, of course ; but 64 years
ought to be a fair period on which to strike an
average in the metal markets.

Down to the beginning of 1912 the capital had of
late years been £175,000 in £1 shares. Then
£25;000 more was issued; and during the past year
those- 2(10,000 shares have been exchanged for
600,000," Still of £1 each, on the pretext that the
old shares had become unwieldy in the market. The
juggle w’abnjt of much practical consequence. It
doesn’t matter much whether the alleged capital is
£200,000 or- £§00,000; for the alleged capital of a
mining co. rareTy bears any close relation to the
market value. ’lncidentally the market value doesn’t
always hear much 1 relation to the real value.
This concern bad a strange, eventful history.
When Zeb Lane „ was the young Apollo among mine
managers, the North? of which he was in charge, was
thought to be likelytfcio'king. But the good surface
stuff was soon lost, > and then gloom settled on the
property. It was eventually turned down as a low-
grade sulphide proposition, Hal Shepperd, still on
the Board, deserves a l of of the credit of resurrect-
ing it. He and a few others saw that sulphides were
coming, and they picked the derelict up for a trifle.
The derelict has paid £3B5f(TOO in divs. during the
past two years. In the six. months just gone by
153,680 tons of ore were-miohfl and milled. Allow-
ing £12,018 off for depreciation (a word will be
said about this presently), . tljq net profit was
£130,000. That means so near to 17s. a ton that
the difference doesn’t matter. Above the 1250 ft.
level it was reckoned six months - ago that there
were 2,000,000 tons. The next level will be at
1400 ft., and nothing has been met in the develop-
ment work to suggest that the ore won’t be as good
there. Two million tons at 17s. (which includes the
payment for the zjnc product) is £1,700,000, or
£2 16s. Bd. per share—on last half-year s results.
Also, the surplus of liquid assets over liabilities is
£190,195, and that’s another 6s. 4d. This paper
will not, however; guarantee that last half-year s
results will continue. The ore is there,, and the ex-
traction of metal will most likely go on improving ;
but there is no telling what the metal markets will
do. Shear £1 a ton off lead, and profits come back
about £20,000 for a half‘year. In the first half of
1909 gross profits got as low as £27,305. The out-
put was then 66,797 tons, so the gross profit at that
time was only a shade over Bs. per ton, though the
Amalgamated Zinc Co. was then, as now, taking the
tailings. And 2,000,000 .tons at Bs. is £BOO,OOO,
or £1 6s. Bd. per share, instead of the previously
mentioned £2 16s. Bd. The share market takes a
view with rather more common-sense in it than usual
where a mine is concerned, and drops itself down
comfortably between the two.
Something was said up above about depreciation,
ine last old co. used to write something off for
wear and tear every term. The new co. hasn’t writ-
ten anything off plant for the past term, but has
dropped £30,000 into a reserve for depreciation
and general purposes. This appears among the re-
serves, and consequently among the profits; so the
true profits are less than the table shows. The
£3265 mentioned as written off is mostly the cost
of the reconstruction, with stamp duty.

Portland Place Mansions, Ltd., is a
scheme for providing St. Kilda (Vic.) with further
‘flat” habitations. The promoter has an option over
a triangular three-acre block well served by tram-
lines, and within a stone's-throw of St. Kilda Esplan-
ade. The capital is £50,000 in £lO shares. The
promoter takes 500 fully paid, 3200 are put on the
market, and 1300 held in reserve. It is proposed to
build shops and a motor garage, and to start a series
of five mansions by converting one of the two private
dwelling-places now on the property into a flattery
containing 90 rooms. If this goes successfully, an-
other will follow, and so on. There is no visible
reason why the flats should not be rushed, and the
scheme looks calculated to supply a want. Half of
the Portland Place estate is the property of Harvey
Patterson, now gone to Maoriland; the other half
belongs to F. P. Beyer, whose house will be the one
to be first dealt with if the shares are taken
up. The estimate of £375 for preliminary expenses
is not extravagant, though £5OOO in shares is a stiff
price to pay for a mere right to purchase at a
present-day valuation.
Wunderlich’s are issuing £40.000 in 7 p.c.
second pref. shares, with no right to more than 7
p.c. As the existing pref. capital is only £40,000,
the second prefs. will be amply protected ; and the
return (cumulative) is choice. Present shareholders
have first call on the new issue.
The first annual report of the Carpathia
is an unsatisfactory document. Those who expected
that at last they would get an official statement
about the mine, the value of the ore, the quantities
taken out and their metal contents, are disappointed.
There is no mine report at all and no summary of
shipments, and the directors say nothing except that
the weekly reports have kept shareholders “fully ad-
vised”—which is nonsense. The weekly reports have
been utterly unsatisfactory, consisting mostly of
vague, indefinite remarks. Profit and loss account
shows a credit of £20,108. Two Is. dividends have
been paid absorbing £10,407 ; and after providing
for outstanding debts there was £lB7B in cash left.
Other more or less liquid assets on July 31 were —
Sundry debtors, £3185; tin ore and stores, £1456.
The financial position is therefore fair, though there
is a poor little lot of cash in hand considering the
vigor with which the rich ore has been rooted out
since operations started. Probably to atone for the
absence of any remarks by the manager, the direc-
tors promise an early report from Dr. Logan Jack.
The drive of 40ft. in the Carpathia is said
to bulk 4 p.c., and another drive of 30ft. gives equal
values. Stated that the grade of ore sent to Bar-
medman is to be reduced to 4 p.c., which is fair
enough, despite the number of handlings. It was
certain that the old rich grade could not be main-
tained, except for small and infrequent parcels, but
tonnage may make up for the lower values.
The prospector’s yarn; “The Bulletin
said the Glamorgan, away out from Grafton
(N.S.W.), hadn’t got below water level. I’ll admit
we haven’t got a deep shaft. But it’s not because
we were fifraid. I’ve been on it 12 years, sticking to
it, nursing it, mostly making a living out of it. But
it was slow work. No money for machinery—just
plugging away, me and a man generally; and when
you’ve got down 90 feet with hand power, it’s
enough. But there’s ore there; think I would have
hung on for 12 years if there wasn’t? Forty tons
I sent away in one parcel—and that’s a fair test.
Black ore, carbonates; in the bottom, sulphides.
When we got down as far as we could in one shaft
we just sank another. The place is full of them.
Now we want a reverb. It costs too much to send
the mullock away with the ore. And why should
we ? There’s plenty wood and water handy ; and if
we make matte we’ll make money. You haven’t got
a chance when you have to sell your crude ore. I’ve
gone as far as I can single-handed ; and that’s whv
the little Glamorgan syndicate went on the market.
Then along comes The Bulletin and says—‘What’s
underneath?’ If I’d had the money to find out, I
wouldn’t be asking for it.” Rees Thomas is the
prospector who puts it that way. And when a man
has stuck to a show for 12 years, and plugged
down half a dozen shafts nearly 100 ft. almost single-
handed, The Bulletin reckons he’s about due to
have his say.

“lodide” : Why don’t the directors of the
lodide (N.S.W.) do something definite bv winzing
from the two occurrences of shinning ore at the 90ft.
level? Here is an ore channel 220 ft. wide by 1000 ft.
long, and the ore happens in bunches, and in that
width of channel bunches are easily missed. Rich
ore is underfoot in the two shipping stopes at the
90ft., and horse sense would dictate that the best
prospecting is by following the ore till it cuts out.

Dividends; Ivanhoe, £35,000; Kalgurli, £24,000,
