Golden Butterfly (W.A.) probably re-
alises by this time that it takes money to open up and
equip a mine. Already a few thousands have been
wrung from shareho.ders, and now they are being
stirred up for a further £l5OO in order to clear
off existing debts for plant. A small profit was
earned from the treatment of ore worth a shade
over 20s. in July, but as some better stuff is now
available for stoping the co. expects to do better
this month. The mine may turn out fairly well, but
not enough is yet known of it in depth to warrant
the shares being regarded as anything but a gamble.
Associated Northern (W.A.) is having
further trouble, this time with the treatment of the
Ora Banda sulphides. To overcome this the co. is
sending ore from the mine to its Kalgoorlie mill for
treatment. Cost of transit is given as 15s. per ton ;
but as the stuff has to be carted over 20 miles, railed
another 30, and then carted from the station to the
plant, this estimate is probably under the mark. The
installation of additions to the plant at Ora Banda
is now under consideration.
Yuanmi (W.A.) has got its sulphide mill
going at last. The plant is expected to handle 100
tons a day, but will probably do more when in full
swing. There is plenty of ore in sight to go on
with ; but, although the shoots are likely to be per-
sistent, they show a strong inclination to shorten as
they go down. Still, if the worst happens, the co. can
turn to its other property at Sandstone for help. At
one time the reef in this show looked like flattening
to nothing, but a steepening took place below No. 4
level, and where steepening occurs'in this way there’s
generally hope for permanency. Then there is the
adjoining Sandstone Development leases, which the
co. bought for a few thousands, and which are since
said to be showing 2oz. prospects,
Ingliston Consols (W.A.), which owns a
prize packet next to the Fenian at Meekatharra,
treated 17,981 tons during the year ended June 30
and got £40,933 for its pains. Costs averaged £l.
After spending several thousands in additions and im-
provements to plant, and laying out a fair sum in
development, the owners—a syndicate of five or six—-
divided £11,200. The manager says the mine looks
good, and that the ore in sight is sufficient to main-
tain the present output for three years. If only the
owners of this and the adjoining Fenian could come
to terms and invite one or two of the cos. working
adjacent shows to join in, what a lovely float the
group would make! With anything like manage-
ment, everybody interested could not fail to make
much more money than is otherwise likely. Unfor-
tunately jealousy between the parties has prevented
an amalgamation in the past, and is more than likely
to do so in the future.
Sweet William Consolidated, at Payne’s
Find (W.A.), is seeking exemption. The co. finds it
a waste of time and money to bog into hard ground
with hand boring, and proposes to spend £2500 on
machine drills. Six months’ respite from labor con-
ditions is asked to enable a suitable plant to be
selected and erected. Before this expenditure is in-
curred, some reliable opinion as regards the possi-
bilities of the field at depth should be got. All the
data The Bulletin has been able to obtain goes to
show that geological indications do not augur well
for permanency.
Mararoa (W.A.) hones to prolong its pro-
fitable existence by tacking three adjoining leases on
to its holding. The idea is to prospect a parallel
reef worked years ago by the defunct Norseman Gold
Mines, Ltd. It is alleged that plenty of 355. to
455. ore was left in the days when only ounces paid.
Volatilising experiments are still in pro-
gress at Gwalia Consolidated (W.A.). In a circular
dated July 24 the Board informs shareholders that,
although promising, results so far are insufficiently
conclusive for the purpose of plant design. The
general managers state that before demonstration can
be considered complete, the present plant ought to
run a bit longer. To do this, a few thousands are
wanted, hence the further call of 2d. per share.
* * * *
The old trouble about the timbering of
Mount Morgan is on deck again. Brigalow is plenti-
ful in the district; and A. A. Boyd, the new under-
ground man —as good a miner as there is in Australia
—made tests and recommended the re-employment
of this timber in place of the much more expensive
ironbark and spotted gum. But. the local mining
inspector condemned the brigalow and his chief sup-
ported him. General-manager Magnus then went
into the business again, and could see no reason why
brigalow should not be used. So he started a fight;
and the warden was called on to hear arguments.
James Hebbard, the recognised authority on mine-
timbering at Broken Hill, left that place for North
Queensland a few days ago, and it is safe to say he
is going to give evidence. David Milne, senior in-
spector in N.S.W., has also been mentioned as a
witness. If Boyd, Hebbard and Milne agree that a
timbering system is safe, this paper won’t feel dis-
posed to contradict them.
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, has
been doing well. The directors were pretty safe in
paying a sixpenny ( £6250) last .Tune, for there is
a substantial credit balance already. The profit of
£1779 for July, with the expectation of something
good for August, makes another dividend this year
probable. Mills’ United crushed 519 tons for £592,
which isn’t payable. So stoping has been stopped.
Nevertheless shares have risen on a development in
the New Brilliant Freehold. A winze from the Free-
hold’s No. 5 level has followed, what is practically
a new reef for 115 ft. It averages about 3ft. thick,
and gives payable prospects. How far it will extend
to the westward, in the direction of Mills’ United,
has yet to be determined. A crosscut is being driven
from No. 6 level, but owing to the unusually fiat
underlay of the reef all the crosscuts at lower levels
will have to be driven long distances. The Free-
hold’s No. 1 level coincides with Mills’ No. 7, No. 4
with No. 10, No. 5 with No. 11, No. 6 with No. 12.
Mills’ United has three levels opened near the boun-
dary below the Freehold’s No. 5, so if this new make
continues to live down, Mills United will have a
chance of getting it by crosscutting. _ Should the
crosscut from the Freehold’s No. 6 strike the reef,
there will be a flutter. The crosscut was in hard
granite at 72ft.
A more or less complete sweep has been
made of the crowd that used to rule the Great
Southern Consols, Rutherglen (Vic.). No one seems
to know why this show should pay ; nevertheless some
outside enthusiasts have gobbled up over 20,000
shares in a lazy market, and have taken control. The
Consols has been one of the dreary wastes at Ruther-
glen, and it will take a lot of whipping to infuse life
into the moribund sluggard.
Broken Hill miners are having the time of
their lives. The B. H. South’s report shows that
for the past half-year that mine’s men on develop-
ment work averaged 19s. 9fd. per shift, and those
on stoping 16s. lfd. Truckers —youths mostly—
averaged 13s. 4jd.
* * * *
Melbourne motor-’bus investors note that
the suburban municipal powers are already chipping
into the business. Brunswick, Essendon, Fitzroy and
Northcote councils have decided to collaborate in the
running of two lines of ’buses to begin with. Pretty
soon, perhaps, the idea will dawn upon the councils
everywhere that they may as well establish taxi-cab
services, and undercut the extortionate private enter-
prisers. Where the motor-’bus prospers, the reason-
able taxi-cab is wanted, as a rule.
Westralian Mines Dept, is at its wits
end to know what to do about Phillips River. A
State railway of 30-odd miles connects it with the
coast, and large sums have been spent in water con-
servation and in other ways ; yet, although the aver-
age metal contents of the lodes are a good deal higher
than those of a lot of other going concerns in the
Commonwealth, the place is badly punctured. A
foreign eo. promoted by the late Charles Kaufman
and others sank over £700,000 in development,
equipment, smelting plant, etc., but indifferent man-
agement and other misfortunes ultimately landed the
co. on the rocks, and it became a total wreck. Now
the field is being almost wholly worked by small
parties which eke out an existence by sending parcels
of picked ore to eastern smelters for treatment. They
complain, however, that it takes from 8 to 10 p.c.,
with gold contents thrown in, to make a profit under
these conditions, and are urging the Government to
take over the local smelters from the defunct foreign
co.’s successors, and lend them a hand. Unfortunately
a lot of the ore is expensive to treat; and, knowing
this and other difficulties that have to be contended
with, the Department realises fliat local smelting is
not likely to reduce the cost of treatment a great
deal. It has, however, sent an expert along to look
into the matter.
Blythe River Iron Mines, Ltd., is the
most reposeful co. in Australia. It is due for its
26th ordinary meeting, and it hasn’t done a thing
yet. It owns a big deposit of very fine ore in Tas-
mania, and it has just got new leases of 21 years
for the best of the property; but it hasn’t any
money to do anything itself, and it can’t find any-
body who wants an iron mine. So it just hangs
on to its thread of hope and watches its funds
dwindle. There is £2183 still left—which, with
the interest, will be enough to last for another 10
or 12 years.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 1, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The ballroom at State Government House
looked as if it had all its electric wires in
curling pins the other day. A new scheme
for lighting this apartment is to blaze on
the coiffure of Airs. John Bull before this
reaches you. The Stricklands’ first ball in
the official chalet is the cause of the recent
disturbance in the pale duck-egg green walls.
The Kindergarten Union sent forth its
students and schoolmarms to sell wattle in
the streets this first day of September—Alon-
day. General-secretary Ellen Desailly spent
a breathless morning last week in search of
some authority on the etiquette of hawkers'
licenses. In a secret stronghold of Aluni-
cipal Law she found a hole in the Act which
permits the occasional amateur to flourish
flowers for sale without a special permit.
So she promptly drove her chariot through
that hole. The Kindergarten damsels are
out on business. Proceeds of their sale of
the yellow blooms will go towards the deposit
money on the purchase of a college for
training students. Hilton, in Liverpool-
street, is likely to be the place. This is an
old-time mansion. It dates from the days
when Darlinghurst was a countrified suburb,
and the inhabitants thereabouts walked
warily o’ nights by the light of a horned
lantern.
The premises are to be carefully searched
for ghosts before any business is done. All
very ancient Sydney houses should be
searched for ghosts before being bought or
leased. Sydney is not so full of spectres as
it was, for many of the old residences
have been pulled down, or else turned into
boarding establishments. But a friend of
mine, who has a splendid collection of dried
spooks, assures me that, at one time, the
place was full of dead people walking. There
was an aged mansion somewhere about
Glenmore-road, in which the vision of a
former proprietor fell downstairs every night
with a howl and broke his visionary neck.
He had died that way many years before,
and it became a habit. Then there was an
old house in North Sydney, where the lady
of the premises couldn’t give an afternoon
tea because three very old female appari-
tions, in early Victorian garniture, used to
attend, and, though they neither ate nor
drank, they cast a gloom over the proceed-
ings. And a bygone ferry boat was
haunted by a non-paying passenger,
who had died on board through heart
failure while reading a Family Herald.
It was impossible to tell that she
ivasn’t real, unless you noticed the date on
the Family Herald, or unless you sat on
her and found that she wasn’t there. (I
believe, however, that a Sydney pressman
invented this ferry-boat ghost.)
Ghosts, as already hinted, are going
out of fashion. Still, it is always
advisable to have an old house care-
fully inspected before you buv it. The
best method is to take a dog "through it.
Dogs, so it is said, can see apparitions which
human eyes do not perceive. Cats are of
no value. When a cat sees a ghost he tries
to scratch it.
t September 1 is too late for Wattle Day in
N.S.W. Alost of the lovely yellow stuff has
run to stalk and leaf at this time o’ spring.
The wattle maidens who patrolled the city
were fortified with open deal boxes slung on
their shoulders and indexed with a tag of
yellow satin ribbon. But their blooms were
rubbing off in fine fluff like egg powder, and
the pavements were strewn with stalks.
Airs. Francis Anderson has mothered the
Free Kindergarten movement in this town
since its first struggles in a room or two in
Woolloomooloo. At a recent meeting, when
the general secretary produced the annual
report and other heavy-weight matter in the
presence of the Strickland ladyship, Mrs.
Anderson made a few pointed remarks re
diamonds. Going to and fro amongst the
eight free kindergartens which have grown
up in our least alluring back lanes, the lady
pondered on the number of young children
who sit on the edge of the pavement with
their feet in the gutter and their eyes
fixed on Melchizedek’s pawnshop across the
way, because the Kindergarten funds are
shouting for help. She also noted that
amongst her acquaintances were many
dames who could part with a few carats
of diamonds without missing them—for the
good of the kiddies. A hurried desire to
cover up anything in the shape of gems
was noted amongst those present. An un-
reliable rumor tore round the premises to
the effect that one bedizened matron
dragged off some of her gear and buried it
in the depths of a handbag to save if from
the penetrating glance of the lady Savon-
arola.
St. Vincent’s Ball careered up and down
the Town Hall floor soon after last week’s
despatches had to go to press too early to
deal with it, in consequence whereof it was
left out. The fancy sets were put to the
vote. Ballot papers gave Mrs. George Sly
first prize for a white and black team. This
was the most effective and uniform group
in the ballroom, as a sartorial affair.
The best attempt to be exactly like its
name was achieved by Mrs. Neil Macdon-
ald’s Press team. This got away with sec-
ond prize. It deserved a double demy encore
and a foolscap or super-royal cheer for its
stiff paper skirts, which exactly represented
the outer sheets that are covered with ink
in every newspaper office in this town.
Airs. Aiacdonald might have simplified mat-
ters by calling her team “Civilisation.” The
press stands for civilisation, despite its lost-
dog advertisements and its occasional sordid
notice to the effect that Jane .Tones, aged
25. dark-haired and domesticated, wants to
meet a middle-aged Calithumpian gentleman
with a view to the above.
Mrs. Ronald Forbes’s Watteau set had an
indeterminate expression about its white
wigs, and was rather lean on its male legs,
but it was handsomely garbed, and contrived
to win third prize. “Autumn” waltzed
round in russet garb. Airs. Jim Hughes
was responsible for this good-looking team.
Mrs. O’Gorman Hughes’s set was frocked in
black velvet, and hung about the neck with
white . fichus. This team, in its feminine
half, illustrated the fact that the fichu, as
an article of apparel, is as dressy as a pillow
case, if you put the latter round your chest
instead of wearing it under your sleepy
head. Airs. Leonard Dodds marched in
front of a heavily panniered Lady Teazle set
of bewigged maids and matrons. Dr. Odillo
Maher gave the glad hand to Governor
Strickland, who was shadowed bv Colonel
Campbell and A.D.C. Forbes. The vice-
regal dais was filled with official and unoffi-
cial personages, who watched the joyous
capers of the Turkey Trot, Bunny Hug and
mere Lancers from an eminence of one foot
above floor level. The John Toohey dame
towered over smaller committee women.
Mrs. W. F. Brennan, one of the early orig-
inals on the committees of St. Vincent’s Hos-
pital balls, beamed on the successful event
over the rim of the gallery rail. The cheque
ought to be large and very wide about the
waist.
The Orsova officers gave a meal, followed
by a dance, the other night. This revel
began with the usual victim, the defence-
less oyster in the shell, and ended with a
Turkey trot or something of the kind at 1
a.m. About 70 friends of skipper Tim
Healey and his mates were bidden to the
fray.
The British Parliamentarians are striking
this shore with a loud thud as I write. They
arrive in batches, and will be seized and fed
by municipal magnates before they have
time to find their shore legs.
The “What-to-do-with-our-Girls” Exhibi-
tion will have unpacked its industries in
the Town Hall on September 17. The open-
ing will be attended to by some local
mikado, whose name and address is still inde-
cisive. It may be that he will be called
Smith, for most people are Smith more or
less. Organising-secretary Grace Burrows
is going to boom the domestic science sec-
tion. which will revolve round the tireless
cooker, etc., in the basement. A model bun-
galow. built of fibro-cement, to ease the
minds of the fire insurance tetrarchs. will
contain everything that the ordinary woman
requires to. keen her out of mischief. Sev-
eral varieties of stoves, a bathroom and a
scullery will comnlete her happiness. Din-
ner parties for eight guests will be served
up each evening in the fibro-cement dining-
room. Each course is to be cooked with a
different kind of stove. You may
have a Primus scalloped oyster, a
kerosene blue-flame whiting, a gas-
baked hunk of mutton, an entree
browned in a tireless cooker, and an electri-
fied or electrocuted pancake, which is much
the same as a muffin struck by lightning.
The hostesses who take the risks of giving
their guests an exhibition meal on consecu-
tive evenings are Airs. W. A. Holman. Airs.
AlacCallum. Airs. Chief Justice Cullen, the
Calico Knight’s bright-eyed lady, Airs. Tom
Hughes. Airs. Damd. Mrs. Greig-Smith, and
Airs. .Tim Alacarthur Onslow.
A few excerpts from a delightful inter-
view in the Datly Telegraph : “When seen
at her residence. Glenyarrah, Double Bav.
she said: ‘You find me in the midst of tak-
ing out my Venetian glassware for a dinner
party’ ”. .. . (Here follows a peroration on
the decadence of the modern domestic, as
noted in her hankering for roller rinks or
picture shows.) “ ‘That table you see there,
belonged to Alarie Antoinette, and is a very
valuable specimen, but it hss been rent in
several places by careless dusting.’”... .At
this crisis the lady interviewer ought to
have fallen flat on her face. After several
salaams she might have inquired whether
the offending domestics had dusted Alarie
Antoinette’s furniture with a garden roller
or a cross-cut saw? An edifying discourse
in the Annie-8 wan-over-the-teacups manner
follows, re midWictorian women, “who grew
old sweetly in the homes they had made into
shrines for their husbands’ and children’s
hearts.” The interviewer meekly asks, “And
how do you account for this unrest?”
“Cheap amusements,” says Airs. Minerva.
whose smile used to dazzle us behind the
footlights. An informative wedge of criti-
cism on the effects of woman’s suffrage, a
hurried glance at her own stage career, a
whiff of regret for the passing of “class dis-
tinctions,” and we come to a young sister,
of whom it is reported: “Forsaking a
luxurious home, she adopted the strenuous
life of a hospital nurse,” etc. After throw-
ing a solid slice of information at the abashed
interviewer, we are reminded that “Men are
not monsters.... Cleopatra did not demand —
she enticed.”
Chief Magistrate Elliot and his wife and
youngsters have left Norfolk Island for
these parts. There is no special term of
office for this thorny billet. The commis-
sion is worded “at the Governor’s pleasure,”
or something to that effect, and when the
tenant incurs the Governor’s displeasure he
goes. In Governor Chelmsford’s time the
lease was renewed with a large red seal and
much official language at the end of each
year, but this was merely an extra half-yard
of red tape. Captain Elliot is a tough and
wary old naval officer. He leaves the island
because he wants to, having beaten the brief
records of his predecessors by several years.
Nothing but an archangel could combine the
duties of prosecutor, judge, jury, official
gaoler, friend and chief magistrate, and re-
main a popular personage on Norfolk Island.
The Elliots weathered many squalls in the
local gossip-pot with credit to themselves.
Mrs. Chief Magistrate is a kindly, motherly
soul, but I fancy she’ll be glad to take a
rest from the task of trying to be a whole
choir of angels in one woman, which has
been her singing part for six years or so.
A recent bridegroom, Cecil Egan Murnin,
who married Miss Ruth Blair, of Mosman,
the other week, is a son of the late G. F.
Murnin. A patriarch looks over the rim
of liis spectacles to remark that Murnin
senior was one of Sydney’s foremost mer-
chants in the years that are dust. lie was
'or a while manager of the Australian
Widows’ Fund, and was an underwriter and
shipowner in his busy moments. As one of
the founders and first directors of the
A. ALP. (1848), the late G. F. Murnin left
his footprints on this city’s commercial
macadam.
Solicitor Charles Tange’s picturesque
country home, Ivoree, was burnt down a
week or two ago. It used to stand in the
midst of the fruits of the earth, mostly
oranges, in the Hawkesbury River district.
Ivoree was not insured at the time of the dis-
aster. The family had moved about 18 miles
nearer to our buzzing hamlet, and was living
at Point Clare when the greedy flames de-
voured everything of Ivoree except some
laundry bric-h-brac. Most of the household
gods had been transplanted to Point Clare
before this whack of bad luck hit one of
the most popular amateur orehardists in
those parts. The local inhabitants turned
out to a dog to fight the fire fiend, and the
last surviving wombat tried to put out the
blaze by spanking it with his tail. Nothing
but a mangle or a tub or two remain to
tell the tale. It is rumored that a leading
rat was being given a dinner in the barn
by his supporters and admirers, and that the
dessert, which was a box of wax matches,
blew up.
Didn’t Miss Dorothy Dibbs marry, a
while ago, a brother or something of the
George Ritchie, of the Union Co., who
plighted his troth to Miss Greta Mills the
other afternoon? The Ritchie family is
well and favorably known amongst the "hill-
tops of Dunedin (Maoriland), where the
cab-horse is shod with patent brakes, so that
he won’t fall off the gable or eaves or roof
of the scenery. (If he does fall off, and
comes clawing backwards down the wall of
the scenery, with his cab behind him, and
screaming for assistance as he goes—well,
in that case lie naturally falls into Princes-
street. and a great scatter ensues, and there
is much wild Scotch language.) To resume
after this long parenthesis, the George
Ritchies are honeymooning in Victoria. The
trim, pointed beard and breezy anecdotes of
the. bride’s male parent. David Mills (of the
Union Line of Red Funnels) used to be
minor landmarks in Melbourne. At present
they flourish in this city.
Mosman’s Town Hall surged with enthu-
siasm the other evening when clever
Dorothy Hopkins stood behind her fiddle.
Mosman’s best-known residents filled the
local municipal barn. These premises, by
the way, are too small for the musical emo-
tions of that leafy suburb. The inhabitants,
in consequence, are often driven to seek
shelter in a new hall with a more generous
girth, in Neutral Bay. Dorothy “Hop”
(her proud parent “Hop” Avas in the audi-
ence) plays with temnerament and consider-
able technique. She is a self-possessed
young damsel—an important item in hand-
ling an instrument so likely to get stage
fright as a fiddle —and her bow arm is as
firm and steady as the limb of a seasoned
professional. Musician Arthur Massey was
accompanist. The girl’s best efforts were
Schubert’s “Die Nebensonnen” and Van
Goen’s “Schertzo.” The youthful Marcus
Burkitt. who hovers in the neighborhood of
nine springs, was loaded to his collar stud
with chocolate boxes for his performance at
the piano. W. H. Parkinson uplifted a sil-
very tenor to open the programme. L a n>,
on Dorothy Hop,’ on the fiddle
musician Massey, at the keyboard, aecom
pamed Mr. Parkinson’s upward fliX" •
"Angels Guard Thee.” The flight, however 11
was nearly a funeral, owing to the snail-,:
pace at which the time was dragged 7, <■
Mendelssohn’s massive concerto in E jlin L
was Miss “Hop’s” heavyweight contribution
to the programme. Miss Phyllis Smith
robed in periwinkle blue, and Chas IW
ham were included in the entertainment'
Leila Waddell. Stael and Cyril Monk have
been, in turn, responsible for Miss Hon
kins’s sure technique. Alfred Norman, who
conducts the flourishing Mosman Musical
'Association, and various members of the
same fraternity, were present.
Conductor Alfred Hill is galvanising the
Amateur Orchestral Society into a new lease
of life. Mendelssohn's Italian symphony
was the strong meat of the Society’s concert
the other night. The youthful M.L. pianist
Frank Hutchens, successfully circumvented
all the fiery complications of a Liszt Rhap-
sody with both hands and a rapid pedal at-
tack by both feet. Fiddler W. J. Coad and
Singer Greder W'ere the other soloists.
Nordica’s geranium-red gown, which she
wore at Saturday evening’s concert, is one
of the star items in her vast repertoire of
clothes. It has been built by a genius who
know’s what to do with the fish-tail train.
This appendix is generally like a dirty little
duster at the end of a dance. Being too
flimsy a rag to have a stout lining to cling
to. it wipes up the floor to its own total
destruction. It is all right for the party
from whom it was copied, for the fish keeps
his extremity clean in the water, but a fish-
tail on land is a different thing. The Xor-
dica specimen, however, had a firm steering-
gear, in the form of a weighty knob of itself,
like a silk tassel. This kept the caudal
finislnnept in order, and guided it in a digni-
fied waggle over the white calico that pro-
tects the Amurkan lady’s skirts from a stray
speck of dust on the platform. The ground-
plan of this geranium vision was toned up
by a black tassel or two. A film of black
fell down one side. Any vacant space on
the corsage was sprinkled with diamonds.
Some slender cords of the same stones sup-
ported the base of a leaning tower of black
osprey mn the singer’s chestnut-brown locks.
This red gown is worth several determined
encores.
Tenor Haul Dufault threatens to
eclipse his 1912 record in Maoriland. The
French-Canadian is putting on weight in bis
velvety middle register. If he could be per-
suaded to climb a note or two higher (Jean
de Rescke would help him without a step-
ladder), he would give the public the benefit
of a top C he is still concealing about his
person. A Wagner evening and a Ballad
afternoon wind u r > the present Nordica co.’s
appearances in this town. The musical four
change their spots—unlike the leopard—on
September 3. That is to say. they carry
their vaccination marks to Auckland.
The cocked hat that is like an Admiral’s
best top gear, if he jumped on its sharp
corners and made it a tricorn, is still
perched on some of our best-dressed coiffures.
Grace I’alotta tossed one of these tricky-
looking episodes on her head the other morn-
ing. It went down the street on top of
Grace and in company with one of the
smartest tailor-built costumes (with a
patent leather waist-belt about half an inch
wide) the between season can supply —for a
handsome consideration.
The Royal Art Society’s rooms throbbed
with the first warm day of the season on
Saturday. Private view- day dragged a herd
of invited guests up to the top floor of
Vickery's Chambers, where they simmered
gentlv together in the presence of Art. Dr.
MacKellar came along after the great cata-
logue chase had fainted quietly away on the
doorstep. The printers are alleged to have
been responsible for the fact that a round
dozen or so of the visitors were unable to
buy catalogues at any price. The companv
in consequence stamped round and butted
into anv likely-looking pictures in search of
a signature.
Lister-Lister needs no label to his creamy
sands and colonies of seagulls. Dattilo
Rubbo’s old men can be diagnosed without
aid. Norman Carter’s mother, a plump,
brisk-looking old lady, is painted by her
artist son on a big canvas, not far from a
striking portrait of the veteran Mrs. George
Rankin, by Watkins. The thin, intellectual
face of this well-known lover of Art was
absent from the show in the flesh. For once
Mrs. Rankin has been forced to remember
her 70-odd summers. She is giving way to
some passing ailment in a sick room. Judge
Ileydon took wary snacks of the show behind
his snecs. Mrs. Phillips Fox might have
compared some heavy consignments of R. A.
Societv’s color with the lighter moods of
French artists. G. W. Hadfield, in the
intervals of wearing a small sailor hat. the
property of a joyous grandson (and placed
on his brow by that masterful imp), con-
trived to run a catalogue to earth behind
some secret fastness in the artists’ eyrie. If
was torn from him, however, by the most
able-bodied in his vicinity.
Yours affectionately.
VANDOBIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 1, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Agricultural Show is to bring the
ebuntry crowd to town nearly a month
later than the period when the railway
smash happened last year. The weather
will be warmer and the days an hour
longer. Last year 170,000 people “did” the
Show, and it is expected that the setting
back of the dates to September 22-27, and
a more elaborate programme of ring per-
formances, will bring the attendance up to
200,000. The Thursday of Show week has
always been a public holiday, which the
shops mostly didn’t observe. This year a
number of emporium bosses are promising
to turn their employees loose for the first
time.
The possibility of Lord Denman riding
his own geegee to victory or otherwise in a
Steeplechase at Caulfield, this Saturday, is
whispered of. The Denman ambition just
now is to achieve immortal fame as the first
vice-regal personage to play the gentleman
jock character, and win Australia’s heart.
His geegee is named Topmast, or Mizzen
Mast, or Jib Boom, or something, and the
steeplechase it is entered for is reserved for
amateur riders. Seeing that Topmast is a
good jumper, and only a few horses will be
out for the event, sports reckon that his Ex.
will not be taking a foolish risk if he
bestrides the animal on Saturday. He has
“schooled” it over a few jumps on the Caul-
field course, and ridden it at work, just like
one of the professional boys. Anyhow,
he intends to have a go with his
trusty steed. If he doesn’t do his own joc-
keying, the crack amateur horseman, Mr.
Highett, will have the mount. Mr. Highett
rode a horse in the Grand National Hurdle
Race at Flemington a while ago.
,G.-G. Denman, though bereft of his lady,
managed to engineer a children’s party for
young Judith’s sixth birthday. The Bartte-
lot dame, Mrs. Guise and Mrs. Clarke were
the only grown-ups allowed in to help the
helpless Helps. Small Judith wore the usual
party garb of white with a big blue sash.
Tea, buns and dancing were the simple order
of the revel, with a conjurer to top off with.
The magician produced a live canary from
somewhere and put it in a gilded cage. This
fowl of mystery and its prison constituted
Nevill’s present to the lady of the party.
The crowd of British legislators who are
to visit this city anon will not include the
only member of the House of Commons in
whom we are interested. George Doughty,
husband of Eugenia Stone (The Bulletin’s
Melbourne chatterer of yore) has no place
in the pleasure van. George is a more active
man of affairs than most of the approaching
picnickers, and we wanted to see him,
anyhow.
The Forrest dame caught a number of
the Liberal Conference people, on Friday
night, and dined them carefully in the tree-
girt bungalow of the Toorak Turnbulls.
The members of the Ladies’ Kennel Club
will tie up their treasured animals in the
Guild Hall, on September 6, and the pic-
ture _ place will be full of yelps and chain
rattlings. It will be for members’ dogs
only, and outside brutes will not even be
allowed to look in at the door.
Mrs. Shelton, one of the really important
dames of Malvern (it is something to be
important in Malvern), is giving a cflarity
dance right off her own cheque book. The
ambitious venture will convulse Malvern in
the local town hall, on Tuesday evening,
September 16.
Henrv Cornish, a local intellectual who
mixes Maeterlinck with football, gave a
dinner to a crowd of St. Kilda footballers,
after a recent Saturday battle. Henry had
his remarks prepared for a triumph ; but the
team nearly spoilt all by not providing the
triumph. Still, they managed to win after
a final burst. Lawyer Billy Williams was
an honored guest, and when he said some-
thing about giving a dinner himself, he was
nearly deafened by the cheering. They’ve
got healthy appetites, these footballers.
The Gaud Mayor, in a sunset blaze of
gold on a plain of pasteboard, invites the
Best People to a party at the Town Hall, on
September 20, to meet the members of the
Empire Parliamentary Association.
Apparently there are still numbers of
women who cling to the obsolete custom of
having a day at home. Hitherto the Day
has been religiously performed each month
by the wives of professordom at the Univer-
sity; but the new Union Club, within its
brainy borders, is modernising the situation
somewhat by observing every Monday. Some
of the professors’ wives have now announced
to the world that they are dropping their
blessed days, and will _be at home at the
Club on Mondays. This has caused a cer-
tain amount of consternation amongst the
fearfully exclusive cats —the~ ones that
reckon themselves away up on the ridge of
the roof and owners of the principal mouse-
trap. They complain bleakly that, under
the new arrangement, they will be liable to
meet Everybody. What a dreary place the
world must be to these people! Just a few
dukes and duchesses like themselves, and
the rest all horrid spectres.
The Newman Society is an exclusive club
that young people mention with pride if
they happen to be members. The older
members are, without exception, learned
folk who wear spectacles, can spell words
of three syllables, and never count on their
fingers in public. This more or less august
assembly revelled carefully in the St. Ivilda
Town Hall, the other night, under a canopy
smothered in pink clematis till it looked like
a Gippsland bush road. Special attention
was given to the comfort and dignity Of the
chaperones. A big, cosy drawing-room, well
carpeted and filled with downy, cushioned
armchairs and footstools, made the evening
of these almost obsolete creatures quite
happy. They sat heavily, gossiped in top-
notes, and talked brightly of the impending
supper. Mrs. Hennessy, out again after an
illness, wore a frivolous ninon frock. The
poor lady had been wearied by too much
popularity, and looked ever so much better
for the restful interval. Mrs. Alawa
Buchanan galloped into the leading lancers
and performed splendidly. Mrs. O’Donnell
brought a string of daughters, while Mrs.
Shelton personally conducted several sons.
A Toorak hostess lately requested her
guests to bring along photos of themselves
taken in infancy to make an evening game
of Who’s Who? Now a number of women
have a very good idea how old a number of
other women are. Even the dame who se-
lected a picture in which her baby dimples
were unobscured by fashion-dated clothes
didn’t escape. She quite overlooked the
fact that the photographer who took it gave
up business in Melbourne’s stone age. The
really artful woman was a spinster who
borrowed the baby photo of a relative many
years younger than herself.
Dr. Hope and his wife, who are at home
on a wide sheep run in northern New South
Wales, motored through three Australian
States, shipped to and petrolled through
some of India, and then embarked for
Britain, with the deliberate intention of
leaving tracks all over it. Then their much-
bumped car was stolen from under their
noses. It was driven off during a wayside
coffee interval, and with it went the rugs,
travelling bags and other accessories. After
all those speeding weeks, it was just as if
some villain had rushed off with the ances-
tral home.
Charles Littlejohn, son of the headmaster
of Scots’ College, has achieved popularity
amongst the Australians in London. (Be-
fore that he was popular at Oxford.) He
is now doing good work at St. Bartholo-
mew’s Hospital, and if he isn’t popular
amongst the paients and germs there must
be something wrong with them.
The Fairbairns have been informed that
they are grandparents again. Young Mrs.
Captain Balmain, only daughter of the
Cool art family, has presented another son
to her admiring warrior husband. The
baby’s first breath was drawn in foggy Lon-
don, so he’ll appreciate the sunshiny land
of his fathers when he throws down his
rattle in it.
Major Griffiths, the beautifully-decorated
warrior who arrived here as exchange offi-
cer from India, is returning to his job, and
taking his rose-pink-and-white wife with
him. Griffiths, with his Indian fixings, used
to look picturesquely barbaric in our work-
manlike processions, and his leopard-skin
saddle cloth, with the head and claws
dangling, will be sadly missed. Mrs. Henty
gave the pair a good-bye party at Glen
Druid, and the guests rested their intellects
by having a guessing competition.
Members of the Victoria League shrieked
at a recent meeting when they beheld their
dignified secretary, the elder Judge Chomley
daughter, take a header on to the over-
polished floor. Fortunately she wasn’t hurt;
but she had to go home for a rest. The
Masonic floor is always glassy; but on this
occasion it was worse than usual, and many
conversations were cut short with a gasp,
the gasper having suddenly discovered that
her feet were passing away, and that she
was in danger of being left without invis-
ible means of support.
Professor Petersen’s ewe lamb, Leonie,
who has hardly cast off the state of flapper-
dom, is booked for matrimony, in October,
with exchange-officer Mackworth, the tall
captain who has an Irish baronet for a
father and a notorious suffragette for a
sister-in-law. Toorak almost sat down on
its best hat with surprise when the news got
round.
A marriage which looks impressive on
paper has just been cabled to a proud mother
in Melbourne. Victor Buesst, a clever
musician who received practically all his
training in Australia, has been wedded with
elaborate ceremonial to Antonia, the Widow
Wonters of Steen, Chateau de Rubens, Bra-
bant, and about three other domiciles, sheds
or ruins in Europe.
Lionel D’Antoine is another eligible gone
from Melbourne girls. Miss Norah Field, a
fair Tasmanian girl, has booked him for
matrimony, a month hence, at Deloraine, in
the island where the apples grow.
A motored youth hereabouts yearns
towards a very young girl who is “just
dying” to wear his diamond half-hoop be-
fore all the world. The devoted pair confide
their secret in stage whispers to friends over
coffee at the evei-iasting opera, where they
regularly box together, chaperoned by nice
young dames. Then they add, gleefully,
“Father doesn’t know yet!” If they go on
talking about it much longer, father is
bound to hear.
Tumultuous Marshall Hall is missed from
the d.c. at Her Majesty’s these Quinlan
Opera nights. Memories of last year’s
Quinlan carnival, and of the Melba and
“Butterfly” seasons previously are asso-
ciated with visions of the long, lank pro-
fessor of enthusiasm, standing up after each
act and roaring “bravo!” like a mad bull.
His uproar was tiresome as a rule,
for it never varied. The good performances
and the merely middling ones moved him
equally in the matter of noise. The rowdy
prof, was far too apt to overdo it. But now
that he isn’t there the old habitues of the
opera feel like a band that has lost the big
drum, and the management also must be
very conscious of his absence. When the
voices on the stage are not exciting an opera
season wants a chief barracker to blow the
smouldering fires of satisfaction into fitful
flame.
Priscilla Verne, most of whose theatrical
press notices are older than she looks,
arrived back in Melbourne, the other day,
wearing a cerise hat. She has been picking
up American stars for William Anderson,
and travelled to the States on the same boai
as the late J. C. Williamson. In her opinion,
the old theatrical chief might have lived
for another 10 years if he hadn’t left Aus-
tralia. “No one on the boat was surprised
to hear of his death,” she says. “He wai
well when we left Sydney, but we ran into
a typhoon, and he fell ill and could not
regain his tone. He would wander up on
deck in the early hours of the morning—a
restless spirit looking for land. ‘lf I can
only get to land before they sew me up in
canvas and drop me overboard,’ he kept
saying. ‘l'll be right.’ His fellow-passen-
gers did not expect to see him reach San
Francisco.”
Miss Verne managed to pick up eight
stars. She engaged Don Matthews, the
original conductor of “The Land of Nod,”
which Anderson is staging. There are
Birdies, and Maisies, and Eddies amongsi:
the batch, also a person with the front name
of Pheul. Priscilla says she had great fun
interviewing the various applicants. One
woman strode in with an aggressive air
and said, “Before we go any way at all.
can I take my dawg with me? I hear a lot
about the quarantine restrictions in your
country; but I won’t go without my dawg.”
A man applicant asked if Australians under
stood the English language. Priscilla lec-
tured him for his ignorance, but he refused
to be ruffled. “I want to know, you know,
because it’s a mightv long way to go for the
eggs.” The way of the star collector was made
smooth by New York’s beautiful telephone
system. She managed to have satisfactory
long-distance business conversations, while
being fitted for clothes in her hotel bedroom.
One big engagement was clinched over the
wire while a shoplady was hauling at her
corset strings.
Grand-opera first-nighters are wandering
round wearing stunned expressions. There
have been 15 first nights so far, and the
unfortunate seat-holders have either suffered
from musical indigestion or allowed their
half-guineas (paid in advance) to go to
waste, and taken a rest cure at home in a
comfortable armchair. Blessed are the poor!
Thev need not attend culture drill, and their
spare time is their own.
From my Adelaide correspondent:— -
Twenty-five girls, all young and passing fair, more
or less, gave a dance in the North Adelaide Institute
last week. Mrs. Poulton, in blue satin, veiled with
black ninon; and Mrs. McMillan, in black, received
the guests on behalf of the hospitable 25. They were
either too (1) bashful or too (2) busy to do it them-
selves. The school next door was used as a supper-
room, and further food and drink were supplied in a
marquee opening off the ballroom. Also, I believe,
there was whisky to be had in an adjacent hostelry,
and lemonade in a room over the way. Our girls do
hit things up when they get really going. Amongst
the revellers were Mrs. H. H. Wigg and her daughter,
Miss Helen Waterhouse, Mrs. Girdlestone, Mrs. Roy
Melin, Mrs. Stanley Murray (in an effective frock of
maize-colored brocade), Mrs. Cowan, Mrs. Robert-
son, Miss May Duncan (whose dress of white chiffon
was mounted over pink), Mrs. F. J. Fisher, Miss
Margaret Huges, and about 2+ tons more female love-
liness than I have room for in this crowded column.
Matrimony has been breaking out in the most un-
expected places. For example, Mr. Herbert Phillips,
the philanthronist, is shortly to espouse Miss Ander
son, of the Y.W.C.A. (I don’t seem to have put this
over happily, but let it pass.) Messrs. James Mar-
shall and Birks are likewise to face the wedding
music ere long. All three are widowers and men of
substance.
Miss Marjorie Lowe, who has put in several cheery
weeks here, has gone back to Westralia.
Mrs. Montgomery Orr has arrived from Sydney, to
stay with her brother, Mr. John Labatt.
Most of the usual racegoers were at the last day
of the A.R.C. Grand National meeting. To get down
to details, I identified Mrs. Crozier and her daughter,
the Hawkers and McFarlanes, Mrs. Reg Colley, Mrs.
O’Leary, the Bowmans, Mrs. Kyffin Thomas, Mrs.
Maxwell Fowler, Miss Muriel Starr, Mrs. A. J.
McLachlan, Mrs. H. S. Newland, Mrs. Burston and
Miss Culross.
Miss Boothby and her sister asked about 20 people
to tea at the May Club on Tuesday in honor of Mrs.
George Coates. The guest consumed her portion of
the meal in a low-necked black velvet garment, sur-
mounted by a black hat. The Coates pictures are
selling well. Are we not a highly-cultured commun-
ity ? And, in any case, did not Lady Bosanquet open
our visitors’ private view on Friday?
Our champion golfers are all in Melbourne. You
know their tremendous mission better than I can tell
it to you. On the principle that “they also serve
who merely,” etc., etc., we are waiting and praying
for them, and watching with straining eyes (ah,
how eagerly!) the daily telegrams from the seat of
the trouble. Heaven send that all may be well.
Special orisons and so on are being sent up for Mrs.
Maxwell Fowler, who, by some scurvy triok- ~

Brook prepared to do itself proud. It raked t™

along affably. Crystal Brook was on its loyal feet
Then somebody shook the distinguished visitor bv the
hand and called him Bill. Or it may have been

command. He inquired. “Him!” somebody
“Him ! Why, that’s old .” And “old d
was, a nice old chap from a neighboring townshin
who builds cottages and coffins, and is illustrious
enough to have once been the local mayor.

From an Australian student in England :
Of the making of serums there is no end. First
Friedmann claimed to have scotched the übiquitous
tubercle bacillus by means of his turtle serum, and
now comes Mehnarto. The latter was a co-worker
with Koch in S’Afriea, and seems to have stumbled
on what he calls the “contra-serum” while fighting
the ravages of sleeping-sickness. Either he was
more diplomatic or less offensive than Friedmann,
d r ene % he ,nore evi-
dence, at any rate, the medical profesh seems to be
lending a passably willing ear to his claims. This
serum is being tested at the Dreadnought Hospital
in Greenwich, and also at a private sanatorium here
at Weston-super-Mare. Its results on two patients
—third-stage cases—personally known to me, are ai
yet distinctly favorable. Friedmann, at present in
America, is concealed in a cloud of impending liti-
gation and conflicting reports. The Government,
however, has appointed a commission, whose first
report, just issued, states that up to the present
results obtained with the turtle serum do not
bear out previous claims. As one claim was that a
single injection made you step out of your coffin and
kick a football, that may well be, without the
remedy being altogether useless. Meanwhile tuber-
culin is now in universal use, and that is 75 per
cent, of the battle against this generation’s deadliest
enemy.
* * • *
”9-3-11”: Kindly add to the list of im-
ported vegetable devils, paspalum, the grass which
the North Coast (N.S.W.) cocky up till recently
relied upon as a friend in need and a joy upon the
face of the landscape. Now he finds that it has
become matted on the surface of the ground, has
sucked the life out of the soil most favorable to its
growth, and is dying. But in death its curse and the
cocky are, unfortunately, not divided. Even on the
alluvial flats ploughing cannot be contemplated
because of the mass of fibrous roots. At the Farm-
ers’ Conference, held at Sydney lately, a proposal
was made that the Government should be asked
to serve out some cheap explosive to blast the land
into a more tractable condition, but it is mere con-
jecture whether the suggested means would produce
the desired result. It would be a strenuous under-
taking, anyhow. Getting rid of these foreign curses
generally is.
* * • •
‘ Tiaki”: Some of the old Maoriland
savages were made of tough stuff. Mahurangi was
a great warrior and a howler of long range. While
sleeping in the bush one day he was captured by a
hostile tribe. Being at once invited to
attend a great feast to be held in his honor,
he was duly led up to the great haangi or
“copper-Maori,” the heated stones glowing
in the great hole like a young volcano. Just
as his hosts were preparing to heave him in,
he gave a sudden shout and flung his captors
to right and left. Then urgent business
called him away, and he departed suddenly.
The anthropophagi gradually \atormed a
crescent in the rear of the flying nsat. There
was no escape. Right in front was a sheer
drop of two or three hundred feet, and
Mahurangi preferred the fall to the fire.
He sprang clear into the air and fell —into
the bushy top of a karaka tree. The pur-
suers built a fire on the brink (it was then
dark) and flung burning brands down to
show where the prospective meal had
jumped off. Some of the embers fell on the
meal’s naked body, but he dared not move.
Next morn, as the grey dawn quivered faintly
over the hills, a voice came out of the mist,
and the figure of the evanescent “meat” loomed
up ghostly and large on an adjacent height.
The owner of the voice remarked that he
would return inside of two moons and go
into the matter further with a view to settle-
ment. But Mahurangi was strategic, and re-
turned inside of two suns, and then there
was a smoke concert intermingled with lamen-
tations and long pig, for the old warrior
cooked his captors in their own haangi.
Tamati Torori will show you to this day the
identical karaka tree (a different one on each
occasion) as proof of this story. He will
also show you the adjacent pub. (About
the pub he never varies.)
» * * *
“Gouger” : With a friendly eye the hatter
watched his mates. One was a large, dark toad,
which sat on its haunches on one of the hearth-
stones, observing with bright, solemn eyes the antics
of a curiously piebald rat. The rat was endeavoring
to reach a piece of brownie, suspended by a string
from a rafter. The brownie was just in reach of the
rat’s paws when standing on its hind legs, but each
time the acrobat made a bite the string would get
into motion, causing the disgusted rodent to fall
back once more. Presently he ran up one of the
kitchen posts, along a rafter directly above the
string. There was a click as his strong little teeth
snapped. The old man broke into a roar of laughter.
The string was tied to a piece of wire. But
presently the rat began to climb cautiously down
the swaying strand. The hatter let him get half-
way, then held a firestick near the brownie. The rat
stood it for a while, turning his head this way and
that, but soon the thick smoke made him jump from
the string. He took up Jiis stand again directly under
the brownie, just as a shrill, piercing cry came from
a tree directly above the humpy. The toad’s little
eves twinkled brighter, and the rat’s ears went up
in a listening expression, like those of a startled
wallaby. The old hatter looked solemn. “Hear
that, Charlie?’’ he said. “That’s our night hawk.
And he s a-lookin’ for you. Ain’t you glad you’re
livin’ with me in a nice, cosy hut, eatin’ brownie,
instead of bein’ out in the cold scrub, with the night
hawk eatin’ you? But it’s time to go ter roost
Here’s yer brownie.” He cut down the piece of
brownie, and stroked the eating rat kindly. The toad
came over with a heavy flop for his share of the
caress; and then sat gazing thoughtfully into the
fading fire while the ancient turned in.

“J. 8.”: Little brother ’possum has been
finding the struggle for existence undulv strenuous
of late in Maoriland. His friends, the Acclimatisa-
tion Societies, are passing round the hat on his
AN INFORMAL AFFAIR.
HOSTESS: “Really, my dear friend, you should not
have dressed for dinner. I told you we were
only a family party."
behalf, and any kindly soul who drops in a fresh
gum-leaf will have his reward. Back in 1858 the
black species from Tassy was introduced to the
Southern districts, and this event was followed by
the introduction and gradual distribution of the
grey variety, the object being to develop a profitable
fur industry. At first, the Government provided
close protection to the marsupial, but he increased
and multiplied so vigorously that that eternal
calamity-howler, the cocky, thought he saw hollow-
eyed ruin staring him in the whiskers. The upshot
of the cocky’s shrieks for help was the withdrawal
of protection from ’possum, and since then (1902)
poison, the gun and other exterminators have been
more or less relentless. Now, it is being
screw-jacked into the agitated Backbone of the
country that the little furry neighbor is not so much
an enemy as an asset. A fair estimate is, that off
waste bush land (and the Great Ranger knows there
is enough of it in M.L.) 40 ’possums a year could
be taken. With dressed skins bringing 15s. to 18s.
each, this is a prospect that appeals to the settler
on his most flexible side. Wherefore, the forth-
coming application for protection to the ’poss’ for
nine months in the year is not likely to raise undue
opposition.

not advertised our coming?"

‘G.V.W.”: To tell the average Maori-
lander that salt mines are included in his great
natural resources is to call tenders for uncompli-
mentary remarks. Still, the truth is mighty and
will prevail—unless it is bought off. The mines
are situated in the S.W. of Marlborough (top of the
South Island) and are worked by sheep. These
animal* first learned to drink the saline fluid from
a series of springs, and afterwards took to chewing
the salt clay along the terraces. Where the terrace
is of sufficient height a tunnel is generally worked.
One is 60ft. to the face; and several are 9ft. or
10ft. high. I have seen three-year-old sheep with
teeth worn to the gums, but in the average animal
the molars last much longer. It is a joyous thing
to see a rush of 40 or 50 beasts to get out of a
tunnel on the arrival of a shepherd. Nothing equals
it but the performance which follows the appearance
of an agile policeman at a pub 10 minutes after
closing time.
* * * *
“Gouger” : When short of a fishing line, the
Daintree River (Q.) Binghi uses a strand of lawyer
vine. Mostly it is about 20ft. long and as strong
as the tales of the Wimmin’s Political League. The
prickles are left on one end and a large scrub worm
is attached to one of the barbs—very unwillingly
on the part of the worm. The bait is then pushed
overboard and sufficient time allowed the fish to
thoroughly swallow it. As Charles Garbage says,
“I leave the rest to my fair reader’s imagination.”
The Binghis catch crayfish with the vine. The
leaves are stripped, the point tapered and held in-
vitingly before the marine fowl. Being highly in-
quisitive, the submarine eagle gradually approaches,
holds out his claws for a grip, then opens his
mouth for a taste. A light tap on the rear end of
the stick, and the sharp end has gone clear down
his throat. The cray vulture’s surprise is painful to
witness. He says in his excitement that the whole
affair is unprecedented.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : I wish to gently remonstrate
with “Cooladee” (B. 31/7/’l3), who set fire to the
big nest of the unique but fast-disappearing shy
mallee hen for no particular reason. He is possessed
of devils and gorged of bullfrogs. His ancestors
were in the same class as the griffin and the Peruvian
dog, and his bald-headed grandfather devoured
thistles according to the law of nature. Thus one
understands the kind of freak “Cooladee” is, and
reproves him with mildness. The mallee fowl is
purely Australian, only being found in the mallee
scrubs. Moreover, the family to which it belongs
blesses the Australian bird region with 27 species of
th« 28 known to the satellite. The other somehow
was chucked, blown or born on the west side of the
p'eat world crack—Wallace’s Line—and hangs out
in Java. Twelve years ago most of the Victorian
Mallee abounded with the mound-builder, and I've
seen half a dozen nests on one sandhill. Now, the
bird is a rarity over most of the vast Mallee tracts.
They have been pushed back and exterminated by
(1) settlement; (2) foxes, which are the deadliest
foe; (3) damphools. In a few years there won’t be
a mallee fowl left alive in Australia excepting on
Kangaroo Island, where the South Aus. Government
has a national reserve. Good Australians will be
glad to know that the colony is doing well; others
can leap through a hangman’s noose with two grind-
stones tied to their ankles.

THE MAIDEN’S PREY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Argus urges that the local immigration
department might, with propriety, take up the busi-
ness of a matrimonial bureau. It holds that a suit-
able response should be made to those young women
who write from England, inquiring if husbands are
provided. One recent correspondent (her name was
Agnes) asked: “Do you know of any nice young man
who is lonely ? I feel sure there are some poor
fellows miles away from anywhere who want cheering
up.”
On ranges high, in forests far,
And where the mirage waters flit,
A crowd of such young chaps there are,
Who’d do with cheering up a bit.
Perhaps on reading this they’ll scoot
To send Miss Agnes their address,
With photographs, and plans of route,
And weekly earnings. If they suit,
Good-bye to all their loneliness.
The papers praise the spirit fine
Which spurs the maidens to their quest;
They’ll go where looms the mountain pine,
Or to the diggings in the West.
To any old extreme they’ll go
To catch a man of any shape,
And cheer the rascal up—although
That’s just what half those blokes we know
Have gone out backwards to escape!
VICTORIA.

The telephone converts the spoken words into
silent electrical impulses. In a single Bell telephone
cable, a hundred conversations can be carried on
side by side without interference. Each conversa-
tion is led through a system of wire pathways to
its proper destination, and whispers its message into
a waiting ear. Without such service, both citv and

A WONDERFUL FLESH BUILDER.
PHYSICIAN’S DISCOVERY ENABLES
THIN PEOPLE TO PUT ON TWO TO
THREE POUNDS A WEEK.
Special Medical Reports, Paris. —That ex-
cessive thinness is due to the inability of the
digestive organs to properly assimilate food
has long been known ; it has, however, re-
mained for Professor Muller, the eminent
scientist and investigator, to discover that
this weakness of the digestive organs is pri-
marily due to lack of nerve force, and to tell
to the world how that lack of nerve force
may be corrected, and thin people, men or
women, old or young, enabled to put on two
to three pounds a week, and become fat,
plump, strong and healthy. Professor Mul-
ler says this may be done without change of
diet, exercises, etc. All he finds necessary
is to strengthen the nervous system, and for
this purpose he recommends a special nerve
food and flesh builder which can be pre-
pared by any chemist, consisting of soz.
Tincture of Cardamoms Compound 8.P.,
2oz. Salrado Compound, 3oz. Elixir Simplex,
and water. One to two tablespoon-
fuls should be taken a few minutes before
each meal, and almost from the beginning
you will note an increased feeling of energy,
vitality and reserve force. This nerve food
is also very beneficial in overcoming the
ravages of tuberculosis, and by some has
been claimed to actually cure consumption.
Because of its nerve and flesh-building pro-
perties it is invaluable in all such wasting
troubles as catarrh, stomach or liver
troubles and neurasthenia; but, naturally,
should not be taken by those who for any
reason object to increasing their weight.*

(B. 19/6/T3) that tubercle is rife amongst cattle
because the breeders are trying to increase the milk
flow, cannot be entertained. You do not get an in-
creased supply of milk by malnutrition and inbreed-
ing, but by judicious feeding and selection. “Cata-
main” also says that the more milk a cow gives the
weaker she becomes, until, eventually, she will have

to be propped up. Against this allegation there is
the famous De Kol breed of Holsteins, which not
only are the world’s record-holders for quantity of
milk yielded, but also are show cattle. The reason
for the spread of tubercle in cattle is the cocky’s
repugnance to testing his herd, in case he might
have to put ,a few cows out of their misery. Tubercle
is not an hereditary diesase; but a calf that sucks a

“Eddyson” : E. Elwood was right down to
it. He was motherless and stony, his nosebag was
empty, his dog was starved, and his feet had gone
back on him. The hunt for a job had been going on
for a fortnight, and had dragged him over about one-
third of the map of Victoria. He sat down by the
roadside and pondered bitterly. While Edward sat
and suffered, and swore, his dog Dodo whined fever-
ishly, and scratched with insane energy at the foot of
a dead gum butt. Edward Elwood paid no heed to
the animal’s exuberant folly till something kicked
out behind hit him on the ear. Then he only addressed
Dodo by name, with a few petulant impieties. Dodo
went on scratching, and whining, and yapping, kick-
ing his loose dirt out aft with even greater spring
and virility, and presently Ned got another blow on
the neck. He turned this time, and reached for a
rock ; but before he could get his good work in on
the dog, his eye fell on the object that had hit him.
It was a sovereign—a bright new sovereign. There
was another on the fold of his coat, and five more
dotted the grass. As he gazed, open-mouthed and
motionless in his desperate amazement, Dodo kicked
change for a fiver straight into his face. In precisely
the twentieth part of a second E. Elwood had dis-
placed his dog Dodo, and was pawing in the excava-
tion. Two seconds’ mining produced an old brown
felt hat full of money. Elwood did not wait to won-
der ; he had no time for problems. A bit of coin had
come to him. His not to reason why, his not to
make reply; it was up to him to shift it, and to
shift it as far as possible before set of sun. He
stowed the hatful of quids and things in his swag,
pulled on his boots, and forgot his feet and all his
other sorrows. He went west, young man. He moved
with celerity. His spirit was refreshed. The world
seemed a fit and proper habitation, after all. Elwood
had covered one mile and a distance when the two
mounted police overhauled him. They wanted to
know where he had spent last night. They were full
of impertinent curiosity, and doubts, and suspicions.
Edward was hauled back to Glover’s to substantiate
his statement that he had spent the night in the
shearers’ hut there. Glover testified to that effect;
two employees of Glover’s were prepared to swear on
nine Bibles that the swagman produced had not left
the hut between the hours of nine at night and six in
the morning. Elwood was released, and resumed his
westward tendency. For a man with sore feet and a
great lack of sustenance he got over the long measure
with wonderful efficiency. At Tollbar he had a meal.
At wee Wee he took train for Melbourne. Settled in
a humble room in a Port boarding-house, Edward El-
wood had time to look about him and grasp facts. He
gathered from some excited remarks in the Herald
that the bank at Sheepskin had been robbed of £212
in gold and silver on the 11th, or early on the morn-
ing of the 12th. He counted and found there was
£2lO in the old brown hat. Sheepskin was a town-
ship two miles and a quarter from Glover’s house at
Back Creek. Elwood recognised the old brown felt
as Glover’s hat, but he said never a word. He was
not the man to betray a poor cocky who had greatly
befriended him.
“Gouger”: I was referee, time-keeper,
bottle-holder and audience at one of the rarest
fights in N.Q. records the other day. A black-
backed, red-bellied water-snake (a deadly variety)
was watching me intently from the bank of a shal-
low creek as I manoeuvred to get a shot at him.
The water was as crystal, and suddenly over his
nibs’s back a big green eel appeared. Instinct must
have made the snake turn his head,
and in l-33rd of an instant he dived
under the bank and wedged himself
in between the roots of a tree. Pok-
ing his head through an opening, he
swayed it viciously to and fro while
the eel bit savagely and cursed in
seven watercress dialects. For 10
minutes neither beast had the advan-
tage, the snake slipping between the
roots with marvellous swiftness at
every rush of the eel. Then the eel
used tactics. Pressing through the
root spaces he slowly jambed Jack
Johnson on the ropes and swung a
dazzler. I started to count, and got
as far as the “o” in one when the
snake, like a streak of buttered elec-
tricity, coiled round and round the
other’s body, and bashed his tusks
into the back of the big head 19 to
the dozen. The eel wheeled round
and round in the water in circles,
plunging, splashing, snapping, but
unable to break away. The betting
was any odds on snake, when more
tactics by eel brought the end. Sight-
ing a narrow opening between two
rocks, he dashed through at ex-
press speed, and literally tore the enemy from
his hold. The little black fellow made for the
bank again, but the distance was too great. There
was one snap as the eel caught up and half a snake
slid into the downstairs cupboard. The other half
followed just as easily. It didn’t even bump on the
bottom step.
“Tiaki” : The Maori is chafing against the
stringent measures adopted by the authorities since
the small-pox outbreak in the North to restrict his
peregrinations. The old men of the tribes reckon
that the “smoora-pox” is nothing more than toretiti,
a skin disease that used to attack the brown man’s
pelt and leave a few holes in it. Before the pakeha
came, Henare had very few ailments, but, in about
the year 1782-3, a vessel was wrecked at Palliser
Bay, and the local cannibal society promptly added
roast crew to the menu. Unfortunately, some of the
meat was suffering from a strange disease, and, on
being stowed away in the brown bingys, turned on
the unsuspecting natives with such deadly effect that,
out of 700 men only a few survived. In one bay
where 300 representatives attended the “long pig”
conference, only two survived. Henare called the
disease te upoko o te rewharewha, but it stalked
THE COLOR GRADE.
right on. The provisions were Scotch, and personified
the first recorded attempt, according to Tregear, in
“The Maori Race,” to form a colony in M.L. There
were 60 in the party.
* * * *
“Gouger”: Scattered over the breadth of
North Queensland, wherever happens to be an iso-
lated little township, are numerous tribes of the
semi-civilised aboriginal, with the congregated man-
hood fast going to waste, owing to the flapdoodle
teaching of the missionary and the opium-charcoal
and diseases of the Chow. What I want to know
is why this good material (for it is good material
in its natural condition) should not be welded into
the defence scheme. According to missionary
statistics there are at present 80,000 known blacks
in Northern Australia. Surely, from that number
there could easily be drawn a few thousand men,
whose disciplined aid would be very welcome in the
time that is sure to write its wrinkle on the azure
brow of things in general. Fighting is the one
thing in our civilisation which the aboriginal can
understand; and he is an adept at learning any-
thing his brain can comprehend. He is a good
horseman, and, what is only generally known to
bushmen who are used to natives, a first-class shot,
whenever he has been enabled to practise with a
rifle. He is inured to the roughest of hardship, i.e.,
travelling long distances over bad country where
food and water are scarce, and, what will count
mightily in the coming guerilla struggle, he will be
able to find his tucker after the regimental com-
missariat has been boiled down for its axle grease.
If the Government erected large schools in the
vicinity of the more numerous tribes, raked in the
young men, clothed them in a cheap and suitable
uniform such as they love to wear, fed them on
good, plain food, drilled them and taught them how
to shoot, they would develop a defence unit of
great assistance on the day the school kids are
suddenly told to put their slates away and go out
and save the old man’s scalp. Of the aboriginal’s
courage there is plenty of evidence all over Aus-
tralia, and his willingness, when led and praised
by the white man, would make him a foe equal to
a herd of half-caste devils and octoroon demons.
»'* * *
“Kola”: Tommy Tanna has struck fresh
trouble. The New Hebrides are overrun with mission-
aries, chiefly Presbyterian, who have played the
deuce with Tommy’s happiness. His sing-sings have
been abolished; and he has been driven to wear
clothes he hates. But the last straw was laid on his
back recently, when a Christadelphian mission was
established on one of the principal islands. The Christ-
adelphians are vegetarians, and they are calling on
Tommy to give up not only his pig, but all flesh,
and even fish ; and to live on yams and taro. Tommy
doesn’t like it; as he puts it: “Missionary plenty
big fool.” But the queer brethren insist that the
meat-eaters will frizzle in perdition when they die,
and they have already coaxed a few timorous nigs to
tread the narrow, pig-less path, and given plenty of
others a dreadful scare.
At the foot of a tree on a possum farm
block in the Leeuwin Peninsula (W.A.) the bone,
a man, also boots, buttons and scraps of elnU, *
discovered the other day. A wire noose
from the tree, and all these things are said to
gest that the owner of the bones must have
himself many years ago. In America the S uw«
tion would be that he was lynched.
ART AND LETTERS.
BOW: “Did that top G sound a triple flat?"
BRUSH: “ G, was it? It sounded to me like L."
“Silas Snell”: When the Rev. James
Alfey received a call to Pigoreet, he thought the
Lord was being good to him. He presently began to
change his mind, and Miss Emma Bayles was en-
tirely responsible for his waning faith. The Rev.
James Alfey was a small, pathetic, blonde clergy-
man, tow-headed, and possessing a marked facial re-
semblance to a distressed gazelle in’ spectacles.
Miss Emma Bayles was a short, plump damsel of 20,
in rude health, with red hair and effective hands
and feet, holder of the Pigoreet milking record, and
champion in her class at turnip-digging and plain
sewing. With a running start she could: wring a
large red cow quite dry in 120 seconds. It took
Emma three Sundays to work from a back seat in
the church right up to a front pew, dead centre,
drawn by the irresistible attraction of the Rev.
James Alfey. Thereafter she was a source of con-
sternation and painful distraction to the presiding
clergyman. She sat right under his eye, and gaped
up at him in a state of adoration that put him off
his subject, made him forget his text, mislay his
“seventhly, dearly beloved brethren,” and entirely
overlook his peroration. It was no use his trying
to ignore Emma while staring fixedly at the other
end of the church. Ten minutes of such behavior
reduced Em to tears, and she would sob and snuffle
noisily to the detriment of the service and the utter
subordination of all holiness. Emma had another
bad habit, too; she would put love letters in the
plate, and when the Rev. Alfey expostulated with
her she fainted heavily, and permitted him to be
discovered with a collapsed damsel on his hands. If
in the course of his sermon the poor young clergy-
man made any very touching or pointedly sentimental
remarks, Emma was shaken to her centre, and
uttered a loud, dolorous barking, or made sobbing
and squashy noises, and simply smothered the rest
of the deliverance. She persisted, too, in leaving
presents of woollen slippers in the pew in the most
pointed manner, and on several occasions she met
James at the church portal with billies of cream.
She would put eggs on his doormat, or sit on the
step nursing a cheese or a melon, publicly awaiting
his home-coming. Mr. Alfey had been officiating at
Pigoreet just three months when Emma’s brother
Sam called to see him. “You know, it’s about Em,”
Sam said mysteriously. “See, it ain’t the same ez
if she’d be a drag on a man, she bein’ champyin’
middle-weight milker, ’n’ good et the plain sewin’,
She’d bring you a tidy bit of land, too. Think it
over.” Mr. Alfey spluttered and expostulated.
“You think it over,” Sam advised. Later Em’s
mother and father called, and Em’s mother cried
for an hour and a half, while Em’s father enlarged
on the advantages Em offered, presumably as a
matrimonial investment. “Three ’undred acres Em’s
got,” said Em’s father. Even when Em’s father had
done, Em’s mother wanted* to stay and continue cry-
ing indefinitely. Meanwhile Em’s conduct in church
was becoming subversive of all discipline, decency
and devotion.. She had filled the church, but scores
of the careless unregenerate were attending divine
worship merely for the gratification of watching her
comic raptures. Then a number of prominent
churchmen formed themselves into a deputation to
wait upon the Rev. James Alfey and expostulate
with him for his seemingly ungenerous and unchris-
tian conduct towards Miss Bayles. They were met
at the door by Emma. Emma held a broom. “Here,
you get out o’ this!” said Emma threateningly. “I
won’t have you botherin’ him. He’s worried enough
as it is, heaving knows. Me an’ ’im was married
this mornin’.”
McPHEE'S DISEASE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Michael Flannery McPhee,
Who’d been on a jamboree,
Was recov’ring from his spree
’Neath the local doctor men;
He had seen the usual crew
Of bush jim-jams, red and blue,
And striped snakes of ev’ry hue
But was getting well again.
It was then I met Joe Greer,
Who’d been with him on the beer,
And remarked: “I’m glad to hear
That McPhee is on the mend.”
“Well,” said Joseph with a curse
On the doctors and the nurse;
“But he’s not; he’s getting worse,
And I fear he’s near his end.
“For to-day, ’twixt you and me,
When I asked him could he see
Snakes now, ‘No,’ says he,
‘Thanks to Hiven, not a one.’
And, it nearly made me sick,
They were crawlin’ on him thick,
And he couldn’t see, poor Mick !
Ah, he’s done —poor lad, he’s done!”
N.S.W
HY. E. HORNE.

Hun of thousands of people have derived
immeasurable benefits
From Sanatogen
Thousands of them have written to the proprietors
to say so. Their testimony records marvellous results,
which, until its discovery, could never have been obtained
by any means at the disposal of the physician.
Among the writers are many celebrities with whose
names
You will
be as familiar as with your own.
Tens of thousands of doctors throughout the world
have prescribed Sanatogen, which a medical authority,
writing in The Medical Press and Circular, states, “has
strengthened the physician’s hand a hundredfold,” while,
he adds, “the wonders brought about by this prepara-
tion are no less manifold than amazing.”
This is high praise for any preparation to
Receive
but it has been endorsed by innumerable statements.
Here, for instance, is one by that famous Authoress,
Madame Sarah Grand : “ Sanatogen has done everything
for me which it is said to be able to do for cases of
nervous weakness and exhaustion. I began to take it
after nearly four years’ enforced idleness from extreme
weakness, and felt the benefit almost immediately. And
now, after taking it steadily three times a day for
twelve weeks, I find myself able to enjoy both work
and play again, and also able to do as much of both as I
ever did.”
To be so revitalised is, indeed, in Shakespeare’s
words, to receive
A Second Life
That experience may be duplicated by every reader
of these words, for Sanatogen so influences the nervous
and muscular systems, the digestion and the great blood-
making organs, that it restores the normal balance dis-
organised by illness or disease.
Sanatogen is sold by all Chemists, in tins, at 2s. 6d.,
3s. 6d. and 6s. 6d.
