“Rosna’s” whoop from London: —
They hung a gold and white canopy over Aus-
tralia’s slice of the Strand this week to protect
King George, in case the infrequent British sun
should blink upon him when he was laying the
foundation-stone of the Commonwealth’s new London
house. They also built him a canvas drawing-room,
with rambler roses climbing like mad up its walls,
and tiger-lilies, daisies and carnations waving their
heads at him. Here on gilt chairs in tidy rows sat
velvet-clad Agents-General and their wives, with our
Shy Commissioner in a blue and pink sash to mar-
shal them. Beyond were stands to seat 2000 Aus-
tralians, or as the Daily Mail grandiloquently de-
scribed them, “lords of vast estates and masters of
huge flocks and herds.” Four thousand people are
said to have grappied for tickets and become sud-
denly proficient in foreign languages when they
couldn't be got. The Queen, girbed in lavender-
flowered silk and a botanic hat, brought along the
royal daughter, solemn 16, in pale green and
sprigged chiffon. The Reid dame was in white; and
pretty Thelma, in a pompadour frock of flower-
dotted silk, handed the officially prescribed posy to
Queen Mary. Lady Denman, the Chief Justice Grif-
fifths, the Archbishop Clarkes, the Muirhead Collins
twain, and six Mr. and Mrs. Agents-General were pre-
sented to the Monarchy. Proceedings were refresh-
ingly slick. Ada Crossley instilled some music into
that soulless ditty, th? Unnatural Anathema. Arch-
bishop Clarke put in a word with Providence; and
George Reid and George Rex had a little polite con-
versation. Then the latter took a gold trowel with
a handle of Australian blackwood, and a gold mallet
with the Commonwealth’s arms bespread on it, mut-
tered an incantation, and the thing was done.
(N.B. —If you are ever hard up for £1 13s.
I can tell you where George Rex buried that sum in
good Australian coins.) One of the lords of vast
estates aforesaid started a coo-ee at the psycho-
logical moment, and the other 1999 took it up with
gusto, much to the joy of the British press, which
shouted it in big headlines next day. Some rags
called it an “ear-splitting shriek” ; others with bet-
ter ears said it was a beautiful, melodious “call.”
All the Strand had wreaths in its hair, and the
jingo public turned out in thousands, as usual, to
look at George and Mary. By 10 a.m. you could
hardly penetrate the Strand. By 12 the high jinks
had begun. By 1 p.m. the Commonwealth was fold-
ing its tents like the Arab and silently stealing
away to lunch, and there wasn’t a jink left.
Archdeacon Boyce writes urging citizens
to vote for No-license at the local option
polls in New South Wales in December.
One portion :
The experience in the 12 no-license electorates in
Maoriland —the 12 gems shining in that country’s
crown— is conclusive that the law is well kept.
Simultaneously, there reached The Bulle-
tin an opinion from Auckland (M.L.)
Star, which lives handy to the question.
This is its experience of at least one of the
shining gems :
Notwithstanding that the King Country is practi-
cally a no-license area, a tremendous amount of
open and illicit drinking of spirit takes place. This
has been proved by the number of convictions for
sly grog during the year, and also by the regrettable
eases of death by alcoholic poisoning at the Tauma-
runui end. Provided the regulations are complied
with, more liquor than can be safely consumed by
an individual can be received from Te Awamutu or
Taihape. The cause of a great deal of the exces-
sive drinking is that a syndicate “imports” a case
of whisky or keg of beer, and on its arrival “sees
it out,” thus drinking more than if supplies were
regulated.
This paper leaves the matter there, merely
remarking that a no-license district in w r hich
you can’t buy three-penn’orth of beer or
sixpence-worth of whisky, but can bring in
a whole keg of each liquor from outside,
doesn’t seem quite the teetotal place it
might be. It appears to have exchanged
small, regular drinks in a glass for large,
irregular, long-interval drinks in a barrel.
R- Maoriland will become no-license from
one end to the other ; shut up its breweries
and distilleries; prohibit the importation or
manufacture of alcohol in any form ; and
thus g 0 tke wko le hog, then Boyce’s gospel

“Straw Hat”: When the morning paper
chattering about white sheets of frost,
it is pleasant to look round and count the
s P r ‘. The draper is
very first to burst into song in the rag
columns of the daily press. But he com-
11|iences the minute the first fury of winter
slackens. The girl with the white
JfeM pops up spasmodically ; but she is a
unreliable guide. vagi ant ray
sunshine IS sufficient to fool her. Then
there are trees and shrubs that are almost
as easily deceived as the summery female,
Under the influence of fermented sunshine
j. key develop Sap to the head and joyously
„„f i„f„„
OU I tender buds that are Ister nipped
by tbe trost. Amongst other first spring
symptoms are (1) the young man who has
left his vest at home. (Later on he is very
sorry for it); (2) the first picnic party,
(Who hasn’t seen that dispirited band shiv-
ering forlornly round the evening camp-
fire that is all smoke?) ; and (3) the
deluded bird that sings unconvincingly for
half an hour of watery sunshine, and then
subsides with apologetic chirps when the
clouds blow up and the wind whistles chil-
lingly amongst the trees. All these signs
are vain and of no account; in fact, they
are only one degree better than the first
summer time-tables that are pasted up, only
to be lashed to pulp by the driving rain.
The one and only sure indication of
Nature’s returning warmth is the first surf
rescue of the season. When the limp
prize is dragged out of the foam and laid
on the beach by a proud retrieving life-
saver, then the hot weather is due, and the
proprietor of a soft-drink fountain can smile
again and oil the cash register.
The story of a multi-millionaire family :
J. B. Joel, the S'African multi-millionaire, who
recently gave 40,000 guineas for a racehorse, has
had a romantic past. He is a nephew of the ex-
circus clown and millionaire Barney Barnato (who
jumped overboard from a Donald Currie liner when
w .en) and bother of the late Woolf Joel
(shot dead in his Johannesburg office by an adven-
turer of sorts). He has, with his brother Solly, so
far managed to survive the bitter enmity which
Israel’s claim-acquiring methods made for him and
others in the early days of the Rand. Some de-
served the enmity even if these two didn’t.
The Johannesburg of those days was a straggling
collection of weatherboard shanties and corrugated
iron “hotels.” The principal pub was the Prince of
Wales, and here Israel transacted most of its deals,
Occasionally a claim-owner, who had refused to sell
his claim, was discovered at the bottom of his shaft
in a battered and extinct condition. There were no
inquests in those days. The Boers didn’t mind ; the
death of a Uitlander meant one less disturber of
their peace. The defunct claim-owner was usually
buried in a convenient mullock heap by some benevo-
lent Jew, who afterwards produced a sale-note and
took possession of the deceaseds claim. Some of
the richest claims on the Rand were acquired in this
way, and formed the nucleus of the immense mining
companies of to-day.
After the murder of their brother Woolf, Jack
and Solly decided that England was the safest place
for them; but the day came when Jack Joel was
called upon to give l evidence in a law-suit in Johan-
nesburg. He was between Charbvdis and Scylla. If
he returned to the Rand he ran a grave risk of losing
his life; if he did not attend he was likely to lose
a part of his fortune. .Money being dear, he went
back. It was a month or two before the war, and
the Rand was in a turmoil. But Joel surmounted
the difficulties and dodged the dangers. Reserving
a compartment in the mail train, he had himself
wrapped up in brown paper, corded, and placed par-
cel-like on the luggage rack. His enemies, aware
of his intending visit, and bent on taking his life,
boarded the train during a temporary stop a few
miles outside of Johannesburg. A close inspection
of each carriage, including the one with the large
brown paper parcel on the rack, failed to reveal
their man, and the man-hunters departed cursing.
Arriving at Johannesburg, Joel was dumped into a
parcel-deliverv van and driven to a friend’s house.
During the hearing of the law-suit he never left the
court building, living in one of the janitor’s rooms.
The ease over and won, Joel had himself brown-
paper parcelled again and deposited in the guard’s
van of the outgoing train. This was on the eve of
war, and on the same train were scores of other
Jews scurrying away from the danger-zone. The
outwitting of his enemies in the manner described
formed the chief topic of J. B. Joel’s conversation
on the homeward-bound liner.
But though he has accomplished great successes
in “finance” and upon the turf, the social sphere in
which the King moves is closed to him. Also, most
of the other social spheres are closed to him. And,
because of this, he is reputed to be a bitterly disap-
nninfnH man
TN . , ‘
Dick Knuckey, still engaged m patching
up the overland telegraph line, was with
Charles Todd and party when the wire was
first stretched. The old warrior is over 70
now. but his one good eye is keen, and the
i j i • i j « i mi
years have not kinked his back. There
were two Knuckeys in the original party—
both coachbuilders and wheelwrights, and
both imbued with the spirit of adventure
Knuckey’s Swamp gets its name from John
Randall Knuckey, long since drifted into
the Great Obscure. The other, Richard
occasionally sallies into the city with
brother bushmen. Recently some of them
got seated in the front row of the dress
circle at Adelaide Theatre Royal. One of
them had purchased a pair of new hoots
for the occasion, and midway through the
show removed them for comfort. The more
sophisticated Knuckey, noting the amuse-
ment of the boiled shirts and the astonish-
ment Ot tbe usher, prevented the latter’s
murder by removing both his pal and his
pal’s boots. “If that usher had spoken to
gob about his boots,” the old man explained
“ he would have t chucked into the stalls ”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Now that Britain has borrowed Maori-
land’s lone and solitary warship for service
in the Mediterranean the situation becomes
more than ever reminiscent of that at Rome
when the Ma Country began to call in the
legions.
The N.S.W. Government’s Compulsory
Vaccination Bill just amounts to a state-
ment that you must get vaccinated unless
you object, but if you don’t feel like it you
needn’t. If you do feel like it blit still
object then you may be fined £5, which you
needn’t pay unless you want to, the alterna-
tive being three months’ voluntary imprison-
ment.
Last week’s Premiers’ Conference between
Watt of Vic. and Holman of N.S.W. tackled
a variety of subjects without reaching any
definite conclusion on most of them. One
momentous question was, however, grappled
with and overcome. The pair decided to pur-
chase and run all the Murray River punts,
whether now municipally or privately owned.
It is on such large matters that the splendid
genius of the State Insects shows out with
blinding brilliance.
The blessed Palace of Peace at the Hague,
which has been under construction amid
much bloodshed for several years, has been
opened at last. The one country which
showed a clear appreciation of the joke was
Brazil —-or perhaps the Brazilian struck a
great idea by accident. Anyhow, he sent his
peace delegation across the Atlantic in a
huge warship. In other words, Pedro brought
bis gun.
THE PARABLE OF SHEPHERD
JOE COOK.
Mr. Cook was informed that the Opposition would
not give any pairs. —News item.
'so Joseph goeth forth to seek that which was lost.
An item from the programme of Percy B.
Colquhoun, who is seeking the Wade party’s
nomination in the Mosmaii district
(N.S.W.)
Decentralisation, such as the opening up of
those two great natural harbors, Port Stephens and
Twofold Bay, with railway connections to existing
lines.
Does any other patriotic candidate propose
to compete in this matter? Don’t all speak
at once.
Constantine, who rules at Athens, threat-
ens to assume the old Byzantine purple robe
and the old Byzantine crown, and declare
himself Emperor of Greece. It is a pictur-
esque. idea, lie is the first Greek king born
in Greece since the Turkish conquest, for
Otho and George were imported foreigners.
His name recalls the old glories, for it was
(’onstantine the Eleventh who defended the
last scrap of the old Greek Empire, and died
fighting at St. Romanus Gate against the
Unspeakable Turk. The finding of the old
imperial togs and the old imperial diadem
will add a shred of pomp to the resumed
dignity. And the assumption of the old title
will signify that Constantine puts in his
definite claim to Constantinople, and gives
the Turk notice to quit. So presumably the
Balkan scrap will presently start again. It
seems as if the new Palace of Peace at the
llame had better be turned into a military
hospital.
SAME OLD PARTY WIT AND
REPARTEE.
SMALL ONE: “ St-top, boys! It's wastin’ lh ’

OTHERS (loud laughter): "Then get him into
harness an start work—or come down!"
Times have been stormy of late. There
have been in quick succession the Boer War,
Chow-Jap. War, Russo-Jap. War, Russian
civil war, Chinese ditto, Mexican ditto, some
more ditto in Central and South America,
the Turco-Italian scrap, the Turko-Serbo-
Greek-Bulgar-Montenegrin row, the new
Balkan war which followed, the chronic
hold-up in Morocco, with France and Spam
both involved, the Portuguese revolution and
some sundries. And all this time the Palace
of Peace at the Hague was building. Ap-
parently the blessed place brought ill-luck.
By appointing a temporary judge
(Solicitor-General E. D. Dobbiei the
Tasmanian Government has rather
cleverly side-stepped a difficulty,
nudge Maclntyre’s retirement leaves
the island with only two judges, one
of whom, the Chief Justice, is not too
robust and may retire at any moment.
Neil Elliott Lewis was marked for
the vacancy, but his retirement from
the Assembly would stagger the Gov-
ernment to death, as the vacated seat
(Denison) would almost certainly be
captured by the Labor party, making
parties 15 all. Premier Solomon and
N. K. Ewing are also understood to
be in the running for a judgeship ; but
Lewis has first call among the politi-
cal lawyers. The appointment of a
temporary judge postpones the trouble
for two years (or until after the next
general election). And the end’of the
world and the General Resurrection of
the Dead and the arrival in Hobart of
the Beast of Revelation, whose num-
ber was 666, may chance before
then.
Wade’s attempted censure upon the
N.S.W. Government’s scheme for handing
over the control of £3.000.000 worth of rail-
way construction to English private enter-
prise was defeated in the Assembly by six
votes. Beeby voted with his old friends.
Briner, whose electorate is to be traversed
by one of the railways that are to be built
under English Private Enterprise supervi-'
sion and built with money raised by Private
Enterprise, went with Beeby. David Fell,
whose name appears as a member of the
subsidiary company which the English
private enterprise and railway-building con-
cern has floated in Australia, didn’t vote.
The Speaker couldn’t vote. It was a close
thing, but Minister Griffith’s English Pri-
vate Enterprise device pulled through, for
the time, at all events.
Some of these days Henry Chinn —the
man whom the Fisher Government ap-
pointed as a construction boss on the trans-
continental railway, and whom the Cook
Government sacked —may bring an action
for damages against his friends. Before he
was appointed one of his disastrous sup-
porters wrote recommending him on the
ground that he had greatly helped the Labor
party in Westralia at the election which
raised Fisher and co. to office. If that sup-
porter had been drowned in the bath-tub
while an infant his fatuous document
wouldn’t have been in evidence to furnish
excuse for the yarn that Chinn’s appoint-
ment was not a matter of merit, but a mere
political job. So Chinn was discharged by
the Liberals as a mere political appointee
or jobee. The Senate, where Labor has a
majority, has appointed an almost-whollv
Labor Commission to consider his case. And
the chairman. Senator de Largie, has de-
clared <at least so it is reported) that “no
more scandalous piece of persecution has
ever taken place” than the Cook party’s at-
titude towards Chinn. After that, even if
de Largie and his friends bring in a verdict
on good evidence that Chinn is a first-class
engineer and deserved his billet on his quali-
fications—well. the verdict will carry tmry
little more weight than a scrap of waste
paner. So another disastrous friend has
rallied round Chinn.
“B.” : Three postal offi-
cials committed sui ode
in N. S. Wales dur-
ing August, and at
each inouest the amount
of overtime the harassed
officers were called upon
to perform was put in as
evidence. Now the new
Federal Government has
framed a. regulation to
save the Postal Depart-
ment’s face in case of
trouble. Every officer
has to sign the time-
books at the hour he
oupht to go off duty,
and not when he actually
goes off. This will pre-
vent the relatives of
future suicides from call-
ing the time-books in evi-
dence. But whether it
will reduce the brain fag that drives men to
fleeiug into the Beyond is not so positive.
In fact, it is almost half doubtful.
It is mentioned in the daily papers that
the N.S.W. Minister for Labor and Industry
(ex-Premier McGowen) “is still hopeful that
there will be an amicable reference of the
Broken Hill dispute to a Wages Board.”
It is a curious situation. There was an
“amicable reference to a Wages Board,” and
wages were fixed. The employers offer to
add something to these amicably-fixed, wages.
Yet there is a strike, which —seeing
that N.S.W. has a Labor Government, and
Broken Hill is a Labor constituency, and is
bossed by a Labor municipal council, and
that the Wages Board against which the
strike is directed, is a Labor institution —is
somewhat dispiriting. Nobody tries to en-
force the law. but ex-Premier McGowen
hopes amicability will presently rage all over
the place.

notice any reliable evidence they could find of a trust
or combine working to the detriment of the people.
If they found any breach of law, whether he agreed
with that law or not, he would prosecute, etc., etc.
THE NEW COP (to the owner of the orchard):
“ An' now I hopes toe'll be heavin' less av
y'r whinin’s and complainin's agin thim
poor divils av trusts; because I'm the man
t' do me duly, I am, an’ ye've only got
t' call me in time t" catch 'em in the act—
an ’ even if they’re me own brothers I'[?]
beat 'em back t' the best phwat's in me
power — an' I can't do more n that, can I?"
“Sufficient unto the day,” said N.S.W.
State Treasurer Cann when asked how the
great deficit was likely to get on next year.
“Sufficient unto the day.” Or, as that other
great shortage man, Louis XV., is said to
have put it: “After me the deluge.” He is
also alleged to have remarked : “Things will
last my time.”
Sydney Evening News (27/8/T3) dis-
interred one Todman, an* English tourist,
who almost wept over the grievances of the
Jap:—
The news that the Californian Government had
passed a law which prohibited Japanese from own-
ing land in that State inflamed the minds of all
classes in Japan. The insult was too much for the
high-spirited sons of Nippon, and they clamored for
war. Ministers pleaded with the populace to In-
patient, but that only added fuel to the flame, and
their resignations—in fact, the resignations of the
whole Government—were demanded. The people
cried for redress, and I can assure you I fully ex-
pected a declaration of war. To appreciate the situ-
tion one wants to know a little of the Japanese.
They are a proud people, sensitive to a degree where
their honor is involved, and touchy when the ques-
tion of color is raised.
Banned from Australia and Maoriland, and now
prohibited from owning land in California, which i-
only another way of expelling them, it is little
wonder that they say, “Must we be forever
sojourners in other lands, must we live as foreigners,
everywhere except where we have conquered? If so,
let us try to conquer now’. Disaster in war is not
worse than insult when pursuing a peaceful course.”
That is the spirit of Japan, and soon it will be the
guiding light of the whole East, for China is waking
from her opium sleep.
Everywhere I went, both in China and Japan, a
feeling of resentment was evident against America
and Australia, produced, I was told by an English-
man resident there, by the anti-alien enactments of
both countries. How long those laws will keep out
the yellow races depends, I suppose, upon the ability
of the people who made them to enforce them, and
from what I learned during my stay in Japan the
people are anxious to test the matter now, especially
as far as America is concerned.
It is heroic snuffle on behalf of a coldly
exclusive race which has, for many years,
refused to allow the white man to own land,
and has steadily shoved him out of bank-
ing, mining, shipowning and general com-
merce, till at last there are less than 7000
whites —British, French, German, Yankee,
Swiss, Spanish, Portuguese and all others —
in the Jap. country. At the last census
there were only 1665 Yankees in Japan, so
vigorously had the Yankee population been
hunted out. Yet in two years (1910 and
1911) nearly 10,000 Japanese moved into
Y'ankee territory, and complained bitterly be-
cause the red carpet wasn’t laid down on the
wharf when they arrived. At the date of
last census there were more Japanese in
Australia alone than there were British sub-
jects of all sorts in the Chrysanthemum
Land, and the brown man was still dissatis-
fied. This clamor of the ill-used Jap grows
wearisome. And when the ill-informed tour-
ist gets hold of it. and the cheap and ill'
informed paper allows the tourist to gam
publicity for what he thinks are his views,
an exhausted feeling supervenes.
THE DRONE AND THE DOER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fact is that Labor politicians.... want Parlia-
ment to consist only of those who “live on the
game,” which means, of course, only of Labor
members. — Aruus (25/8/Y3.)
The loafing Labor member sat
Around, and talked of this and that,
And cheered, and bounced upon the springs
That held him up.... He did these things
Not as a hobby, nor a fad —
It was the only job he had!
He earned his three squares, and his bed
Serving his country thus, he said.
Twelve quid a week the blighter g0t....
A lucky loafer, was he not ?
Now here, now there —but mostly there —-
With hurried fingers in his hair.
Important papers in each fist,
An agile anti-Socialist,
The Liberal member simply flew,
He had so much hard toil to do.
A pea on a hot plate, he went
On his own gilt-edged business bent.
Twelve quid a week this hustler got. . . .
A proper patriot, was he not ?
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Paris Nesbit. K.C., talks of a crusade
against enclosed-ground coursing in Adelaide. The
secretary of the Plumpton cordially invited him -to
come arid see how innocent the sport is, and what a
sporting chance the hare has of escape; to which he
replied that getting away for the day, only to be
killed next week, is not “escape,” and that he was
sending two men to collect evidence, the spectacle
being too painful for himself. Next, Nesbit appealed
to the S.P.C.A. to obtain an injunction against the
meeting; but the lady secretary somewhat acidly
replied that he himself, convinced of the iniquity
of the thing, was the very best person to obtain it,
and so really suppress the cruelty. The K.C. retorted
that it is too big and expensive a fight for his pri-
vate purse; so he sent the whole correspondence to
the papers, and there the matter is side-tracked.
The only Vic. League match that could
interest spectators drew over 20,000 of them to Vic-
toria Park on Saturday, in spite of driving rain
storms. ’Wood and South were to decide as to the
occupancy of the second and third places. The game
would have been more attractive had the ground been
less slippery, the ball less- greasy and the umpire
sufficiently intelligent to keep a grip of things. As
it was, the players lost all confidence in him, flouted
and bluffed him, and in the later stages played much
as they thought fit. This writer hates to berate an
official” who usually gets more curses and fewer pence
than he deserves, but there are occasions when it is
obvious he has mistaken his vocation, and this was
one of them. The Magpies took the lead at the
start, but lost it when the Red-and-Whites set going
a series of concerted rushes, and at one stage were
three goals in arrear. In the last quarter, however,
by organised, determined play, they crept up to
within two points of their opponents. Then the
bell rang, with the board showing: South, 56 points;
’Wood, 54. The position now is that South will play
St. Kilda, and ’Roy will collide with ’Wood, the
winners playing off ; and while this writer wouldn’t
care to back any one of the four and throw a goal
in, he opines that, given fine weather, ’Wood should
get there, while if undue moisture prevails, South
should about do it. In the other games ’Roy passed
Carlton out finally by 55 to 32. Geelong donkey-
licked St. Kilda with 108 to 40. Essendon des-
patched Melbourne with 52 to 42. Richmond anni-
hilated ’Varsity with 101 to 59. Just here it is
pertinent to reflect that though these Students have
won only one game in two seasons, they would be a
great team if they would practise and play concert-
edly. They have a champion centre, a tip-top
follower, many good place men and a star for-
ward in young Park. This lad, though assisted by
such poor organisation, has made and utilised so
many opportunities that he has this season kicked
four more goals than either ’Roy’s or St. Hilda’s
champion forward and twice as many as the best
’Woodman or Southerner —yet his team couldn’t win
a match. System beats individual brilliance every
time.
The second Vic. Ass. semi-final —North
v. Brunswick —on the East Melbourne ground proved
a disappointment to most of the 17,000 who “Darted ’
£367 to look at it. Up to five minutes after half-time
it was fast, close and exciting, with North three goals
eight behinds, ’Wick four goals two behinds—26 points
all. But thereafter, though all the 32 players
seemed to be moving about more or less hurriedlv,
the scoring was done by the Northern men only, the
ultimate tally being North, 64 points; ’Wick, the
same old 26” What the Brickies meant by it or
what they thought of themselves Heaven knows, but
their barraekers held fixed and freely-expressed
opinions on the subject, though the language in
which they vented them wouldn’t pass even in the
corroboree presided over by Alligator Johnson. The
match ended ’Wick’s ’l3 career, and left ’Scray and
North to scramble for the flag and the glory, ’Scray
having a second chance if it is defeated the first
time.
The Vic. Junior Ass. also luxuriated in a
well-attended semi-final—’Town Juniors v. Yarra-
ville —which was so vigorous that several players
were laid out, one having to retire with a damaged
thigh and another with a broken leg. Yarraville
ultimately got on top with 35 points to 30. The great
weakness Of these juniors is an abiding tendency to
leave their places and keep “on the ball” all the
time. This is not only tiring, but dangerous, because
when 30 lusty youths are scrimmaging about
close-packed, someone is pretty sure to get hurt.
Besides, that sort of play is neither spectacular nor
successful.
Saturday saw the absolute final end of
Union Rugby in Sydney for this season. South
Sydney secured the premiership of the B division
by blotting out Western Suburbs (13 to six), and
thus maintaining its lead of four points in the
Competition over North Sydney. This made a final
unnecessary, as it was not possible to wipe out the
margin in” one match. So the struggle ended with
South Sydney, 18; North Sydnev, 14; Manly, 12;
Balmain, 10; Western Suburbs eight; and St.
George, six. In Saturday’s games Balmain threw
St. George into the dust-box and shut the lid. the
figures being 33 to three. North Sydney overthrew
Manly by 10 points to six. #
Eastern Suburbs, Newtown, Balmain and
Western Suburbs were the first to fall in the sudden-
death competition for the Rugby League City Cup.
On Saturday last the remaining four fell upon one
another, and two more were killed off. North Syd-
ney rushed the bones of South Sydney to the ceme-
tery by 19 points to 10, and • Glebe just managed
to scramble out on top of Annandale with 11 points
to 10. The struggle between the two Sydneys was
very remarkable. The wind was strong, and South,
by ballooning the ball, turned it to the very best
account. North struggled hard, and put up a splen-
did defence, but the Southern score was
10 to nil. The case seemed hopeless, and
the patient was being measured for his
coffin, but North struggled on, and at half-time it had
put up five points. Then came its turn with the
wind, which was still strong. Against that breeze
and the forwards of the North, the Southern team
made no headway, while the Northmen put on 14
points, and so won out by 19 to 10. The Glebe-
Annandale struggle was very grim, being apparently
evenly balanced. They rocked and swayed, and
both'sides scored a try, but Glebe managed to add
the extras while Annandale failed, and so Glebe ended
the first half five to three. After that fortune oscil-
lated, and. when the whistle blew*. Glebe happened
to be leading by 11 to 10. Had the bugle gone
a minute or so later, it might have been the other-
wav—a feather would have turned the balance.
Next Saturday sees the final. After that John
Brown’s body will be mouldering in the dust.
The N.S.W. Football Leaguers got
Queensland down again in the return match, and
once more fell upon it, but on this occasion Queens-
land put up a harder kick', and was defeated by
only 21 to IT —a thing that might happen to the
best team on earth, which Queensland isn’t. The
great Northern State won the toss, but there did not
seem much to choose between the ends. However,
it astonished the natives by scoring almost immedi-
ately after the kick off, and added the extras, too.
N.S.W. let loose a fine passing charge with a view
to wiping things out, but although the try was
pressed home the kick at goal failed. Queensland
then added a couple with a penalty goal which is
declared by the experts to have been something
special. N.S.W. came away with another try, but
again failing to convert, Queensland still led by
seven to six. This shattering fact roused the
Waters. to renewed efforts, and they made the pace
so hot the Queensland combination started to go to
pieces under the strain. By half-time N. S. Wales
had made the score 16 to nine. But apparently that
burst of energy had left its effect behind it, and
in the second half the Ma State team failed to get
properly going. Queensland attacked, and, although
the defence was stubborn, it was broken up and
Queensland added eight points. But in the mean-
time Ma’s men had sneaked five, and so were still
on top. The team was to have played at Ipswich,
but owing to casualties, contusions and other damages
this was impossible. The victors made straight for
the Harbor and beauty, and arrived safely there on
Tuesday morning.
The Australian Leaguers put on a rather
novel game on Saturday last. The Native Born
Cornstalks met a team made up of Australians born
in other States. It was a great struggle, and in the
end the Native Born Cornstalks beat the other Native
Born with a margin of four points—32 to 28. The
scribes are advocating that it be made an annual
event, as it aroused a great deal of interest, and
attracted a good crowd. The crowd seems to have
got its money’s worth, as the game was said to have
been absolutely the finest played during the season.
It was Australia Day on the lioofball
fields of Maoriland on Saturday. The Rugby League
team walked into Auckland and ran through it with
27 points to two. Auckland is the League citadel
in the Rickettv Isles, and as Canterbury was route'!
by 45 to five," and Taranaki smashed by 16 to five,
the country is naked and defenceless before the in-
vaders. On the same day the N.S.W. Union Rugby
crowd descended on Taranaki, which holds the Union
championship of Massey’s Land, and carries the Ran-
furly Shield on its ample shoulders which are made
broad by leaning against the cow. A great struggle
ensued, but the Australians clawed their way to the
top by 11 points to nine. The same N.S.W. team
had previously downed Auckland Union by 15 points
to 13.
Admirers and spurners of racing, lovers
and scorners of lucre got a bomb when they read
in their Parliamentary news the other morning that
there are 484 racecourses in the Ma State. No
wonder Patrick Mcßenjamin waxes fat. What a
wealth of revenue lies in those 484 racecourses when
the Government gets sufficient sense to pass a Totali-
sator Bill! *
The rare profits in the game! Ex-Aus-
tralian trainer Wootton, in his English action against
Sevier, acknowledged having bought an estate in
Australia for £40,000, and said he had £2OOO a
year from Australian property. His son Frank, the
jockey, aged 19, had £31,000 invested in Australia.
“Amazing!” exclaimed Judge Darling. “Amazing!”
say all of us.
N.S.W. Professional Rowers’ League is
becoming more a reality than a dream. There is
no sport in which a strong, controlling hand can do
more good, at the present time, than rowing.
There is quite an army of young rowers coming along,
and the League has a golden opportunity to do a
service for itself and them.
On the Homebush-Granville Road (Syd-
ney), on Saturday, the landscape was obliterated
by a dust storm.” A cloud of solid earth, thick
enough to grow radishes in, obscured the landscape,
and through the murk and muck of it toiled the first
15 cyclists of the Ma State. They were seeking the
Individual Road Champion. They found him at the
10-mile, but didn’t know it. So they chased him the
other 30 miles, when he was revealed unto them as
E. W. Pedersen, of the Petersham Club, strangely
enough. C. M. Cutliffe, of the Redfern Club, and
W. Wylie, of the Petersham Club, and Brook, of the
Redfern Club, were the placed men in that order.
Pedersen takes the Dunlop Gold Medal and the Title,
while Cutliffe annexes the Toohey Gold Medal.
Pedersen’s time was 2hr. lmin. 40sec. for the 40
miles, and, considering the conditions, it was almost
miraculous.
The Motor Cycle Club of South Aus-
tralia announces that its proposed Reliability Test
run from Adelaide to Melbourne is fixed to start on
Monday, December 29. It will be spread over five
days, of which the second will be the toughest, when
the Coorong Desert will have to be navigated at 15
miles an hour as far as Kingston, a distance of 92
miles. After that, the pace rushes up to 20 miles
an hour to Robe, making a total run of 118 f miles
for the day. The total distance from capital to
capital is about 557 J miles. This will be the first
occasion that a motor contest of any description has
been held over this inter-State route, and the cross-
ing of the Coorong Desert gives it some of the snap
of a great adventure.
Looks as though the already struggling
game of cricket is to be further harassed in its pur-
suit of the nimble public bob. A body or shindykit
or something is now negotiating with the N.S.W.
Baseball Association to induce it to join in a ven-
ture to. run a summer division. The arrival of a
Yankee team here at the end of the coming summer
is giving the idea a boost, and as the Association
is endeavoring to get control of some enclosed
grounds it looks as though most serious business is
contemplated.
The resurrection of Jessop and the dis-
covery of young Tennyson has probably “bucked up”
English cricket a bit; but, at the beginning of
July, the scribes were l moaning that if an English
XI were wanted this season, there would not be a
single “Gentleman” in it. They also remarked,
what is about 384 times more important, that Eng-
land’s star professionals are mostly of the mature
age of 35 or over —-some of them a good deal over—
and the outlook for coming seasons is therefore
blue.
The much deferred Maoriland cricket in-
vasion of Australia will happen this coming season.
The first attack will be at Sydney, on December 9,
and matches against N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria
and South Australia will be sandwiched between
other events.

“Mate”: The two youthful Australians
who have been playing chess for £5O a side and
the championship of Australia are as unlike the
alleged comic paper’s idea of chess players as they
well could be. V'iner, who" Won, is a typical up-
country Australian, 30 years of age 6ft. high,
unassuming, unemotional, stolid and hard-faced. Crack-
anthorp, the challenger, is a city bank-clerk, small,
spare and a bundle of nerves to look at. He is also
in early manhood. The result proved that sto.idity
is superior to nerves in a chess match of any length.
After standing up well to his opponent at first,
Crackanthorp dropped his bundle, and Viner won
by seven games to one.
Let us neioice ! I ! At last the ’Tienley-
-011-Parramatta Regatta” has been set down, for
better or worse, for October 11. The venture
has a strong whiff of society about it, and these
high society ventures have never been too successful
in Svdnev. The happenings at the opening of the
famous Hampden Riding, Driving and Cycling Club,
“an organisation to bring the nicest people together
in the nicest way,” many years ago, caused the
blood of high society to run cold. That was the
occasion when the Club let itself go at Centennial
Park, and an awfully-arrayed Chinese merchant
dashed down the incline, on a bike, with his pigtail
waving in the wind, between the late Sydney Burde-
kin and a tony A.D.C. The incident killed the
Hampden Riding, Driving and Wheeling Club, or
whatever it was, stone dead as a power in the land.
The London Daily Mail’s offer of £5OOO
to the British > aviator who could fly round* Great
Britain and Ireland on a British-built hydro-aero-
plane tempted only two entrants. Owing to engine
troubles one of these withdrew, and the other, an
Australian named Hawker, started at the beginning
of last week. After many adventures and narrow
escapes, he finally came to grief over Lough Shinny,
15 miles from Dublin. The machine fell 100 ft.
through a side slip, and although Hawker escaped
unhurt his mechanician, Kauper, sustained serious
injuries to his head and had an arm broken. The
coastguard rescued the men. It was a wonderful
flight, the aeronaut having sailed right up the east
coast of Britain, crossed Scotland over the Cale-
donian Canal, and traversed a large section of the
Irish coast before the disaster happened. He had
covered 1043 miles in 21 hours, and the Daily Mail
awarded him £lOOO of the prize he failed to win.
.Tust at present great glory is his. The same paper
is offering another huge prize for a trans-Atlantic
flight—an event for which there will probably be
even fewer entrants. Equally, if not more, daring
was the recent flight by four Farman biplanes,
mounted by French officers, across 500 miles of the
Sahara. They started from an aeroplane post on the
edge of the desert at Biskra (Algeria), and flew to
Touggourt, a military station in an oasis of the same
name. The whole four biplanes made the outward
trip safely, and next day returned without mishap.
The flight of 1000 miles in two days is not extra-
ordinary, but it was a daring business when the coun-
try over which it was made is considered. It is a
curious thing, but the average inhabitant of the
French territories in Northern Africa sees far
more aeroplane flights than does the average Aus-
tralian. Every French military post has several
machines in almost constant use. The desert air
seems to be favorable for aerial navigation. Aus-
tralia’s aerial backwardness is pathetic.
With his vaccination marks protected by
an elastic stocking, Australian aboriginal Jerry
Jerome met Jack Cordell in the Brisbane Stadium on
Saturday night, and outed him in exactly 2min.
35sec. ‘And it took the big fellow just about the
same space of time to recover his equilibrium.
Although so brief, it was a hot fight while it lasted.
Cordell towered over the elusive black, who opened
proceedings with a right pat on the cheek and a
piston-like wallop in the stomach. Cordell got in
close, and let fly a broadside of short body blows
which caused the Australian to gasp and clinch very
hard. But finding he could not imprison Cordell s
arms, Jeremiah jumped away, and as he went he
assumed a worried expression. Cordell followed up
and met a terrific swing on the jaw, and two
more in the stomach. Then he crashed on
and got home a left and right. As he was backing
away Jerry landed a left on the jaw. Cordell went
down with a bang, and stayed there, and the next
thing was the spectacle of the black citizen carrying
the fallen gladiator to his corner.
Next Saturday night will see Jeremiah
Jerome, of Australia, back in Sydney Stadium, up
against the warlike Harry Mansfield; and an inter-
esting scrap should follow. But the real titbit
comes on a week later, when the much-talked-of
ba.ue between Rat Bradley and Dave Smith will rage
like the Wowser in his fury. When he met Jim
Sullivan, Bradley scaled lOst. 131 b., but in this riot
he expects to heave about list. lib. of meat and
muscle around. Smith has promised to get as close
to list. 41b. as he can without weakening himself,
so as to make .the scrap betwen a middle and light
heavyweight as fair as possible. It should be a
hefty battle, for Smith is one of our best boxers,
and Bradley has knocked out every man he has met
in Australia. He is generally regarded as unortho-
dox —why, it is hard to say. He has the soundest
defence this writer has ever gazed upon, and his
footwork is perfect. If there is one thing more
than another that marks the scientific fighter, it is
his ability to use his feet. Let anyone mark how
Bradley uses his feet, and then say if he is unscien-
tific. Truly, he is unusual, in that he never worries
about scoring points, and goes straight in to end
things by a knock-out blow. And, as said before,
every time he has fought in this country he has
achieved his purpose and climbed to the pinnacle
of his ambition. So Smith is up against the propo-
sition of his life. „
Nowadays, immediately a boxer attains to
fame he feels called upon to write a book and let the
world know exactly how he does it. Tahmmy Burns
started it, and it has been going on ever since. Even
Jack Johnson arose to announce that he had penned
and inked a little thing of his own. In fact it is
said that Johnson has perpetrated two books, and
is threatening to write a third, but not in connection
with boxing. The latest arrival is “My Methods on
Boxing as a Fine Art,” by Georges Carpentier, cham-
pion of Europe.; It has been done into English by
F. Hurdman-Lucas, and is published at the offices of
Boxing, by Ewart, Seymour and Co., of London. A
number of portraits show Carpentier in various poses
in everyday dress, and represent him as being more
like a beauty-actor than a pug. However, Carpentier
knows what ‘he is writing about, and illustrates his.
own special wallops with great clearness, so that his’
aspiring young rival may read, mark, learn and out-
wardly punch the same. It is a fool’s game, as Burns
found out. Johnson read his book and carefully
arranged never to let little Tahmmy fight the sort of
scrap he had described with such exquisite lucidity.
In fact, the book gave Burns away, and he might as
well have struck Johnson with a family Bible as tried
the old carefully-expounded game on him.
Georges Carpentier throws some light on
ia dark subject. Everybody has noticed that the bulk
of the Yankee fighters who have raided these shores
have been hard hitters. Georges says it is due to the
fact that they use the ball only for show, and really
practise punching on human opponents who are
padded about the body and masked with face protec-
tors so that the man who is learning to pound can
let himself go. It is the first time the writer has
seen the statement, and if it is correct it is a
wonder that it is not more generally known.
The Australian career of Petty-Officer
Curran came to a sudden end last week, as far as
the Baker circuit is concerned. “Nutty” went down
to Melbourne to entertain the brethren by bashing
Doran, with Owen Moran as referee. But when he
got into the ring he either wouldn’t or couldn’t fight,
and apparently tried hard for a foul. Referee Mo
warned him some half-dozen times, but
apparently dreaming with one fixed idea in his mi?
So somewhere about half-way through the seonnii
round referee Moran woke him up and told him h
was disqualified. Reg Baker was on his wav ha t
from Adelaide, and on his arrival at Melbourne h
held an inquiry. He heard what “Nutty” had t
say, took evidence from his manager and inter
viewed the pressmen who were seated round th
ring, and then coming on to Sydney he got Moran's
report. The various accounts were unanimously
against Curran, so Baker cancelled his contract aid
disqualified him for all eternity as far as his Stadium*
are concerned. it was severe, but it enhances the
reputation of the Baker management for clean
business.
Remembering the magnificent showing
Waldemar Holberg had put up against Hughie
Mehegan, an enormous crowd swarmed into the great
Sydney Stadium to see the gallant Dane battle with
Herbert McCoy, of Melbourne, easily Australia’s fore-
most lightweight. McCoy is a splendidly plucky
fighter; McCoy is easily Australia’s foremost ring
general; McCoy is a scientific boxer, a man capable
of taking more than the average amount of punish-
ment and yet keeping his head; a brainy warrior
who can snatch victory from the very jaws of dis-
aster. And Herbert McCoy is the most unfair fighter
in his class in Australia to-day, so far as this writer
has seen. Curiously enough, it is when least hard-
pressed that he is foulest. If he were at the last
gasp there might be some excuse for it. But there
is none. It is when he is on top of the wave of
success that Herbert McCoy puts both his front feet
in the trough, and, to all appearance, deliberately
hangs on with one hand and uppercuts with the
other. On at least three occasions in his fight with
Waldemar Holberg, the Melbournian apparently-
tried to uppercut the visitor with his naked
forearm instead of his glove. If he persists in his
present practices, it is only a question of time til!
the Stadium management does with him as it did
with “Nutty” Curran, and disqualifies him for foul
fighting.
The fight, though stubbornly contested, was tedi-
ous. It was’ hard slogging all the time ; and a genuine
strenuous go from start to finish, in which neither of
the principals felt any ennui. Holberg’s crowning
disadvantage is the brevity of his reach. He coun-
ters every blow and follows up with the other fist;
and he is as quick as lightning in his returns. But
the shortness of his reach brings all his splendid
quickness to nought unless he can duck away from
the other’s lead. The longer arm 19 times out of
20 makes impact first, a fact that did Holberg’s
mouth and nose no good. He hurled himself into
the fray with magnificent recklessness, but the cool-
headed McCoy avoided his splendid rushes and fol-
lowed up with counter attacks that won him many
points. Yet there were occasions when Holberg got
in a succession of wallops which caused McCoy to
suddenly look 90 years older. McCoy’s face takes
on a strangely wizened expression when he stops a
few that hurt, and he looks as though the sins of the
world had fallen on him with a crash. The features
seem to suddenly crinkle, and the whole countenance
assumes the aspect of an over-fried egg, out of which
the eyes stare, and in the midst of which the lips
move "as if they were pronouncing an incantation.
Altogether, it is an unpleasant sight when McCoy
stops what is coming to him. Somewhere about half-
way through he assumed his wild aspect, and with
it so annoyed the Dane that he didn’t dare to go in
close to the unusual-looking object and finish it. The
bell brought relief, and after his minute’s respite it
was the same cheerful, warlike McCoy that came
out of the corner and mixed things. McCoy’s stamina
is splendid. As the battle raged on it became more
and more obvious that Holberg’s on}y chance of win-
ning was a knock-out; and he went for it delibef-
ately, regardless of punishment. But McCoy was
too good a ring general for that. Then Holberg s
right seemed to go, and he grew very chary of using
it for offence. Now his right had been his chief
offensive battery. But still he bored in bravely, and
swung his left about in a way that made anything
seem possible; sticking it straight out in front and
hurling himself bodily at the foe. One mighty swing
and uppercut just missed McCoy, who rushed m and
smote with left and right, and then adroitly carried
his diaphragm out of the line of a vicious stab.
McCoy’s footwork was certainly excellent. When
the last gong sounded, referee Scott placed his hand
on McCoy’s bright head, and for once there was only
approval from the massed faces around the roped
square.

'Dol": The picturesque Italian of whom
we read and hear has not yet appeared in
'\ estralia. though his countrymen are there
h> the swarm. Recently I had to inspect
six goldfield Italian-run hotels, where the
foreigners congregate and live much as
Chinamen exist in their quarters in Sydney
and Melbourne. Dancing and singing' were
in progress at each place. In fact, the gold-
field Italians seem to be always dancing and
singing when they are not asleep or at work
or jabbing each other with knives. The
“music” came from an accordeon or a con-
certina. There was a piano at each place,
but it stood idle. Though the dancing was
graceful, the performers were never clean in
person. The gentleman always took his coat
off. before beginning, and put it on at the
finish, just as he would after some heavy
work. As to the singing, anything more
discordant could hardly be conceived of. Not
a single solo was given ; everyone joined in.
shouting and roaring, and struggling to give
an imitation of 14 sozzled bulls at the tail-
end of a smoke concert.

One of the most conspicuous features of modern social life is the tendency to avoid the cares and
annoyances of housekeeping by adopting life in flats. Though much in vogue in the older countries of
the world it is only comparatively recently that this habit has developed in Australia, and, to cope with
the demand, mansions of flats are gradually becoming features of our city and suburban environment.
The essential element of success in establishing mansion flats consists in selecting the right suburb
and locality, and in erecting structures that shall be commanding in position, artistic in design, and
commodious and convenient in their internal arrangements.
No suburb of Melbourne can surpass St. Kilda as regards qualifications for residential purposes. It
is needless to dwell unon the charm of situation, bold views and delightful approach from the City that
have so long made it "the favorite summer suburban resort of Victoria.
The Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring from the vendor, E. Branfill Harrison, all
his right, title and interest in and -“"tain op'-mns which he holds over the property hereinafcer
described.

The corner of the property, at the junction of Acland and Barkly streets, at present occupied by a
large, brick rink, it is proposed to convert into twelve Shops, and a large Garage.
The Garage at the corner, it is certain, would fulfil a long-felt want. One need only view the many
hundreds of motor-cars that pass and repass in the vicinity daily to realise what a boon a garage, so
close to the Esplanade, would be to motorists and the residents of these Mansions.
Although these buildings would revolutionise the traffic at this corner, yet, such is the fine growth
of the trees on the property that they are and will be completely concealed from the Mansions, and would
not in any way impair their privacy.
On the completion of the first mansion, and its successful launching, it is proposed to at once erect
a second, similar one, to be followed by three more in succession, the complete proposition beina a set ot
five mansions of simi’ar design and appointments, standing in common grounds, artistically laid out in
lawns, shrubberies and flow’er beds, and forming one homogeneous whole.
It will thus be seen that the Company is acquiring a self-contained block, which it is proposed to
convert into a populous residential and business area, while still retaining the beauty and privacy ot
the Central Mansions.
The Capital subscribed, £32,000, will amply suffice for the purchase of the freeholds, less a smad
amount to remain out on mortgage, and for the building and furnishing of the first mansion, while >_
reserve Capital, £13,000, will be available for the commencement of the second, the question of raising
the extra Capital for the completion of the whole scheme by the issue of debentures, by increase w
Capital, or by mortgage, being left for subsequent decision by the shareholders.
In these estates the Company has ample security for the Capital invested, in valuable freeholds of
rising value, and buildings that fulfil a want, and will be in demand. Shops in that neighborhood ar
needed, and it is only necessary to note the local demand for same to realise that they will let ream,
and command good rentals.
The returns from the letting of the flats, the dining-room, the catering, the shops, garage, etc., wiH.
on a close estimate, return app. 15%, after due deductions for all contingencies.
A large proportion of the shares have already been applied for, and it is anticipated that the whol
will be readily taken up.

“The Old Soldier” : There is some quite
unexpected plain speaking in the Contem-
porary Review about the alleged gift by
the Malay States to the British Government
of a big warship at a cost of £2,250,000.
Even if the Malay rulers had given this sum out
of their own private resources, it has been shown
that their position is one of such dependence upon
their British advisers, and upon their British nomi-
nated Councils, that the gift must altogether lack
spontaneity. But not even this semblance of spon-
taneity is to be found here.
The gift was engineered by the Chief Sec-
retary, Brockman, and the High Commis-
sioner, who got the Raja of Perak to make
the necessary motion in the Federal Council
with the High Commissioner in the chair.
There is no doubt that the heavy strain of
providing money for constantly increasing
armaments has forced the British Govern-
ment to this scheme, but even now the cost
of the maintenance of the ship has not been
put on the Estimates, and nothing has been
provided for its replacement when wrecked,
worn out or obsolete. The Conteai porary
finds a parallel to the Malay battleship :
It was under the stress of a somewhat similar
financial pressure that the Government of 1765 was
induced to pass the famous Stamp Act, which was
the cause of the revolt of the American colonies.
That is a nice, cheerful sort of suggestion,
though the Contemporary is careful to add
that it does not suggest that the Malay
States may revolt, as the Americans did.
But it is such an easy way of getting out of
a stringent financial situation that the job
is pretty sure to be repeated sooner or later
with other dependencies. The Malay States
are having a boom just now, and the new
ship will be paid for out of a surplus which
ought to have been spent on roads, educa-
tion and sanitation. Meantime the death-
rate among plantation coolies has doubled
in three years, and planters are resisting
the enforcement of sanitary regulations.
—-
“Bobstay” : Much to the surprise of those
interested and to the dismay of the Cook
Ministry, the Federal Navigation Act has
received the royal assent,, and will become
law as soon as the flabbergasted Cabinet has
courage to issue the necessary proclamation.
Sydney Mourning Herald, in the first
spasm of amazement, called loudly on Cook
to refuse to proclaim the Act, on the ground
that he didn’t pass it, but he is hardly so
fresh a bird as that. There are bushels of
opportunities for delay, but, sooner or later,
the mark has to be toed. The chief point of
interest raised by the new legislation is the
definition of coastal trade in the clause
framed to prevent foreign and British vessels
carrying inter-State passengers and cargo,
unless their owners observe all the condi-
tions to which Australian-owned vessels are
liable. This will hit the P. and O. and
other piebald cos. hard. Cos. like the Orient
will also be hampered, though to a
less degree, and it is threatened that the
British lines will retaliate by refusing to
tender for the mail contracts. If* this is
done, Australians will have to mess around
and do the job in a hopeless way themselves
with the aid of State ships or the insignifi-
cant cockle-shells, none of which run m ,„i
over 10,000 tons, belonging to the Thu
Steam Ship Co the A.U.S.N. or some BU S
deplorably helpless concern. It shows vo‘
the trouble a white nation makes for itseli
when it has the audacity to interfere with
Chow- and Lascar-wage competition in it,
coast-wise trade. Still it is pleasing to re
member that Australian-owned ships ocea"
sionally carried the mails to England in th
old days, and that ships both owned ami
built in Australia have carried mails m
China. They used to build ships even at
places like Jervis Bay and Eden and the
Northern Ilivers before the Congestion dis*
ease got N.S.W. down, and even after the
Congestion disease got N.S.W down Sydney
was a big shin-building centre till the bor
rowing and importing disease fell on this
country’s chest and made a finish. And
Hobart —think of it I—once rivalled Glasgow
as a shipbuilding centre, but the importing
and foreign-borrowing disease got it down
also, and it died through not putting a ",()
per cent, duty on imported ships. \Vp|l
this country may get over all these diseases!
From “The Pariah”: —
The easy way to explain how the trouble in China
is going to end is to say that out of the dragon's
labor will come a new dynasty—that of Yuan. But
the end will not be so simple. In 1908, after al4
weeks’ trip in the interior of China—my last and
longest in that part of the great country—l ven-
tured in a Bulletin par. to suggest that 'the king-
dom would eventually split up into three parts
according to racial, climatic, linguistic and (less
markedly) religious differences; and the more I
watch the development of the Chinese situation the
more inevitable this seems to be. In the South there
is a degenerate mixed population, in which Chinese
Filipino, Arab, Parsee, the undefined Hokka and
other elements are injudiciously jumbled. When a
foreigner speaks to a Middle Chinese of the Asiatic
he has met in the Hongkong-Canton area, he re-
ceives an apologetic but firm explanation: “B’long
Cantonese. Man that side no b’long pukka Chinese. 1 '
These southerners are entirely cordial in their hatred
of their more northern, purer-blooded cousins, from
whom they differ markedly in stature and feature.
Shanghai and Canton have little in common, even
their languages being mutually unintelligible. The
same is true of the Middle and the North, only the
feeling of hostility is much more intense, for the
North is regarded as the home of the Manchu
ciespoiler.
The South, having for centuries been accustomed
to an order of affairs in which brigandage and piracy
were the profitable recreations, will be a sort of hatin-
American State, with a cheerful scrap-and-come-again
policy, and much sudden death for its rulers. The
Middle, which embraces all the best of the race, will
become a powerful merchant-State, and its patriot-
ism will, no doubt, lead it along the thorny path
of a civilisation backed up by militarism. It will
certainly have a republican form of government. The
North, insignificant in area, will for a time probably
maintain the worst traditions and intrigues of Asiatic
courts. Here a diplomatic battle between Japan and
Russia will be fought, and the country will probably
be again - the theatre of war, when a much wiser
and better prepared Bear will, swift and sharp, des-
cend upon a certain Monkey whose head is very
swelled—and Russia will secure its long-sought-for
access to the sea.
For manv years yet China will provide the news-
papers with sensational incidents. Which leads me
to remark that some steps should be taken to estab-
lish direct wireless communication with “the East.”
The Sydney Telegraph casuallv remarked some
months ago that the Thursday Island plant could
speak with Hongkong. Then why not give our
operators the useful exercise?

THE KING OF ALL
TALKING MACHINES.
WE introduce this splendid musi-
cal instrument to the Public
with every confidence, know-
ing that it is the finest sound-reproduc-
ing instrument that has» been made.
Handsome in appearance, solidly and
faithfully. built of carefully selected
material, it is beautifully finished, and
will be aii admired addition to any
home, no matter how well furnished.-
The Cabinet or concealed horn style
is undoubtedly the instrument of the
future for home entertainment. In
appearance and economy of space, and
for convenience of operation, it is far
in advance of anything hitherto offered.
The patented sound chamber of the
Bexonola is an entirely new design, and
gives a tonal result which is a revela-
tion, and must be heard to be appreci-
ated. The tone is full in volume, rich
and clear, without noisiness, and the
quality is delightful.
The Flush Top, with removable motor board, is a feature of the
Ilexonola which is found in no other instrument. Those who have had
experience of other cabinet instruments, in which the operating parts are
at the bottom of a dark, dust-filled pit, will appreciate this feature. They
will find it a pleasure to operate the Bexonola, in which everything is so
placed as to be clearly viewed and conveniently handled.
By a simple operation, all that is necessary being the turning of four
screws, the motor board, with motor attached, can be removed for the
occasional oiling that is necessary.
The Motor of the Bexonola is the finest quality Swiss, with perfected
worm gear governor system, ensuring steady and silent running. We
guarantee the Bexonola motor, and will replace, free of charge, any part

AUDITORS’ CERTIFICATE AND REPORT.
In accordance with the provisions of section 113 of the Companies (Consolidation) Act, 1908, we
report to the shareholders as follows: —
We have examined the above balance-sheet with the books at head office, and have had access to the
branch accounts transmitted to the head office from Australia and New Zealand. AVe have verined the
correctness of the cash, money at call and shore notice, investments, and the bills of exchange held in
London, and having obtained all the information and explanations we have required, we are of opinion
that such balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the
company’s ahairs according to the best of our information, and the explanations given to us, and as shown
by the books of the company and the said branch accounts.
WHINNEY, SMITH & WHINNNEY, (
London, E.C., Ist July, 1913. Chartered Accountants, 1
Auditors.
REPORT OF PROCEEDINGS AT THE ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING OF THE
PROPRIETORS, HELD IN LONDON, 21st JULY, 1913.
Mr. H. P. STURGIS in the Chair.
The Chairman moved the adoption of the report and accounts as follows*.
Gentlemen, —It now becomes my duty to move the adoption of the report and accounts, and, in doing
so, I shall not have to detain you very long, because the half-year under review, ending 28th February
last, has been really an uneventful one. At the same time, the Bank’s trading again snovvs very satisfac-
tory results. After making provision for doubtful debts and contingencies, we have been able to Place
£50,000 to the reserve fund, raising that fund to £1,500,000, and to declare a dividend which, with
the bonus, is equal to 14 per cent, per annum. In this connection it is interesting to note that tor the
first time in the Bank’s history the total of the reserve fund equals its paid-up capital, thus the paid-up
capital and the reserve fund together amount to £3,000,000 ; and these, with the reserve liability ot
proprietors, equal to a further j 1.3,000,000 on the shares existing on 28th 1 ebruary, formed a total ot
£6 000,000. This shows a position of strength which should be eminently satisfactory to the depositors
and the Bank’s customers generally. When the issue of new shares, now being offered, is completed, a
further amount of £900,000 will be provided, of which £500,000 will be paid-up capital and £400,000
premium account, which in due course will be added to the reserve fund. 1 may point out here that the
success of the issue of the new shares is already assured, these new shares commanding a substantial
premium in the market over the price at which they are being offered to the shareholders. The directors
have every confidence that this increase to the Bank’s resources can be employed to good advantage. Ihe
figures in the balance-sheet call for no comment from me. The incidental increase usual in February in
the item “Balance of remittances and drafts in transitu and bills receivable London Office, and the
consequent decrease in “Specie on hand and cash balances,” are again shown. The liquid and readily
realisable assets amount to £10,491,000, while our liabilities to the public amount to £24,080,000.
After allowin'* for the t 1,000,000 of investments set aside for the reserve fund, the liquid assets amount
to 7/10 in the £1 of our liabilities. Our investments at the close of the half-year called for no provision
for depreciation; and the position was the same at the end of May; but since then, as you are no doubt
aware there has been a severe drop in the value of securities generally, and , to-day our own investments
show some depreciation on the.prices at which they stand in our books, for which depreciation, however,
vour directors have made full provision. The stringency of money in the Commonwealth and Dominion
has continued throughout the half-year, and though there may be some signs of the monetary position
becoming easier, there are many enterprises, both public and private, only waiting for such conditions to
corhe forward as borrowers; so that the demand from various quarters .is. likely, for some time, to keep
nace with the supply The wool clip, owing to the heavy mortality in the early part of 1912—estimated
' at about 9 000 000 sheep and lambs—-showed a substantial decrease, stated at between 250,000 and 300,000
bales Prices ’ obtained however, for the wool have been Very satisfactory. The harvest, prospects of
which for some time were discouraging, has, owing to very favorable conditions at the ripening stage,
exceeded expectations, and resulted in a crop of about 80,000,000 bushels, considerably better than the
previous year, and well up to the average of recent years Dairying, which also for a time suffered
from the drought now promises well, and is an increasing industry, especially in New Zealand. Dairymen
also are paying more attention to the selection of their cows, drafting inferior animals, and replacing them
with animals of higher quality. Real estate business has naturally been restricted by the tightness of
monev which has also affected trade to some extent. Trade, however,, is generally sound, and very few
failures are reported The policy of closer settlement is, generally speaking, being steadily pursued by
the various Sthte Governments, and the resulting increase in population cannot but.be of advantage, to
the various localities As to the future. The season opened under very favorable conditions, good
autunin rains havin'* fallen over nearly the whole of the wheat belt in Australia, and over a very large
and important section of the, pastoral .area. A. telegram from the general maanger, received in the last
few davs reports as follows: “Conditions in Western Australia very fair; South Australia, rain wanted;
all other States very good. To-day I see in The Times a notice from the Agent General of South
Australia statin'* that useful rains have fallen, and we have just received a telegram to the effect that
there have been good rains also in portions of AVestem Australia. It is always a pleasure to the
directors and I am sure to shareholders also, to recognise by their thanks how much they feel is due to
the staff’ for any good results which are shown in the half-year; and I would ask you again on this
occasion to join with the directors in tendering their thanks to Mr. Russell, the general maanger, and to
Air Willis the London maanger, and to all serving under tHem, for the loyal and able manner in
which thev’ have carried out their duties. In this connection, it may interest shareholders to know that
the general manager has lately been to New Zealand, where he visited nearly all the branches, and has
since sent us a very satisfactorv report of the Bank’s business and staff in the Dominion. He is at present
visiting Queensland. AVe consider that such visits are of great advantage to everyone concerned. I now
beg to move the following resolution: “That the directors’ report and statement of accounts, as submitted,
bfi a< Mr. te W. O. Gilchrist seconded the motion, which was then put and carried unanimously.

Cockaigne : This has been one of the worst
Bonbon theatrical seasons ever known. Over 50
failures at the West End have to be recorded. The
champion slump was llawtre.y’s, with “The Perfect
r . e > h y Houghton, author of the successful
Handle Wakes.’’ It ran only four nights. Yet
managers had been rushing for Houghton as they
! *°f, Maugham. Oid play-revivals have taken
better than novelties; the taste is more for incident
than character. Ten of the 30 West End theatres
were closed in July, at the top of the season. The
observations of the managers arc interesting. Gran-
ville Barker demands Government or Municipal sub-
sid\. A ext somebody will demand the subsidising
ot cook-shops, which are equally imuortant. Man-
ageress Gertrude Kingston complains that the Eng-
lish are not theatregoers, like French or Germans.
Cyril Maude blames the playwrights. But his judg-
lsn 1 alwa y s sound, lie predicted the failure
ot his great success last year, “Bunty Pulls the
Strings, which ran 500 nights. True, other man-
agers had still worse judgment, for nearlv a dozen
refused that pray altogether. It is significant
that the music-halls are always full. But, then,
they charge 2s. 6d. to ss. for stalls, while the
theatres demand from 7s. '6d. to 10s. 6d. and pro-
vide infinitely less comfort.
The new Tivoli in Adelaide will collar the old
name ot the little King William-street house and
' own to the central but hitherto unfashion-
able Grote street, close to Victoria Square; and
manager Marcus will move over for the opening on
September 6. The Brennan-Fuller circuit has ob-
tained the reversion of the old house. The new one
is easily the largest theatre in Adelaide, and will
take ballet or spectacular shows that are cramped
anywhere else.
‘‘Screen”: Mayor Hennessy, Ex-Mayor Weedon,
p'ii ' dudgeon and other sizeable men are directors
of the newly-formed Melbourne Daylight Pictures Co.
the prospectus has the alluring statement that the
Combine’s “Quo Vadis” raked in a profit of £20,000.
Of course the Daylight Film is to fetch in much
more. The screen is transparent, and on to it
lantern slides or moving pictures are projected
through darkness. No matter what light thev are
shown in, they appear with the greatest brilliancy.
Combined with this is the “Optical Stage.” The
audience cannot see the screen, so Film figures can
be mixed up with living people. It has all been
done successfully in Europe, and experimental per-
formances in Melbourne have given good results.
Harry Lauder has been telling the interviewers
on the other side that when he returns from his
Australian tour he means to go into management
and “have a West End theatre of his own.” He is
going to produce Scoti-h comedies and play the
principal parts in t them himself. And bang will go
at least saxpence of Henry’s hard-sung sayings^
“Abjoneselem”: When will the critics get sense
and cease to liken GenSe to a sunbeam kissing rose-
leaves, a snowflake flirting with space, or thistle-
down wafted in the breeze? Being a coarse per-on
who is habitually inflicted with milk bills, butchers’
accounts and gas collectors, I maintain tint Genge
really dorsn’t, resemble a sunbeam kissing a flower,
or a cabbage, or even a stick of rhubarb. Nor is
she any more like a snowflake in space than I am
like the Archangel Gabriel when I step on a tack
on the way to my hath. In London similar similes
were applied to Lydia Kyasht, whom I saw with the
high-marking Volinin in “The Dance of First Love.”
Kyasht impressed me as a very beautiful ind dainty
young girl, and Volinin as a handsome young buck,
but I am willing to take my oath —nay, more, bet
a quid—that had T gone through precisely similar
gyrations with my best girl, in the presence of her
crudely unsympathetic dog of a father, he would not
have interpreted them to mean that we were im-
paled by the arrows of Love. Not he. He would
merely have deduced from the performance that here
was vet another valid reason for chasing me off the
premises for life.
Hugh Mclntosh is extending his sphere in Mel-
bourne, having acquired land for a songandance
tabernacle in the shopular suburb of Prahran. It is
intended that the programme at the P’ran theatre
shall be the same as at the Opera House. Vaude-
villains will be booked for two turns a night, and
will journey from city to suburb per motor.
“Dumper”: Longford, producer of “Australia
Calls,” has scored a success with his latest, “Pom-
my Arrives in Australia”—the first of a
series of “pommy” pictures, for which the “Abo-
riginalities” column should yield a rich harvest.
This film breaks new ground, being the first long
comedy attempted locally. Interesting to watch real
“pommies” who roll up to see themselves as others
see them. They take the fun good-naturedly. Long-
ford is an actor, gifted with imagination and a
strong personality. His is probably the largest
“producing” experience in Australia. Peculiarly
enough, he rarely sees moving pictures other than
his own. He is associated now with the Fraser
Film Co., which has Franklyn Barrett as cinemato-
grapher.
“Petramos”: Once a year one of the Bostock
family revives the disappearing circus at the
“World’s Fair” in London. Last Christmas I saw
round the ring ancient Sangers, Wombwells, Hol-
wavs, Fossetts and Astle.ys sniffing the sawdust with
relish. One of the ringmasters was a grandfatherly
Ohmy, famed in circus circles for his presence of
mind in emergencies. Whilst appearing aS Dick
Turpin once lie was escaping from the sheriff’s
officers, when his horse baulked at a hurdle, stop-
ping dead. Ohmy peered behind him. “Ah, well,”
he said, “the sheriff’s a long way behind. I’ll read
vou next week’s programme.” Descendants of
Astley are still in the business; but the present head
of the house has put his animals on the stage. I
saw him in a music-hall at Dover introducing a
trick pony on a bicycle.
Isn’t it time that actors and actresses formed a
union to object to the employment of animals on the
stage ? Talk about cheap Chinese labor! At one
Sydney theatre “educated” dogs are competing with
vaudeville artists. At another, men and women are
kept off the boards to make way for erudite sea-
lions, or some such amphibians. Somewhere else are,
or were recently, a crowd of birds. Truly there are
some great plays that were obviously intended for
animals. “Romeo and Juliet,” for instance, was
obviously written to be acted by cats; and “Chanti-
cleer” was designed to be acted by anything from
a loaf of bread to a barndoor rooster. But to make
these plays intelligible, they could be acted only by
men and women disguised as birds, animals and in-
animate objects. These dogs, sea-lions and birds ard
not in that category. They are not human, and to
make room for them on the stage, humans have to
be kept off it.
“Ike Pagliacei”; Houdini, the man who invented
the best methods of getting out of handcuffs, and
author of “How to Break out of Gaol” (since pro-
hibited), is the finest sleight-of-hand man of to-day,
though the general public knows naught of his
conjuring. At the Vaudeville Club in London, in
the presence of several of the cleverest professional
legerdemaniacs, Houdini asked me to push five
needles into his mouth at short intervals. He pre-
tended to swallow them, taking fully a minute to
digest each. A piece of cotton, knotted so that it
could be identified, was then placed in his mouth
by a conjurer whose character resembled Mrs.
Caesar’s. Five minutes later the thread reappeared
with the needles dangling on it. Houdini’s first
professional billet was as a freak in an American
museum. In those days he pushed pins through his
cheek. His first handcuff job got him 255. weekly.
At Holborn Empire (London) he was lately drawing
£2OO a week. I recollect Houdini’s wife calling
on a London agent and begging him to come round
home and remonstrate with her husband, who for
18 hours had been trying to get out of a straight-
jacket. She was afraid he would burst into the next
world. The agent rushed round to release Houdini,
who, however, threatened him with sudden annihila-
tion. “I’ve been here 18 hours,” he said, “and
another half-hour will see me out.” And it did.
“8.J.”: Charles Garvice has dfhmatised one ol
his stories, “Just a Girl,” the scene of which is
laid partly in this country. The play is to be put
on here shortly by Bert Bailey, who (may the
Lord forgive him!) was the malefactor who sug-
gested to Garbage that he should turn his talents
to dramatic authorship.

DEAF?
If you are (leaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them, they
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain .off them -the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. ,
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds. ,
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.

across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the W'ilson

the best adapt-

faintest sound.
