the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve!.
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.

IN BANANA LAND
Protector Howard is forming a settlement
for half-castes near Gayndah, with intent to.
employ them in clearing prickly-pear. Ac-
cording to Howard, the Queensland hybrid
is active and intelligent, and only wants
guidance. Perhaps; but most of all he
wants prevention.
Queensland Legislative Council the other
day put through a Cremation Bill. With a
singular sense of fitness, Denham allows his
Leg. Council to deal with all matters per-
taining to death, burial, cemeteries, tombs,
etc., before they come to the Assembly.
1 A Hughenden paper tells how old Martin
Gorman was rescued the other day after
doing a solid week without food or water.
He was on the track, and at last turned
his old mare loose. Feeling too sick to stir,
he made a shade of the saddle-cloth, and
curled himself up to wait for the end. But
the mare refused to leave, although her foal
was paddocked a few miles away; she iust
wandered about till she picked up a rescue
party, which she guided back to the old
battler under the saddle-cloth. Small wonder
Billjim has dropped the bike, by which he
was fascinated a few years ago, and has
mostly got back to the faithful moke. No
bike ever fetched a rescue partv to a perish-
ing bikist.
“Soapsuds” is annoyed

J" Western Queensland (B. 21/8/’l3). He says:

. Thomas X arker. who died the other dav
in Georgetown (N.Q.), was known bv
miners all over Australia as a desperate
prospector. He arrived in Victoria in 1853
with a bit of knowledge of chemistry. There
was hardly a Victorian rush that did not
see him. lie was at Clunes, Ivorong, Mclvor
Ileasant Creek, Back Creek and Yandoit.
Next he opened a drug store at Wood’s
loint. lie went to Maoriland, and was
familiar at Hokitika. He dropped down in
Queensland, and bobbed up in Fiji, where he
did well, supplying traders and blackbirders
with medicine chests. Thakombau had for
his Premier J. S. Butters, from Melbourne.
Jack Rider, also from Melbourne, was
Hansard reporter. They managed to put
Rider in gaol for some breach of privilege.
Things bubbled, and there was an attempt
at revolution. After Parker had rendered
loyal service to Butters, he thought affairs
were growing too risky, and fled back to
V ictoria, where he settled until gold drew
him to the Etheridge. But the diggings
were poor, and he had to dron back on to
pharmacy again. Such are the men who
have opened Australia—the Legion that
never was lasted.
John May, Labor member for Flinders, is
entitled to some of the thanks of his day and
generation. For years he advocated a
"age of £llO p.a. for public ser-
ants aged 21, but till recently without suc-
cess. This year, however, the Government
has adopted the principle, and as a result
KntJ h^ S 011 of Joy P° ur<3 d on his
DaJd pate by public servants generally. Also,
. e s been stayed with flagons and com-
°™, with apples, and butter has been
handed to him on a lordly dish, and the
beams of Ins house are cedar—or words to
that effect.
A little chapter of industrial history ik
closed by an auctioneer’s announcement that
the Alice River settlement has been sold with
all stock for £3lllO. The Alice River settle-
ment was inaugurated by a section of the
stnkers in the great ’9l dispute to deirion-
t what Labor, unfettered by capitalistic
restrictions, could do. The settlers soon
wearied of the experiment. Some deserted:
some sold out ; and the place finally drifted
into the hands of two or three men as a
Purely private venture, the end of which is
contained in the above-mentioned sale '
notice.

According to the Courier (August 22).
M.L.A. Booker told the Assembly that for
the future he “reserved to himself the right
of speaking and voting absolutely according
to his own convictions.” The Opposition
immediately cried "Hear, hear.” If any
Oppositionist had said the same thing, his
comrades would have denounced him as a
traitor—m strict accordance with the spirit
or I arty Government.
I tan . Bishop Barnes delivered his
Budget speech the other evening, and in the
re-hash of old figures he forgot to mention
one thing- that while the State gained a
whole majestic 2.4 per cent, of population
last year, it lost territory to the prickly-pear
enemy at the rate of a million acres. An-
other fact, passed unnoticed, is that each
year the area under wheat crop gets less.
The bulk of the newcomers axe speculators,
not farmers ; they want to make money, but
they don't want to work. It is this that has
helped to give the pear so much rope. If
man refuses to crop the land, the pear takes
on the contract. Queensland doesn’t know
what agriculture means. It only under-
stands grazing four-footed animals. Half the
vegetables eaten in Brisbane come from the
South, while tens of thousands of acres of
land close to the city remain idle. Nine-
tenths, if not more, of the apples and pears
eaten in the State are Tasmanian, though
blanthorpe can grow fruit of this class
equa! to anything in the world. Thousands
ot tons of wheat are imported each year from
the- South, mainly because the land-owner
would rather grow sheep and prickly pear.
Ihe wrong sort of man has got on to the
land. He wants to see railways built —not
to carry his produce to market, but to in-
crease the value of his holding so that he can
sen it. Barnes ought to have made a few
Hundred remarks about these things. A Bud-
get speech in Queensland with a few such
novelties in it would be preferable to the
old hash about Queensland being destined to
become the richest member of the group

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One careless hour the Angel at the Gate
Of Eden slept, and Eve, alert, elate,
Stole in and left her Adam at his toil
Li battling with the Earth’s unkindlv soil.
She went again in joyful mood to roam
About the windings of that garden home.
Her husband missed her, sought the
wakened guard
And told how Eve had passed that way un-
barred,
Intending, doubtless, she would spend an
hour
In sweet revisit of her ancient bower ;
1 erchance repenting of her primal sin,
And hopeful that her tears would pardon
win.
At: last they found her, just at eventide,
Asleep and still, reclining on her side ;
Iler hair dishevelled, cheeks a trifle pale.
As one who dreamt on some unhappy tale —*
A ripe, half-eaten apple near her knee,
Beneath the same old sad, Forbidden Tree!

My Dear Thargomindaii,—
Governor Macgregor is still going to and
-fro upon the earth (the northern end of
the earth after you leave N. S. Wales and
previous to bumping the butt end of York
Peninsula) in pursuit of vice-regal jobs—
the kind of jobs that feed out of the bottle
and take what’s coming to them lying down.
On Friday he hurled open a new School of
Arts at Murgon, and to-day he swooped
down upon Area B 7 (that’s a military, not
a cattle, brand) and decorated the cadets
with some tom-toms or kettle drums.

AUSTRALIA JANE.
A further growl has been made to Min
rnter Barnes re the Government restrictior
on indiscriminate boring in Western Queens-
land. Barnes, however, was prepared t or
it, and hurled some statistics at the growlers
that were hard to dodge. The N S Wales
flow has decreased 38 per cent, in the last
years, and Bananaland’s flow is
45,000.000 gallons (9 per cent.), less than it
was 1- months ago. In reply, the deputation
could merely mutter something about de-
tective casings and reach for its hat. *
“Itan,” from the North:—
_ I am a prophet. As sure as the sun, if the game
is not watched, Badger will get a long extension
of his co. s rights to the tramways in Brisbane.
1 he local authorities, mostly incapables, cannot agree
amongst themselves about taking the lines over The
Government cannot see where the money is to come
from to buy up the service, for all the millions the
htate can borrow within the next few years will be
wanted to placate Cohen and satisfy the clamor for
new railways. (Haven’t you noticed the plentiful
pars saying that £12,000,000 is due in 1915?) In
the meantime the public is shrieking for the exten-
SK)n of lines which have been promised for years,
and if Badger builds them he has the right to use
inrm , , seven J' ear s after the charter is up in
1920. While the Government and the local authori-
ties are squabbling about a basis of agreement,
Badger sits tight and says little. This bickering,
the louder the better, suits Badger. While these
past-masters in the art of wrangling keep up the
game, the co. is safe. The Government pretends
to be determined ; hut Bob Philp and other influential
people are barracking for a time extension for all
they are worth. The trams don’t meet the public
necessities by long chalks; and these advocates point
to the easy way of getting the public necessities
attended to. Not a word is said about the outrage-
ously short sections. Not a word is said, either,
abojit what will happen at the end of the extended
period. It all shows the utter madness of allowing
such utilities to pass out of the hands of the public
in thp first .place.

Sunbeam (and others) : For the ten
thousandth time, there is no room in The Bulletin
to throw either bricks or bouquets 'at unaccepted
paragraphs. Hundreds of them are not printed because
there is no room for them ; hundreds more wouldn’t
be printed if space was consumed explaining why
the other hundreds were crowded 0ut.... Tower Hall:
Brief personal pars, most acceptable; no space—and
even less inclination —for reprint. .. .Chardibus:
Righti .. .Tower Hill: Letter received with thanks.
Don’t fully agree with you, but it is a pleasure to
hear from you, all the same... .T.K.A.: You will
find that mud-slinging idea in Mark Twain’s “Yankee
at the Court of King Arthur.”... .A. Turner: Have
carefully noted your statement that the Donnelys
(boatbuilders), Reynolds (boatbuilder), George Ellis
(boatbuilder and celebrated sailing man), Murdoch
(cricketer), Percy Spence (artist), Gladstone Eyre
(artist), C. Messenger (well-known sculler), “Dally" 1 ’
Messenger (boss footballer), Eraser (footballer) and
Harold Hardwick (champion swimmer), were all born
in Duke-street, Balmain. Can't verify the statement,
but take your word for it. Also, a member of The
Bulletin staff once lived there, and had the cele-
brated George Pile and his dog for neighbors. And
a horde of the New (or Rue) Australia crowd which
went to Paraguay had a temporary dwelling in the
next street.... Barabbas: Your latest heap of cheery
abuse wasn’t so comic as its predecessor. Perhaps
the vein is getting worked out. There are men who
can curse at high velocity for 10 minutes, or for
two columns, without using the same curse twice, but
they are rare —sadly rare. . . .Mint: Good fooling ; but
a little in that vein goes a long way... .Le Taon: In
reply to your simple and leg-pulling rqpase query:
Another Bulletin contributor recently noticed a
large, sluggish animal of a brown color." It had four
legs, spikes on its head, no visible signs of intelli-
gence, and something like a small portmanteau
dangling from the under part of it. Also it had
a wet nose, which it rested on the fence.
Several of it went to the ton. He asked a
passing native the name of it, and the native said:
“Garn. Kidyerdon’tnow.” It seems a most impro-
bable name. .. .Arthur J. P.: Fair jingle, but not
worth print. Second last line shovvs your lack of
skill... .Bellerive: Pathos this time:- —
POVERTY.
A man against an opponent
May deliver blow for blow
But poverty cruel poverty
Is a strong and bitter foe
Some men when unable to
Keep the grim wolf from the door
Will steal in desperation
And are dealt with by the law
With warm clothes and provisions.
You can wade'th thro the snow
But poverty cruel poverty
Is a strong and bitter foe.

Ernest GMitty, an ex-pupil of the ■ Insti-
tute for the Blind at Auckland (M.L.), can
show a record that any unde’s nephew might
be proud of. He entered the school as a
boy of eight, went on to the University at
17, scored several successes, took his B.A.
degree fit 21, and a scholarship next year
at St. John’s and wrote M.A. after
his name three years later. Two years ago
he was, ordained a ..deacon, put in two use-
ful years ak cmt» of an Auckland city
parish, and. ; thcir' adapted an appointment
on the staff of S£ John’s College at Auck-
land. Last April the Government appointed
him as one of its representatives on the
Board of the Blind Institute, and the selec-
tion gave satisfaction all round. In addi-
tion to all his other achievements Mr. Chitty
is a fine organist, and gives recitals on his
favorite instrument.

Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, August 15, 1913.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVAL COLLEGE.
Appointment of Two Masters.
A RPLICATIONS are invited from persons qualified
for appointment as Senior Master or Junior
Master in the Royal Australian Naval College.
Applicants must be of Australian birth or perman-
ently domiciled in Australia, and not more than 45
years of age.
Qualifications (educational) necessary:—
(а) Must be a University graduate, with special
qualifications in and aptitude for a particular
subject of instruction ;
(б) As Senior Master, must have obtained high
honors in mathematics at a British University,
and must be qualified in physics ;
(c) As Junior Master, must be qualified to teach
history, geography and English. Preference will
be given to candidates possessing an honors
degree in history or English.
Rates of pay: Senior Master, minimum, £450 ;
maximum, £525 per annum. Junior Master, mini-
mum, £350 ; maximum, £425 per annum.
Full particulars regarding conditions of appoint-
ment, etc., may be obtained from the Naval Secre-
tary, Navy Office, Melbourne, with whom applica-
tions close on SEPTEMBER 15, 1913.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE,
Secretary, Department of Defence.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, August 23, 1913.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVY.
Vacancy for Surgeon.
APPLICATIONS are invited from persons qualified
for appointment as Surgeon in the Royal Aus-
tralian Navy: term of appointment to be tor three
years, with option of re-engaging for further three
years if services required.
Present rates of pay range from £387 16s. 3d.
on appointment to £433 Bs. 9d. after four years’
service, including deferred pay, but exclusive of
allowances for uniform and rations ( £39 10s. lOd.
per annum).
Candidates, whose ages must not exceed 28 years,
or in special circumstances 30 years, must be duly
registered practitioners under States’ Medical Acts.
Further particulars may be obtained from the
Naval Secretary, Navy Office, Lonsdale Street, Mel-
bourne, with whom applications close on SEPTEM-
BER 30, 1913.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE,
Secretary, Department of Defence.

NOTE. —The said Anne Gulliver’s father, George
Gulliver, F.R.C.S.E., and Surgeon in the Royal Regi-
ment of Horseguards, is believed to have had five
brothers and sisters, the children of Samuel Gulliver
and Mary his wife, all born at Banbury, Oxfordshire,
England. One of such sisters, Georgians, married
Thomas Stretton. The said Anne Gulliver s mother,
Anne Keown, had four brothers, namely—Richard
Keown, John Keown, William Keown (otherwise
Boyd), and Henry Keown, and three sisters, namely—-
Mary Keown, who married William Beers; Margaret
Keown, who died a Spinster; and Isabella Keown,
who married Robert Boyd. They are all the children
of Richard Keown, Attorney at Law, and Mary, his
wife, late of Ballvdugan, County Down, Ireland.

The Best Treatment
for Itching Scalps, Dan-
druff and Falling Hair
To allay itching and irritation of the scalp,
prevent dry, thin and falling hair, remove
crusts scales and dandruff, and promote the
growth and beauty of the hair, the following
special treatment is most effective, agreeable
and economical. On retiring, comb the hair
out straight all around, then begin at the side
and make a parting, gently rubbing Cuticura
ointment into the parting with a bit of soft
flannel held over the end of the finger. Anoint
additional partings about half an inch apart
until the whole scalp has been treated, the pur-
pose being to get the Cuticura ointment on the
scalp skin rather than on the hair. The
next morning, shampoo with Cuticura soap
and hot water Shampoos alone may be
used as often as agreeable, but once or
twice a month is generally sufficient for
this special treatment for women’s hair. Cuti-
cura Soap and Ointment are sold throughout
the world. A liberal sample of each, with
32-page booklet on the care and treatment
of the skin and scalp, sent post-free. Address
E. Towns & Co., Dept. L. Sydney, N. S. W.

For Particular People,

Business, Robbery, etc
“Hamer”: The man or woman who owns a little
capital with which to dig out a fortune is often an
amazingly simple creature. In a Sydney court the
other day a case cropped up in which a man had
“invested” £3O in a partnership. The firm was to
manufacture and sell a patent cabinet “to do away
with servant girls.” How the cabinet was going to
abolish Mary Ellen was not explained to him. A
cabinet can’t fry chops or roast water. He didn't
even see the cabinet, but was impressed by a photo-
graph which his partner said was it. And he didn’t
get a receipt for his money. A still more amazing
thing about the innocence of men of this type is that,
while fairly intelligent and wide-awake citizens have
a struggle for a crust, the other sort manages to
get the small sums which they lose through buying
cabinets which are to get up early and cook the
breakfast. Perhaps they are too slow to see the
possibilities of money-spending that fly past their
doors, and so one insensible copper after another
accumulates on their hands as do on a
wreck. A wreck isn’t bright or acquisitive, yet it
can accumulate more barnacles than a Rockefeller.
“Tail-Shaft”: Seems as if the “biggering” of
steamships for the Suez trip to Australia has about
reached its limit for the present. The P. & 0. has
mounted in tonnage from the Moldavia s 10,000,
through the Macedonia’s 10,500 and the Malwa s
11 000 to the Medina’s and the Maloja s 12,500;
and the Orient line has pretty well reached its top
with the 13,000-ton Orama. The Suez ditch is only
<>9ft deep, and isn’t likely to be more for years to
come and until it is more the really huge chaps
will ’have to come by the Cape. The White Star
held the Cape record with 12,000-tonners. Then
came the 15,000-ton Nestor and Ulysses; and now,
while the 18,500-ton Ceramic is already on its way
to Australia, a couple of 20,000-ton Blue Funnelers
are reported as laid down. Sydney will have to
hurrv up with that proposed 40ft. low-water entrance-
channel. Melbourne, on the other hand, vvill have
to commit commercial suicide. And when Melbourne
commits commercial suicide, and is found lying dead
among its samples in Elinders-street, with its throat
cut and its head blown off, and an empty poison
bottle in one hand, and its gun in its other hand,
and a look of terror on its absent face, the local
police will probably decide that it perished of vanity
and egotism and dandruff and swelled head and a
shocking lack of foresight, combined with rush of
pomp to the brain. And incidentally that it died
of thirst through insufficient salt water in the
harbor. *

rhe morning and the evening lights—
The trees, the scenery, and sights—
The girls and boys in bathing-tiglits
Are grand—just grand 1
The prospects and the aspects, too,
Are. fair and marvellously blue ;
And green and brown and every hue
Outstand—outstand!
And, then, the fine statistics show
Exactly what one wants to know,
And how to look and where to go,
At once— at once !
They prove without the slightest doubt
That, when the dust is not about,
The bull frogs raise a joyous shout
For bunce—plain bunce 1
The rabbits and the native bears
Perambulate in friendly pairs,
Discussing Dryberra affairs
With force— strong force;
And, now and then, they stop to All
Their gaze with some hump-shouldered hill
That feeds the Cotter’s mighty rill—•
Of course—of course !
They peer, and pry, and plan a crown,
A City that shall stagger Brown
And Jones, and anger every town
Extant—extant;
They show where wharves and docks will be,
And where the Fleet shall put to sea,
And merchants cultivate with glee
The Peezness Plant!
And then they say: “The Architect—
The Layer-Out whom we expect,
Will be here shortly to collect
New views-— true views
Of all the bush and rocks around,
And all the dry and dusty ground
Whereon Australia has found
Glad IOU’s!”
“He will define the great Exchange,
The Opera, the Rifle Range,
The place where dogs that have the mange
Are killed —just killed !
And he will set the station there
And rear our Parliament in air,
And make the City gay and fair,
And filled— quite filled 1
“And clouds and shadows, too, will come;
And pubs wherein they sell hot rum ;
And drapers’ shops where things should hum
Will be designed ;
And lovely parks, and dim green glades.
And banks with marble colonnades,
And grocers-shops of various grades
The world will find 1
“It will be his peculiar care
To lay them out, both here and there;
Though now the place is dry and bare,
And though no trace
Of water permanent abides,
The Plan—the Winning Plan—provides
That every curve and angle hides
A Popu lace l”
The morning and the evening lights,
Of which the Morning Herald skites,
Have got Australia dazed to rights—
They make us pant 1

O wild W T est wind that blasts and kills —
With all your charm, you can’t pay bills
Like Peezness Plant l

also tried other rem-

cures for my com-

my position most keenly, as I was unable to attend these owing to my

“When in America, I was treated by several skin Specialists, and
although with their treatment the trouble would disappear for a short
while, it would again return severer than ever. I really thought I
would never be rid of this terrible eczema, when I read of a case of;
similar severity to my own that had been cured by Zam-Buk, and I at
once determined to give this balm a trial.
“I persevered with Zam-Buk, for the first few applications soothed
the awful itching, and in a short time the eruptions were cleansed and

found myself completely cured, and at time of writing it is over two
years since Zam-Buk proved so beneficial to me, and I have not had
any return of the trouble, so it is apparent that the cure is complete
and permanent.

other sufferers from this dreaded skin complaint, and anyone suffering

The Ocean Singer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAD just returned, and I had proposed
to crown a royal time by giving my
partner Howard the shock of his life.
I knew his habits, and it was £lOOO
to a gooseberry that he would sit down to
dinner at 6.45 p.m. I had my latch-key,
and I would let myself into the bachelor
home unobserved, and while Howard was
busy on his soup, lean over his shoulder and
howl at him. So I crowded on my evening
suit, jumped into a cab, got to the scene of
action, and walked through the door just in
time to see Millie, the maid, vanish in the
direction of the kitchen.
I had forgotten the mirror, and as I
stepped lightly into the room Howard
looked at the glass opposite. I stopped,
appalled at the change in the man’s fea-
tures. Instead of the happy, round, red
face of my old pard was a drawn, haggard
countenance with the hair turning grey at
the temples. And he, when he saw me,
whom he believed to be some thousands ot
miles away, standing silently behind him, in
evening dress —which was most unusual for
me —took me to be a spectre come to convey
the knowledge that I was dead and show'
him a copy of my tombstone. So we looked
steadily at one another in the glass. Then
as I stepped forward he swung round and
held out his hand.
“Flesh and blood, after all!” he said.
“What did you think I was?”
“A ghost, old man. I’m glad you’re
back.”
“What have you been doing to yourself?
You’re looking a bit fagged.”
He was on his feet, bustling round with
the glasses; and he seemed for a moment
to be his old self again. We drank to our
reunion. Millie set for me, and we sat
down to table once more, and for a while
Howard forgot his troubles, whatever they
were, as he listened to my brief sketch of
my travels and of the heroic deeds I didn’t
do. Then I declared my innings closed and
asked him to get busy.
“Well, business is all right. We got
taken down for a few' thousand by Monsen ;
but we got it all back and more in the in-
crease of business. So we’re no worse off.”
“And where is Monsen?” I asked.
“He was doing five years’ stretch. I
thought he’d have got the benefit of his
previous good character, struck 18 months,
and got the first offender’s ticket. But the
Judge jumped on him with both feet and
handed it out heavy and hard. I was sorry
for him. Now I wish he had been given a
life sentence and then beheaded.”
“Well, don’t worry about that. I hope
that hasn’t got on your nerves. When a
man starts to dip, he’s put his soul on the
table, so to speak, and usually the odds are
not worth the risk.”
“They weren’t in Monsen's case. He
lasted exactly 17 weeks. What we’d have
lost had he kept going for 17 years Lord
knows; for he hit up £3380 what little time
be was going.”
“Anything recovered?”
“Not a bean ! Said his wife bolted with
it. Police asked me to put up the cost of
bringing her back. Not me! Why throw
good beans after bad?”
“Ever see her?”
“Not before the smash,” he said shortly.
Then he stopped. It seemed that he had
seen her after the smash.
“Well, now tell me what’s worrying you?”
“I’m in love —that’s all. Dammit!”
* * * *
I sat on my bed, and looked lovingly
round the walls. Howard, a bit more cheer-
ful, sank into an easy chair and put his feet
up as was his wont to do. Then he dis-
carded his cigar, lit his pipe, and leaning
back commenced :
t “It was the evening of the day I won the
Championship of the Pacific with the Aero-
lite. I all but missed the race fooling round
trying to get Monsen treated as a first
offender—heavens ! he’s a twentieth offender,
I think—and the starting flag was falling
as I dashed for the line. Result; I started
about 20yds. behind scratch. But the old
boat was on her mettle ; and after a devil of
a struggle with a French boat from Noumea
I landed the bacon. After the race I cut
from the crowd and got into Bela-
sario’s cafe, with my clothes still stiff with
salt from the spray and my hair thick with
it. I didn’t tvant champagne—l was drunk
already with that wild rush through the
water. I wanted something to eat in rest-
ful surroundings, and got to a corner table
decorated with roses from Belasario’s own
garden, and waited to see what the Lord
would send me.
“He sent a waitress w r ith a demeanor of
mingled hauteur and affability-—a lovely
girl with coppery-colored burnished hair
which threw back the light disdainfully and
arms that were masterpieces.
“But it was her bearing and her voice
that took me. She whispered the word
‘consomme’ as though it were a caress ; her
waiting was a splendid art ; her serving of
boiled fish a rite; her passing of the wine
ah endearment; and the whole an alluring
ceremony by a splendid priestess.”
I sat on the bed and gaped helplessly. I
had known Howard in curious moods, heard
wild bursts of weird eloquence —but never
anything like this.
“I was sitting there, chewing a stick of
celery, when she came along with the bill,
and as she folded it up and put it under the
edge of my plate she looked at me with a
smile and almost whispered :
“ ‘So you won !’
“ ‘You bet!’ I said. ‘But it was a close
thing. That Frenchman would have beaten
me had he not shipped a big sea.’ t
“‘What was it like? I love motor boats,
she breathed. 1
“After that I’ve no very clear notion of
what I said; but I know about the next
thing I remember was that I was taking
ner in a taxi to where the Aerolite was
moored. She looked more alluring than
ever in a long, fawn-tinted cloak with her
coppery hair bunched up under a dainty
little soft felt hat. In less than no time we
were rocking out through the Heads into
the moonlit ocean. A great sea, green-tipped
in the moonlight, smashed on the bows and
P°ured overhead in a cloud of spray.
“rnu ra i. n h ow canopy !’ she exclaimed.
-*- ae wind came right down in our teeth.
±ne Aerolite sprang skyward like a wild
tmng, trembled a moment on the windy
n • moon lit water, and then dived
m vLu , in a purple hollow, all strewn
W1 ,i broken naoonbeams.
W e had been through it all scores of
times, old man; but I never realised the
gorgeous loveliness of it till that night. As
we dived down Miranda (that is her name)
clung to me and gasped. You know what it
lfe - V ke down there —the breathless stillness
ot it all; the peace of Nirvana. Then the
heave of the water, the wild bound skyward
again over the foaming ridge that formed
'n?i? rin ? watery crater we were in.
u? ri PP e d wave screamed; the wind
whipped us across the faces, and blew down
into our lungs. The madness of the
tumbling sea fell on her, and she sang, sang
like a siren and there was nothing in my
. ears.
He sighed. When he had finished sighing
terribly.
lie bounded and rolled through miles of
this till instinct and the petrol gauge told
me it was time to turn. If the run out had
been glorious that homeward was equally
so. Gosh ! It was great to see the white-
maned horses of the Lord toiling heavily in
front, and to overtake them, and rip through
them, leaving them screaming with rage at
the presumption of man and his many in-
ventions. Then those frowning Heads rose
up like the gates of dreamland or perdition
or some dashed old place, and we passed
through them into the ordinary world
again.”
Howard’s mood was a quaint mixture of
poetry and blasphemy. I couldn’t get the
key to his emotions.
“That was the start of it. I became be-
witched, enchanted. Life without her be-
came impossible; and one night I asked her
to marry me.”
“And she refused,” I interrupted.
“She did. How did you know?”
“I remember your face the moment I saw
it in the glass. The girl was wise!”
“Why?”
“She knew it was not the girl Miranda
your fickle nature was in love with at all.
It was the singing siren of the sea. In
breeze and moonlight, singing in the sea,
she’s an idea. Married and in a home she
will be a siren no longer—just an ordinary
woman ordering grilled chops for breakfast.
And you don’t want an ordinary woman—
you’ll want a singing siren all the time. No,
marriage would have been a hideous disil-
lusionment for both of you. The girl is
right. Don’t dream of it.”
Howard’s reply, I regret to say, was un-
printable. Also it was intensely picturesque.
“Now, if you are wise,” I went on, “you
will keep these delightful memories in all
their picturesque beauty by breaking it
right off. It is obvious that this is the
time to break it off, for if it is done now
Miranda will be to you always young,
sirenic, and associated with music, moonlit
seas, and ecstasy. Marry her, and the day
will come when you will wonder if the stout,
not to say shapeless, party with the com-
monplace face waddling beside you can pos-
sibly have been that lovely girl. It’s cruel.
Don’t do it!”
When Howard had exhausted himself by
the blowing off process I continued :
“The trouble is that you are not fitted with
domestic affection any more than you are
with a Westinghouse brake. This is the
first time you have been in love —and it is
with a picturesque phantom and not a real
woman. The man who is fitted with
domestic affection grows old with his wife.
He is never disillusioned, because he never
had any illusions to dis. You’ve got about
the biggest swag of the article I ever came
across. As a matter of truth, didn’t she
tell you the same thing in a different and
less direct way?”
“She did! She did!” Then he helped
himself to another whisky. “But she ad-
mitted later on that she said it only to
cover her real reason. And that was, that
she was Monsen’s sister; and it was
through him being in gaol she had to go
waiting. She didn’t find out exactly who
I was till we’d both fallen in love, she said.”
“That was rough!” I remarked foolishly.
Inwardly I also thought that it was a trifle
hollow, not to say bogus.
Howard wiped his unwise forehead.
“I explained I had already done my best
to get him released as a first offender and
failed. She told me the family had done the
same, but the law people said no restitution
had been made. Were restitution made he
would probably be released at once, as he
had already served some months. I saw
the chance. I gave her £3500 to make
restitution with and a cheque for £2OO so
that she could take him away and give him
a fresh start, and I threw in a hint of
further assistance. Could any blighted
jackass have done more?”
I admitted that it seemed to be the
supreme jackass act.
“Next time I saw her she was radiant.
She had received the promise of a release ;
and in our delight we went out and picked
our furniture, selected a house out Cran-
brooke way and fixed the day for the wed-
ding.”
“Then everything’s all right then!” I said
in a staggered sort of way.
“No, it’s all wrong—wrong to Hades. He
was released, and she took him up the line,
saying she would be away for a week or so,
nursing him. That night, before she went
to meet him at the railway station, was the
last time I saw her.”
“What happened? Accident? Sudden
death? Unitarianism ?”
“No! She was his wife instead of his
sister—that’s all. They had pulled some
other strings too, and he had been released
on general principles. We didn’t get a cent
—only a bad cheque by way of refund. The
blamed thing purported to come from the
Crown Law Department, but it might as
well have come from a peanut barrow.
Monsen stole my money, and then he got
away with the money I gave his wife so
that she could refund my own money to
myself. And she wrote me from some
blamed old Portuguese island saying she
was sorry. One of her bronze hairs had
dropped into the letter, too.”
“What are you going to do?” I asked
feebly. „
“Get drunk and eat a policeman,”
thundered Howard.
C. A. JEFFRIES.

The Musterers’ Camp.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE were lifting 1000 head of cattle
from Bulgroo (W.Q.), but arrived
there a week before they were
ready. So we pitched camp by the side of
a scrub-sheltered lagoou on the run. Before
us was a clear plain, white and waving with
Mitchell grass, wlipre the final drafting was
to take place, and we were to take delivery.
Behind us was a wild and luxuriant tract
of country, dotted with clumps of brigalow,
long stretches of lignum, serpentine lagoons,
broad marshes and thick scrubs, culminating
in Thunda Mountains, which sheltered
thousands of wild cattle that only came
down on to the lowlands at night.
The greater part of Bulgroo was un-
fenced, and half the cattle on it had never
seen a yard. In deference to the notoriety
they had gained as rushers on the road,
drovers who lifted them accepted no respon-
sibility for losses. Some town drovers about
a month before started with 500 of the
beauties. When they got to Eurangella,
three days’ journey, they had 125. These
got boxed with a neighboring herd and were
abandoned. In a day or two they were back
on their own run, huge gaps in the inter-
vening fences marking their passage.
“How did you come to lose them?” one
of the men was subsequently asked.
“No trouble at all to lose them,” he
answered. “They were a bit too fiery about
the heels.”
Tfiere were six men in our camp, includ-
ing a youthful cook, whose name was Sam
Slingsby. Of the 14 horses we possessed
eight were unbroken colts. These we had
to break in to take with us on the road.
Five of the six men enjoyed the prospect.
They had been born and bred on the western
cattle runs; in fact, the creaking saddle was
about the only cradle they had known in
their infancy. They were crack riders,
smart all-round bushmen, bristling with
animal spirits and good humor. My dubi-
ous remarks anent managing half-bred
scrubbers in thick timber on such mounts
only made these chaps more hilarious than
usual. To go on the roads with wild cattle
and mounted on brumbies was their ideal of
enjoyment.
We built a sapling yard, and the first
task every morning was to get them into it.
It took our combined force, two dogs, a
quantity of small timber, and much noise to
accomplish this. Four of us were on foot,
and we closed round, performing like so
many aborigines at a battue. There was
one brute that was full to the brim of objec-
tions, and he made it a practice to break
back three or four times every morning. He
demolished our yard several times before he
was caught, and he smashed through it
twice with the tackle on. Again he tried
to jump out, but landed on his head, and a
heavy sapling fell across his neck. When
he got up the hobbles were on him.
A couple were saddled the second morn-
ing, but the next day saw all six listed for
riding exercise. Sam came out in a cook’s
cap, and selected a long-tailed iron-grey,
that being the most docile-looking of the
uncivilised quadrupeds. But he was a de-
ceptive animal, for bis would-be rider was
no sooner on than he was off again, throw-
ing a somersault before he dropped among
the tussocks. Samuel wasn’t much of a
rider, but he was young and ambitious. He
was always ready to throw off his apron
for a morning seat on a buckjumper. He
averaged two busters a day for the week.
Still he said he was “getting on.”
My mount was a tall chestnut, which
only' pigjumped at the start, and I was one
of those who laughed when Sam threw a
somersault out of his saddle. I set off
confidently to help run the horse back. Half
a mile from camp, when I had worked up
a swinging canter behind the runaway—
which the chestnut was eager to join—an
emu sprang out of a lignum bush close in
front of me. The horse nropped and
wheeled about sharply, then gave two flying
bucks —and the second animal was riderless.
I carried five emu eggs back to camp in my
hat.
Trusting to that distance which lends
enchantment to the view and the quickness
with which the buster happened, I explained
that I was dismounting for those eggs when
the chestnut sprang away from me. B.y-
and-bye, having remounted, I was slung
clean out of the saddle before all eyes. Only
the cook laughed; the veterans rode round
and round with straight faces, peering at
the ground. Presently one asked ; “Where’s
the nest?” It was evident that my emu
was a failure.
We schooled the horses every morning,
and in the afternoon we shot ducks and
pigeons, and caught fish. We had good
times until the musterers arrived. They
came with 4000 head of mixed cattle, among
which were many clean skins that had been
cut off among the foothills. We were now
25 in camp, with a hundred horses. These
were caught in the open, and every morning
saw half a dozen bucking performances
before the cavalcade rode out. The muster-
ing was not yet completed, so we tailed the
mob all day while the Bulgroo men were
after more cattle, and watched them at
night, five men on each watch. It was cold
weather, being near the end of May. and
with fingers and toes numbed, riding round
hour after hour, we watched the stars rising
and setting as only drovers and astronomers
watch them. These western cattlemen know
the stars as they know their native hills.
They lie under them and ride under them
through the long nights year after year.
The first night with our big mob was wet.
There was only one tent, which the mus-
terers’ cook used as a storehouse. He was a
tall, silent man, always in spotless white
apron and cap. He wore top boots as well,
for he had much mud to wade through round
the tables, which consisted of several bags
thrown down on the gro=s. He eyed the
addition to liis huge family with disfavor,
but he served us well. He gave us yeast
bread —huge loaves of it baked in a camp-
oven, roast beef and corned beef, meat and
fruit pies, curry, vegetables, cakes and
brownie, plum pudding and ]am roll, blanc-
mange, custards (with emu eggs), butter,
jam and condiments. He took a. pride in his
table, but compliments only elicited a grunt.
We had trained horses for night w T ork—-
and needed them. Four thousand head take
some stopping when they rush, and these,
with the restless wild scrubbers among them,
required little to set them going. Indeed
they hounded off without any apparent cause
whatever. They were camped in the clear,
but it was a miserable night, dark and wet,
and the big blaze by the tent was only a dull
glow through the drizzle. Sam and 1 were
riding together, which we had no right to be
doing: but the mob was so quiet and con-
tented-looking, a few standing up here and
there, as there always are on a camp. Then
suddenly we heard the well-known sound of
the stampede, like the roar of a cataract, or
the deep rumble of distant thunder. The
4000 were up and off before we had time to
turn round. There was a quick tightening
of reins, an instant’s breathless poise in
rigid stirrups, then a concerted yell from
several quarters, the plunging of excited
night horses, and we were in pursuit. The
other three, being nearer the lead, had
jumped away with the cattle, and from each
side the stockwhips rang out, mingled with
the cries of the men.
For less than a minute Sam and I rode
together at the tail; then the true position
must have struck us at once, for we swung
out, he to the right, and I to the left. Like
an explosion the fire had blazed up among
the cattle, and gone out. A shower of sparks
had made one spot brilliant for a moment,
to be followed immediately by a deeper
darkness.
The leaders had split before coming to the
fire, but the mass crowding behind had gra-
dually closed the fork till the camp was
smothered. Down went the tent to the
rattle of tinware and buckets; over went the
station waggonette, splintered into a thou-
sand pieces; bells jingled and clanked as
they were kicked along the ground, and from
overhead came the derisive shout and yells
of men who, in all manner of deshabille, had
been rudely awakened and hurried from
warm beds to wet treetops.
We were past the wreckage in a fla«h, and
very soon the sounds changed to the crash-
ing and crackling of timber and brittle un-
derwood, as the frantic mob poured into the
scrub and lignum.
At intervals we heard the frenzied bellow
of a fallen beast; several were left killed or
crippled in the scrub ; and presently a snort-
ing horse came trotting back with empty
saddle and trailing rein. It was Sam’s, and
it had gone right round the cattle; but Sam
hadn’t. Beyond the scrub the broad lagoon
took a long sweep to the north-east, and
opining that the mob would swing with it,
Sam, being an ambitious young man, cut
swiftly across to the point where he could
easily cross out of danger, intending then to
surprise his compatriots by airing his heroic
voice in the lead. But a vine yanked him
out of the saddle, and he contemplated the
frailties of the human race on an owl roost,
subsequently tramping back, the one dis-
graced unit of the watch.
His surmise, however, was correct. The
huge mob swung with the lagoon, and this
turn giving us an advantage, in five minutes
we had them ringing. The bovine tongues
erstwhile silent, were now clamorous, call-
ing for mates that had been lost in the
jumble. Hound and round they went, cough-
ing and lowing to the tune of the cracking
whip, the clashing horn and the trampling
hoof, and the steadying cry of the men, till
they finally quieted down. There we held
them till thp next watch relieved us; there
they were held till morning, the curving
lagoon serving as a brake, and as they were
drawn out to the open at dawn the wild
bulls were shot down in their tracks. Not
that they were the ringleaders, for hulls \
are usually the best campers of all cattle,
hut because they were scrub-bred, and rifles
had not been handy till then.
There was no such thing as “turning in”
when we rode back to camp. There was
nothing to turn into. All the available
wood had been collected, and a bonfire was
blazing. Four men stood or squatted by it,
smoking ; the others, presenting a delightful
study in expressions, were searching the
trampled earth for pipes and tobacco. Bat-
tered and muddy saddles, broken bridles,
damaged campware and sundries were
gathered up and thrown into a heap. Be-
draggled swags, torn blankets, clothing and
other effects were sorted out and claimed
by this or that one. As for the cook, he was
calamity impersonated. As each piece of
his silverware or cutlery was recovered and
carefully piled in the firelight, he looked
volumes of bitterness. The collecting lasted
till the fire burnt low, then we lay down in
heaps by the coals.
At daylight the cook started collecting
again. He mustered up a few tins of jam,
two bottles of pickles, a bucket of potatoes,
two pumpkins, and some odds and ends, and
having cooked everything there was to cook,
25 wet and hungry men sat down to break-
fast. And still they were merry men.
“What’s yours? Spuds or pumpkin?”
—ns the query all round the mud-covered
bags.
“Try some jam with them,” one sug-
gested.
“Go easy with the nickles, Jack,” said an-
other, “but don’t be afraid of the tea. There’s
plenty more of it in the lagoon.”
Pack-horses were sent to the homestead
after breakfast for fresh supplies. Before
these arrived we had dined sumptuously on
emu eggs, which were plentiful in the
locality. We had poached eggs, boiled eges.
fried eggs and roast eggs. The bushman
loves variety.
Thence on, till our bullocks were drafted
out and we took charge, all the spare time
we had from watching and tailing was de-
voted to cleaning and repairing our gear
and other property, and washing our clothes
and blankets. We took our damaged gar-
ments with us when tailing, a couple at a
time, and while the cattle were feeding in
the open we would squat down in the grass,
holding our horses, and patiently stitch up
the rents.
When we rode away from the musterers'
camp for the last time, we said good-bye to
our silent chef. He doffed his cap and bowu
to us—and that was all.
EDWABD S. SORENSON.

Needs a Waterous Mill
If you’re in the timber business you need a Waterous. It may be a
Circular or a Bandmill —but it’s the Waterous you need. The
Waterous is the standard mill —though not perhaps the cheapest. ' It
gives the most efficient service under any conditions. Its speed, its

because of these, Waterous Mills are in active operation in every
corner of the world.
We don’t expect to sell a Waterous to you on the strength of
this advertisement, but we do hope to have a word from you saying

the hilt that it is to your interest to put in a Waterous Mill. If you
want to get at the REAL facts write us now.
